Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
The Lost Kuttab System
The author describes the decline of traditional learning methods and praises the old kuttab system.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key features and benefits of the traditional kuttab education system as described by Sanusi.

Core Idea
True education begins with the Quran and builds intellect and character.

Sanusi opens his book with a heartfelt lament for the loss of traditional Islamic education. He contrasts the old kuttab system, which produced sound intellect and refined character, with modern methods that he sees as a calamity. This passage sets the stage for understanding why he wrote his theological work.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice the emotional tone and the description of the old system.
The opening includes praise of God and the Prophet, which is typical for Islamic books.
2
Identify the calamity
What does the author say has changed? Find the phrase 'نازلة مفجعة' (calamitous disaster) and note what changed: 'مناهج تلقّي العلوم' (methods of receiving knowledge).
3
Describe the old system
List the components of the traditional kuttab education: learning reading/writing, memorizing Quran (50,000 words), hadith, scientific poems, literary poetry. What effect did this have on the child?
The author says the soul is polished with manners ('يُصقَلُ نفسه بالأدب الجم').
4
Reflect on the outcome
Why does the author consider the old system superior? Think about the statement: 'العقل له حرمة' (the intellect had sanctity) and that the child memorized truth, not lies.
This connects to the theme of sound reasoning that Sanusi will emphasize later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين يدي الكتاب
نحمدُكَ خاشعين يا ذا العظمة والكبرياء ، ونصلي ونسلم مشتاقينَ على حياتنا وملاذنا حبيبك محمد ذي الثناء والبهاء محمد ذي الثناء والبهاء ، وعلى آله النجباء وأصحابه العرفاء ، وتابعيهم بإحسان إلى يوم تمور السماء
وبعد :
فمِنْ سنواتٍ لا تُعدُّ بالقرون ، نزلَتْ بساحتِنا نازلة مفجعة ؛ حينَ تغيَّرت على أمَّتنا مناهج تلقّي العلوم ، فغابت مناهجها التي كانت في زمن ليس ببعيد سبباً لتكونَ الأمم تبعاً ولها الصدارة ، وتكون لغيرها المدنيَّةُ ولها الحضارة ؛ يوم كان للعقل حرمة أياً كان توجههُ ومقصده ، فلا يؤسّس إلا على سديد النظر ، وصائب الفكر . كانت رحلة العقل تبدأ من الكتاتيب ، بين الأساطين والمحاريب ؛ فيتعلم الطفل أصول القراءة والكتابة ، ويحفظ القرآن العظيم ، فيَضُمُّ إلى جنباتِهِ قرابة خمسين ألفَ مفردة لغوية ثرة بالتعبير والحيوية ، وحسبك أنه كلامُ الله تعالى المعجزُ الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه ، ويحفظ إلى ذلك بعضاً من الآثار النبوية ، والأراجيز العلمية ، والأشعار الأدبيَّة ، فتُصقَلُ نفسه بالأدب الجم وهو لا يشعر ؛ إذ يحفظ الصدق ولا يُحشى بالأكاذيب ، وتتفتّحُ في نفسه
Introduction to the Book
We praise You humbly, O Possessor of Majesty and Pride, and we pray and send peace longingly upon our life and refuge, Your beloved Muhammad, possessor of praise and splendor, possessor of praise and splendor, and upon his noble family, his virtuous companions, and those who follow them in excellence until the day the heavens swirl.
To proceed:
For many years, not counted by centuries, a calamitous disaster has befallen our situation when the methods of receiving knowledge changed for our nation. So those methods disappeared which, not long ago, were the cause for nations to follow us and for us to have precedence, while others had material civilization and we had culture. It was a time when the intellect had sanctity, whatever its orientation and aim, not being established except upon sound reasoning and correct thought. The journey of the intellect used to begin in the kuttabs (Quranic schools), among the masters and prayer niches. There the child would learn the basics of reading and writing, memorize the Mighty Quran, gathering within himself nearly fifty thousand linguistic vocabulary words rich in expression and vitality. It suffices you that it is the miraculous speech of Allah Almighty, which falsehood cannot approach from before it or from behind it. And along with that he would memorize some of the prophetic traditions, scientific didactic poems, and literary poetry. Thus his soul would be polished with refined manners without his feeling; for he memorized truth and was not stuffed with lies, and would open up in himself
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the book's introduction (بين يدي الكتاب). It begins with a praise of God and blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad, then launches into a lament about the decline of traditional Islamic educational methods. The author describes how the change in curricula was a calamity, because the old system was the reason other nations followed the Muslim community and Muslims held precedence. In that system, the intellect was respected and was built only upon sound reasoning and correct thought. The journey of the intellect started in the kuttabs, where children learned reading and writing, memorized the Quran (acquiring nearly fifty thousand rich vocabulary words), and also memorized hadith, scientific poems, and literary poetry. This polished their character with good manners without them being aware, as they were filled with truth, not lies, and their inner selves opened up.

Key Terms
الكُتَّاب (kuttab)
مناهج التلقّي (methods of receiving knowledge)
الآثار النبوية (prophetic traditions)
الأراجيز العلمية (scientific didactic poems)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects all modern education and wants to return to a purely traditional system.
Correction: The author is not rejecting all modern education, but mourning the loss of a holistic system that integrated Quran, language, and character. He acknowledges that the old system was the cause of Muslim precedence.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what did the child gain from memorizing the Quran in the traditional kuttab system?
A) Only religious blessings
B) Nearly fifty thousand linguistic vocabulary words rich in expression
C) A modern scientific worldview
D) The ability to debate philosophical questions

Key Takeaway
The traditional kuttab system built a strong foundation by integrating Quran memorization, language acquisition, and character education.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've just read how the kuttab system started with the Quran and built the intellect upon truth. When you seek knowledge today, do you begin with the Quran as your foundation, or have you let other sources take its place? Will you commit to making the Quran the starting point of your learning journey from this moment?
?
The author laments that the old methods disappeared and we lost precedence. Have you personally felt this loss in your own education? Are you willing to seek out and revive even a small part of this holistic approach in your own life?
?
The child in the kuttab memorized truth and was not stuffed with lies. What lies or falsehoods have you accepted in your own thinking because your foundation wasn't built on the Quran? How can you cleanse your intellect starting today?

Method of Learning
beginner
4 min
The Lament Over Changing Methods
Author laments the loss of traditional educational methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of modern educational changes and his call to return to the traditional method.

Core Idea
The traditional method was the foundation of Islamic civilization and reverence for reason.

This lesson begins the foreword with a heartfelt praise of Allah and the Prophet, followed by a lament over how the nation's educational methods have changed. The author argues that the old method gave the Ummah precedence and preserved the sanctity of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening praise and lament
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'نحمدك' and ends with 'صائب الفكر'. Notice how the author transitions from praise to a historical complaint.
2
Identify the two eras
The passage contrasts a past era of correct methodology with a present one of change. Identify the phrases that describe each era.
The past era is described as having 'sanctity for reason' and 'sound consideration'.
3
Reflect on the claim
Consider why the author says the change is 'grievous' and how he links it to the loss of leadership and civilization.
Think about the cause-and-effect relationship presented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين يدي الكتاب
نحمدُكَ خاشعين يا ذا العظمة والكبرياء ، ونصلي ونسلم مشتاقينَ على حياتنا وملاذنا حبيبك محمد ذي الثناء والبهاء ، وعلى آله النجباء وأصحابه العرفاء ، وتابعيهم بإحسان إلى يوم تمور السماء.
وبعد :
فمِنْ سنواتٍ لا تُعدُّ بالقرون ، نزلَتْ بساحتِنا نازلة مفجعة ؛ حينَ تغيَّرت على أمَّتنا مناهج تلقّي العلوم ، فغابت مناهجها التي كانت في زمن ليس ببعيد سبباً لتكونَ الأمم تبعاً ولها الصدارة ، وتكون لغيرها المدنيَّةُ ولها الحضارة ؛ يوم كان للعقل حرمة أياً كان توجههُ ومقصده ، فلا يؤسّس إلا على سديد النظر ، وصائب الفكر . كانت رحلة العقل تبدأ من الكتاتيب ، بين الأساطين والمحاريب ؛ فيتعلم الطفل أصول القراءة والكتابة ، ويحفظ القرآن العظيم ، فيَضُمُّ إلى جنباتِهِ قرابة خمسين ألفَ مفردة لغوية ثرة بالتعبير والحيوية ، وحسبك أنه كلامُ الله تعالى المعجزُ الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه ، ويحفظ إلى ذلك بعضاً من الآثار النبوية ، والأراجيز العلمية ، والأشعار الأدبيَّة ، فتُصقَلُ نفسه بالأدب الجم وهو لا يشعر ؛ إذ يحفظ الصدق ولا يُحشى بالأكاذيب ، وتتفتّحُ في نفسه براعة التعبير ، وتُشرِقُ على مِقْوَلِهِ وبَنانِهِ على نعومتها شمس المعاني ؛ فيحسنُ القول باللسان ، ويُرزقُ حدَّةَ الجَنان ، ويُسطّر روائع البيان.
ثم يصير إلى أيد أمينة ؛ تُقرتُهُ مبادئ العلوم ، وترعاه رعاية الآباء الرشيدة للأبناء ، فتجمعُ له التربية إلى العلم ، وتحنو عليه برفق ، فإن حرنَتْ نفسه زجرته زجر الحريص عليه بعصا التأديب ، وإِن كَسِلَ هُمَزَ بمهماز الشدَّة ؛ حتى تلين قناته ، ويستقيم جذعُهُ.
فيقرأ المتون العلمية التي انعقدَتْ خناصر المتخصصين على فضلها ، وأثبتت الأيام حُسنَ خَبرِها ومَخْبَرِها ، ويحفظها عن ظهر قلب إن كانت له هذه الملكة ، وإلا فيُمهل ولا يُهمل ، حتى يتمرَّس على ذلك ، في وسط مفعم بالمنافسة والتشجيع ، فإن تعسر عليه رضي منه أستاذه بالفهم ، فكم ممَّنْ قصر باعُهُ في الحفظ وملك الحفظ رضي ناصية العلم.
فيتقلب الطالب بين « الآجرومية » و « الألفية » في النحو ، فيحفظ الأولى بأمثلتها ، ويفهم جملها ، ويدرج بحفظ الثانية إلى أن تتهيَّاً له سنوات الشرح عند نضجه ويفاعته ، وبها يدرج لسانه على العربية فمن لم يأخذ بأصول العربية بطرف يمكّنه من قراءة العلوم.. فغالباً ما يُمالُ به إلى علوم عامَّةٍ تنفعُهُ وتنفعُ مجتمعه ؛ كالطب والرياضيات ونحوها ، أو يُسعى به إلى حرفةٍ يسترزقُ الله بها حلالاً ، وتحفظه عن أن يكون عالةً يتكفَّفُ الناس.
ومن جملة ما يحفظُهُ الطالب : « قواعد العقائد » أو « العقيدة
Foreword
We praise You humbly, O Possessor of Majesty and Pride, and we pray and send peace longingly upon our life and refuge, Your beloved Muhammad, possessor of praise and splendor, and upon his noble family, his distinguished companions, and their followers in excellence until the day the heavens surge.
To proceed:
From centuries not counted, a grievous calamity befell us: the methods of acquiring knowledge changed upon our nation, and its curricula vanished that were, not long ago, a reason for nations to be followers while we had precedence, and for others to have civilization while we had culture; on a day when reason had sanctity whatever its orientation and purpose, and was only founded upon sound consideration and correct thought. The journey of reason began from the primary schools (al-katatib), among the pillars and prayer niches; the child learns the basics of reading and writing, memorizes the great Quran, thus gathering to his side nearly fifty thousand linguistic words rich in expression and vitality, and it suffices that it is the inimitable speech of Allah the Exalted, to which falsehood cannot come from before it nor from behind it. And he memorizes along with that some of the prophetic traditions, scientific poems, and literary poetry; thus his soul is polished with good literature without him feeling, for he memorizes truth and is not filled with lies, and the brilliance of expression blossoms in his soul, and the sun of meanings shines upon his tongue and his fingers despite their tenderness; so he speaks well with the tongue, is granted sharpness of the heart, and writes wondrous eloquence.
Then he passes to trustworthy hands; they teach him the principles of the sciences gradually, and care for him with the wise care of fathers for sons, combining for him upbringing with knowledge, and show him gentleness; if his soul becomes unruly, they restrain him with the restraint of a keen person with the stick of discipline, and if he becomes lazy, they prod him with the spur of firmness until his spear becomes soft and his trunk straightens.
He then reads the scientific texts (al-mutun) whose excellence the fingers of specialists have clasped, and time has proven the goodness of their news and state; he memorizes them by heart if he has that faculty, otherwise he is given time and not neglected, until he practices that in a environment full of competition and encouragement. If it is difficult for him, his teacher is satisfied with his understanding, for how many whose hand fell short in memorization but mastered understanding have taken the forelock of knowledge?
So the student alternates between al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah in grammar, memorizing the first with its examples, understanding its sentences, and advancing by memorizing the second until the years of commentary become ready for him at his maturity and youth. With them his tongue becomes fluent in Arabic. Whoever does not take a share of the foundations of Arabic enabling him to read the sciences... often he is directed to general sciences that benefit him and his society, like medicine, mathematics and the like, or he is taken to a craft by which he seeks lawful provision from Allah, and which protects him from being a burden begging from people.
And among what the student memorizes: 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (Foundations of Doctrines) or 'al-'Aqidah' (the Creed).
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is an introduction by the author/editor describing the traditional Islamic educational method and lamenting its change. It begins by praising Allah and invoking blessings on the Prophet, then proceeds to describe a grievous change in the methods of acquiring knowledge. The author states that the old method was the reason for the Islamic nation's precedence and civilization. He then details the journey of the student: starting in the kuttab (primary schools), the child learns reading and writing, memorizes the entire Quran (nearly fifty thousand words), and along with that memorizes hadith, scientific poems, and poetry. This polishes his soul with good literature and develops his eloquence. Then the student moves to trustworthy teachers who teach him the principles of sciences with a combination of discipline and gentleness. He reads and memorizes the classical texts (mutun) that are well-regarded by specialists. The author emphasizes that if the student cannot memorize, understanding is acceptable. He specifically mentions the two famous grammar texts: al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah. Mastering these allows the student to proceed to other sciences; otherwise, he may be directed to practical fields like medicine or mathematics, or learn a trade. The passage concludes by stating that among the texts the student memorizes is 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (or 'al-'Aqidah'), indicating the importance of creedal studies.

Key Terms
الكتاتيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author describe as the cause of the nation's loss of leadership?
A) The change in methods of acquiring knowledge
B) Lack of resources
C) Foreign invasion
D) Weakness in faith

Key Takeaway
The traditional educational method is presented as the foundation of Islamic civilization and the proper cultivation of reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic knowledge, do you treat your own reasoning as sacred and founded on sound thought, or do you accept ideas without careful examination? What will you change in your learning habits today to ensure your intellect is built on solid ground?
?
The author calls the loss of traditional methods a 'grievous calamity'. How personally concerned are you about the state of your own education? What concrete step will you take this week to reconnect with a methodology that prioritizes reason and sound consideration?
?
If the method that produced Islamic civilization has vanished, what methods have you adopted in its place? Are you content with them, or do you feel a responsibility to revive the old way in your own life? Write one specific commitment.

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
The Kuttab: Foundation of Education
Describes the comprehensive curriculum of the traditional kuttab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive early education in the kuttab, including Quran memorization, hadith, poetry, and its holistic benefits.

Core Idea
The kuttab moulded the child's character and eloquence through Quran, hadith, and poetry.

The author now describes the first stage of the traditional journey: the kuttab (primary school). Here, children were immersed in the Quran, prophetic traditions, and literary poetry, gaining immense linguistic and moral benefits. This lesson explains how this stage prepared the mind and soul for further learning.

Guided Path
1
Read the kuttab description
Read the Arabic from 'كانت رحلة العقل' to 'ويُسطّر روائع البيان'. Notice the sequence: basics, Quran, hadith, poetry, and the resulting benefits.
2
Count the elements
Identify what the child specifically learns: reading/writing, Quran (nearly 50,000 words), hadith, scientific poems, and literary poetry.
The author highlights the sheer volume of vocabulary from Quran.
3
Understand the benefits
The passage lists outcomes: polished soul, hatred of lies, eloquence, sharp mind, and beautiful writing. How does this connect to the method?
This shows the method's holistic approach to character and intellect.

ثم يصير إلى أيد أمينة ؛ تُقرتُهُ مبادئ العلوم ، وترعاه رعاية الآباء الرشيدة للأبناء ، فتجمعُ له التربية إلى العلم ، وتحنو عليه برفق ، فإن حرنَتْ نفسه زجرته زجر الحريص عليه بعصا التأديب ، وإِن كَسِلَ هُمَزَ بمهماز الشدَّة ؛ حتى تلين قناته ، ويستقيم جذعُهُ.

Foreword
We praise You humbly, O Possessor of Majesty and Pride, and we pray and send peace longingly upon our life and refuge, Your beloved Muhammad, possessor of praise and splendor, and upon his noble family, his distinguished companions, and their followers in excellence until the day the heavens surge.
To proceed:
From centuries not counted, a grievous calamity befell us: the methods of acquiring knowledge changed upon our nation, and its curricula vanished that were, not long ago, a reason for nations to be followers while we had precedence, and for others to have civilization while we had culture; on a day when reason had sanctity whatever its orientation and purpose, and was only founded upon sound consideration and correct thought. The journey of reason began from the primary schools (al-katatib), among the pillars and prayer niches; the child learns the basics of reading and writing, memorizes the great Quran, thus gathering to his side nearly fifty thousand linguistic words rich in expression and vitality, and it suffices that it is the inimitable speech of Allah the Exalted, to which falsehood cannot come from before it nor from behind it. And he memorizes along with that some of the prophetic traditions, scientific poems, and literary poetry; thus his soul is polished with good literature without him feeling, for he memorizes truth and is not filled with lies, and the brilliance of expression blossoms in his soul, and the sun of meanings shines upon his tongue and his fingers despite their tenderness; so he speaks well with the tongue, is granted sharpness of the heart, and writes wondrous eloquence.
Then he passes to trustworthy hands; they teach him the principles of the sciences gradually, and care for him with the wise care of fathers for sons, combining for him upbringing with knowledge, and show him gentleness; if his soul becomes unruly, they restrain him with the restraint of a keen person with the stick of discipline, and if he becomes lazy, they prod him with the spur of firmness until his spear becomes soft and his trunk straightens.
He then reads the scientific texts (al-mutun) whose excellence the fingers of specialists have clasped, and time has proven the goodness of their news and state; he memorizes them by heart if he has that faculty, otherwise he is given time and not neglected, until he practices that in a environment full of competition and encouragement. If it is difficult for him, his teacher is satisfied with his understanding, for how many whose hand fell short in memorization but mastered understanding have taken the forelock of knowledge?
So the student alternates between al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah in grammar, memorizing the first with its examples, understanding its sentences, and advancing by memorizing the second until the years of commentary become ready for him at his maturity and youth. With them his tongue becomes fluent in Arabic. Whoever does not take a share of the foundations of Arabic enabling him to read the sciences... often he is directed to general sciences that benefit him and his society, like medicine, mathematics and the like, or he is taken to a craft by which he seeks lawful provision from Allah, and which protects him from being a burden begging from people.
And among what the student memorizes: 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (Foundations of Doctrines) or 'al-'Aqidah' (the Creed).
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is an introduction by the author/editor describing the traditional Islamic educational method and lamenting its change. It begins by praising Allah and invoking blessings on the Prophet, then proceeds to describe a grievous change in the methods of acquiring knowledge. The author states that the old method was the reason for the Islamic nation's precedence and civilization. He then details the journey of the student: starting in the kuttab (primary schools), the child learns reading and writing, memorizes the entire Quran (nearly fifty thousand words), and along with that memorizes hadith, scientific poems, and poetry. This polishes his soul with good literature and develops his eloquence. Then the student moves to trustworthy teachers who teach him the principles of sciences with a combination of discipline and gentleness. He reads and memorizes the classical texts (mutun) that are well-regarded by specialists. The author emphasizes that if the student cannot memorize, understanding is acceptable. He specifically mentions the two famous grammar texts: al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah. Mastering these allows the student to proceed to other sciences; otherwise, he may be directed to practical fields like medicine or mathematics, or learn a trade. The passage concludes by stating that among the texts the student memorizes is 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (or 'al-'Aqidah'), indicating the importance of creedal studies.

Key Terms
الكتاتيب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Traditional education was only about rote memorization without understanding.
Correction: The passage shows that memorization was combined with gradual understanding and produced eloquence and moral character, not mere repetition.

D) Five thousand

Key Takeaway
The kuttab stage equipped the child with a rich linguistic and moral foundation through a balanced curriculum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The child in the kuttab memorized the Quran and hadith, and was filled with truth, not lies. What falsehoods, even small ones, have you allowed into your daily mindset or speech? Will you commit to replacing one such falsehood with a truth from Allah's book today?
?
The passage says the child's soul was polished with good literature 'without him feeling.' Are you aware of how your current learning habits—what you read, listen to, or memorize—are shaping your character? Will you audit your sources of knowledge this week and remove anything that dulls your soul?
?
The kuttab produced eloquence and sharpness of heart. Does your study of Islam result in better speech and deeper sincerity, or just more information? What specific step will you take today to let what you learn transform your tongue and heart?

Method of Learning
beginner

Progressing to Teachers and Advanced Texts
Describes the role of teachers and the study of mutun with emphasis on grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the next stage of education: guidance from teachers, mastery of classical texts (mutun), flexibility in memorization, and the focus on grammar texts like al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah.

Core Idea
After kuttab, students were entrusted to teachers who combined discipline with gentleness and emphasized mastering foundational texts.

The author now moves to the next phase: after the kuttab, students are placed under capable teachers who gradually teach the principles of sciences with a balance of care and discipline. The student then reads and memorizes trusted texts (mutun), especially the grammar works al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah. The lesson also notes that students who struggle with memorization are not abandoned; they may be directed to other beneficial fields.

Guided Path
1
Read the transition passage
Read the Arabic from 'ثم يصير إلى أيد أمينة' to the end. Follow the flow: from teacher's role to reading mutun, then grammar texts.
2
Analyze the teaching method
Notice how the teacher combines discipline and gentleness: 'زجرته بعصا التأديب' and 'مهماز الشدة'. What does this balance achieve?
3
Understand the role of grammar texts
The two key texts are al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah. Why are they emphasized? What is the purpose of memorizing them?
They are foundational for Arabic, allowing access to other sciences.
4
Explore flexibility
The passage says if memorization is hard, understanding suffices. Also, student may be directed to other sciences or trades. What does this show about the system?
It shows the system was practical and compassionate, not rigid.

بين يدي الكتاب
نحمدُكَ خاشعين يا ذا العظمة والكبرياء ، ونصلي ونسلم مشتاقينَ على حياتنا وملاذنا حبيبك محمد ذي الثناء والبهاء ، وعلى آله النجباء وأصحابه العرفاء ، وتابعيهم بإحسان إلى يوم تمور السماء.
وبعد :
فمِنْ سنواتٍ لا تُعدُّ بالقرون ، نزلَتْ بساحتِنا نازلة مفجعة ؛ حينَ تغيَّرت على أمَّتنا مناهج تلقّي العلوم ، فغابت مناهجها التي كانت في زمن ليس ببعيد سبباً لتكونَ الأمم تبعاً ولها الصدارة ، وتكون لغيرها المدنيَّةُ ولها الحضارة ؛ يوم كان للعقل حرمة أياً كان توجههُ ومقصده ، فلا يؤسّس إلا على سديد النظر ، وصائب الفكر . كانت رحلة العقل تبدأ من الكتاتيب ، بين الأساطين والمحاريب ؛ فيتعلم الطفل أصول القراءة والكتابة ، ويحفظ القرآن العظيم ، فيَضُمُّ إلى جنباتِهِ قرابة خمسين ألفَ مفردة لغوية ثرة بالتعبير والحيوية ، وحسبك أنه كلامُ الله تعالى المعجزُ الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه ، ويحفظ إلى ذلك بعضاً من الآثار النبوية ، والأراجيز العلمية ، والأشعار الأدبيَّة ، فتُصقَلُ نفسه بالأدب الجم وهو لا يشعر ؛ إذ يحفظ الصدق ولا يُحشى بالأكاذيب ، وتتفتّحُ في نفسه براعة التعبير ، وتُشرِقُ على مِقْوَلِهِ وبَنانِهِ على نعومتها شمس المعاني ؛ فيحسنُ القول باللسان ، ويُرزقُ حدَّةَ الجَنان ، ويُسطّر روائع البيان.
ثم يصير إلى أيد أمينة ؛ تُقرتُهُ مبادئ العلوم ، وترعاه رعاية الآباء الرشيدة للأبناء ، فتجمعُ له التربية إلى العلم ، وتحنو عليه برفق ، فإن حرنَتْ نفسه زجرته زجر الحريص عليه بعصا التأديب ، وإِن كَسِلَ هُمَزَ بمهماز الشدَّة ؛ حتى تلين قناته ، ويستقيم جذعُهُ.
فيقرأ المتون العلمية التي انعقدَتْ خناصر المتخصصين على فضلها ، وأثبتت الأيام حُسنَ خَبرِها ومَخْبَرِها ، ويحفظها عن ظهر قلب إن كانت له هذه الملكة ، وإلا فيُمهل ولا يُهمل ، حتى يتمرَّس على ذلك ، في وسط مفعم بالمنافسة والتشجيع ، فإن تعسر عليه رضي منه أستاذه بالفهم ، فكم ممَّنْ قصر باعُهُ في الحفظ وملك الحفظ رضي ناصية العلم.
فيتقلب الطالب بين « الآجرومية » و « الألفية » في النحو ، فيحفظ الأولى بأمثلتها ، ويفهم جملها ، ويدرج بحفظ الثانية إلى أن تتهيَّاً له سنوات الشرح عند نضجه ويفاعته ، وبها يدرج لسانه على العربية فمن لم يأخذ بأصول العربية بطرف يمكّنه من قراءة العلوم.. فغالباً ما يُمالُ به إلى علوم عامَّةٍ تنفعُهُ وتنفعُ مجتمعه ؛ كالطب والرياضيات ونحوها ، أو يُسعى به إلى حرفةٍ يسترزقُ الله بها حلالاً ، وتحفظه عن أن يكون عالةً يتكفَّفُ الناس.
ومن جملة ما يحفظُهُ الطالب : « قواعد العقائد » أو « العقيدة
Foreword
We praise You humbly, O Possessor of Majesty and Pride, and we pray and send peace longingly upon our life and refuge, Your beloved Muhammad, possessor of praise and splendor, and upon his noble family, his distinguished companions, and their followers in excellence until the day the heavens surge.
To proceed:
From centuries not counted, a grievous calamity befell us: the methods of acquiring knowledge changed upon our nation, and its curricula vanished that were, not long ago, a reason for nations to be followers while we had precedence, and for others to have civilization while we had culture; on a day when reason had sanctity whatever its orientation and purpose, and was only founded upon sound consideration and correct thought. The journey of reason began from the primary schools (al-katatib), among the pillars and prayer niches; the child learns the basics of reading and writing, memorizes the great Quran, thus gathering to his side nearly fifty thousand linguistic words rich in expression and vitality, and it suffices that it is the inimitable speech of Allah the Exalted, to which falsehood cannot come from before it nor from behind it. And he memorizes along with that some of the prophetic traditions, scientific poems, and literary poetry; thus his soul is polished with good literature without him feeling, for he memorizes truth and is not filled with lies, and the brilliance of expression blossoms in his soul, and the sun of meanings shines upon his tongue and his fingers despite their tenderness; so he speaks well with the tongue, is granted sharpness of the heart, and writes wondrous eloquence.
Then he passes to trustworthy hands; they teach him the principles of the sciences gradually, and care for him with the wise care of fathers for sons, combining for him upbringing with knowledge, and show him gentleness; if his soul becomes unruly, they restrain him with the restraint of a keen person with the stick of discipline, and if he becomes lazy, they prod him with the spur of firmness until his spear becomes soft and his trunk straightens.
He then reads the scientific texts (al-mutun) whose excellence the fingers of specialists have clasped, and time has proven the goodness of their news and state; he memorizes them by heart if he has that faculty, otherwise he is given time and not neglected, until he practices that in a environment full of competition and encouragement. If it is difficult for him, his teacher is satisfied with his understanding, for how many whose hand fell short in memorization but mastered understanding have taken the forelock of knowledge?
So the student alternates between al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah in grammar, memorizing the first with its examples, understanding its sentences, and advancing by memorizing the second until the years of commentary become ready for him at his maturity and youth. With them his tongue becomes fluent in Arabic. Whoever does not take a share of the foundations of Arabic enabling him to read the sciences... often he is directed to general sciences that benefit him and his society, like medicine, mathematics and the like, or he is taken to a craft by which he seeks lawful provision from Allah, and which protects him from being a burden begging from people.
And among what the student memorizes: 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (Foundations of Doctrines) or 'al-'Aqidah' (the Creed).
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is an introduction by the author/editor describing the traditional Islamic educational method and lamenting its change. It begins by praising Allah and invoking blessings on the Prophet, then proceeds to describe a grievous change in the methods of acquiring knowledge. The author states that the old method was the reason for the Islamic nation's precedence and civilization. He then details the journey of the student: starting in the kuttab (primary schools), the child learns reading and writing, memorizes the entire Quran (nearly fifty thousand words), and along with that memorizes hadith, scientific poems, and poetry. This polishes his soul with good literature and develops his eloquence. Then the student moves to trustworthy teachers who teach him the principles of sciences with a combination of discipline and gentleness. He reads and memorizes the classical texts (mutun) that are well-regarded by specialists. The author emphasizes that if the student cannot memorize, understanding is acceptable. He specifically mentions the two famous grammar texts: al-Ajrumiyyah and al-Alfiyyah. Mastering these allows the student to proceed to other sciences; otherwise, he may be directed to practical fields like medicine or mathematics, or learn a trade. The passage concludes by stating that among the texts the student memorizes is 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (or 'al-'Aqidah'), indicating the importance of creedal studies.

The Ideal Learning Environment and Memorization
Sanusi describes the teacher's approach and the initial memorization stage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of a nurturing yet disciplined environment and the role of memorization in early Islamic education.

Core Idea
A firm but loving teacher cultivates both knowledge and character.

Sanusi begins by painting a picture of the ideal educational setting: a skilled teacher who combines discipline with gentleness. The student is guided through memorization of foundational texts, with the teacher adapting methods based on the student's abilities. This passage shows that memorization is not mere repetition but a step toward understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the teacher's role
Notice how the teacher balances discipline and care. Identify the metaphors used (stick of discipline, spur of severity).
The teacher is like a father, combining upbringing with knowledge.
2
Understand the memorization phase
The student reads and memorizes scientific texts (al-mutūn al-ʿilmiyya). Consider why memorization is emphasized here.
Memorization is done by heart if possible; otherwise understanding suffices.
3
Reflect on the competitive environment
The learning is set in a context of competition and encouragement. How does this motivate the student?
The environment is designed to foster excellence.

براعة التعبير ، وتُشرِقُ على مِقْوَلِهِ وبَنانِهِ على نعومتها شمس المعاني ؛ فيحسنُ القول باللسان ، ويُرزقُ حدَّةَ الجَنان ، ويُسطّر روائع البيان ثم يصير إلى أيد أمينة ؛ تُقرتُهُ مبادئ العلوم ، وترعاه رعاية الآباء الرشيدة للأبناء ، فتجمعُ له التربية إلى العلم ، وتحنو عليه برفق ، فإن حرنَتْ نفسه زجرته زجر الحريص عليه بعصا التأديب ، وإِن كَسِلَ هُمَزَ بمهماز الشدَّة ؛ حتى تلين قناته ، ويستقيم جذعُهُ مفعم بالمنافسة والتشجيع ، فإن تعسر عليه رضي منه أستاذه بالفهم ، فكم ممَّنْ قصر باعُهُ في الحفظ وملك الحفظ رضي ناصية العلم فيتقلب الطالب بين ( الآجرومية » و « الألفية » في النحو ، فيحفظ الأولى بأمثلتها ، ويفهم جملها ، ويدرج بحفظ الثانية إلى أن تتهيَّاً له سنوات الشرح عند نضجه ويفاعته ، وبها يدرج لسانه على العربية فمن لم يأخذ بأصول العربية بطرف يمكّنه من قراءة العلوم.. فغالباً ما يُمالُ به إلى علوم عامَّةٍ تنفعُهُ وتنفعُ مجتمعه ؛ كالطب والرياضيات ونحوها ، أو يُسعى به إلى حرفةٍ يسترزقُ الله بها حلالاً ، وتحفظه عن أن يكون عالةً يتكفَّفُ الناس ومن جملة ما يحفظُهُ الطالب : « قواعد العقائد » أو « العقيدة الصغرى » ، أو ( منظومة ابن عاشر » ، أو ( جوهرة التوحيد » وغيرها من المنظومات العقدية ، وقُلْ مثل هذا في عموم المتون العلمية على اختلافها ؛ كـ ( البيقونية » في مصطلح الحديث ، و نظم الورقات » في الأصول ، و الرحبية » في الفرائض ، و ( الجزرية » في التجويد ، و السلَّم المنورق » في المنطق ، و « متن العزّي » في التصريف ، و أرجوزة ابن سينا » في الطب ، في سن طينها لين وطبعها قابل ؛ فمتى ما ولَّتْ ولَّتْ معها ملكة الحفظ لتصير إلى كلفة وعناء ثم يدرس إذا بدأت مخايل النجابة تظهر عليه أصول العلوم ، ويتعلم مجرداتها ؛ من الرياضيات وغيرها ، ويقرأ فيما يقرأ : « شرح المقدمات السنوسية » بعد حفظ متنها ، وبها يتعرف على معالم هذا الفنّ ، فلا يزال يتدرّج فيه ؛ من صغرى صغراه » إلى « كبراه » ، حتى ترسخ في نفسه حقائق العلوم لا عن تقليد ذميم ، ولا عن تعصب أعمى ، بل كلُّ ذلك بالبراهين التي تقصَّتها وتأثرتها عيون أهل الخبرة ، لتسلمَهُ إلى شمس الحقيقة في رابعة النهار ، فلا وَهُم ولا جحد • فإن أراد التخصُّص وآنَ أوانه ، ورأى أساتيذُه فيه التأهل له .. أخذوا بيده فأقرؤوه من الكتب مطولاتها ، وأسعفوه بكل إجابة عند حَزَنِ مشكلاتها ، حتى يريضَ عليها ويألفها ، ويصير رأساً بها . ويمشي بها خبباً بعد كودنة(١) (١) الخبب : السير السريع ، والكودنة : السير البطيء مع تثاقل
The eloquence of expression, and the sun of meanings shines upon his speech and his fingers in their tenderness; so he speaks well with the tongue, and is granted sharpness of mind, and he writes marvels of eloquence. Then it passes to trustworthy hands: they teach him the principles of the sciences, and care for him with the wise care of fathers for sons, combining for him upbringing with knowledge, and be gentle with him. If his soul becomes stubborn, he rebukes him with the rebuke of one eager for him, with the stick of discipline. And if he is lazy, he prods him with the spur of severity until his spear shaft becomes pliable and his trunk straightens. So he reads the scientific texts (al-mutūn al-ʿilmiyya) whose merits the fingers of specialists have grasped, and whose goodness of report and experience days have proven. And he memorizes them by heart if he has this faculty; otherwise he is given time but not neglected, until he becomes practiced in that, in an environment full of competition and encouragement. If it becomes difficult for him, his teacher is satisfied with understanding. For how many whose reach fell short in memorization and [yet] possessed understanding have attained the pinnacle of knowledge! So the student moves back and forth between al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya in grammar (naḥw). He memorizes the first with its examples, and understands its sentences, and proceeds by memorizing the second until the years of commentary become ready for him at his maturity and adolescence. By it his tongue becomes trained in Arabic. So whoever does not take hold of the principles of Arabic with a handle that enables him to read the sciences... then often he is directed toward general sciences that benefit him and his community, like medicine, mathematics, and similar, or is led to a trade by which he may earn lawful provision from God, and which preserves him from being a burden on people, begging from them. Among what the student memorizes: 'The Rules of Creeds' (Qawāʿid al-ʿĀqāʾid) or 'The Small Creed' (al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā), or 'The Poem of Ibn ʿĀshir' (Manẓūma Ibn ʿĀshir), or 'The Jewel of Tawḥīd' (Jawharat al-Tawḥīd), and other creedal poems. Say the same regarding scientific texts in general of various types, like al-Bayqūniyya in the terminology of ḥadīth, Naẓm al-Waraqāt in Uṣūl, al-Raḥbiyya in inheritance, al-Jazariyya in tajwīd, al-Sullam al-Munawraq in logic (manṭiq), Matn al-ʿIzzī in morphology (taṣrīf), and Urjūza Ibn Sīnā in medicine, in an age when their clay is soft and their nature receptive. For whenever the faculty of memorization passes away, it passes away with it, becoming toil and hardship. Then, if signs of excellence begin to appear on him, he studies the foundations of the sciences (uṣūl al-ʿulūm) and their abstracts, including mathematics and others. And among what he reads is 'The Commentary on the Sanūsī Prolegomena' (Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya) after memorizing its text, and by it he learns the landmarks of this art (al-fann). He continues to progress in it from its smallest to its largest until the realities of the sciences become firmly rooted in his soul, not through blameworthy imitation nor blind partisanship, but all through the proofs that the eyes of experts have scrutinized and absorbed, to deliver him to the sun of truth in the daylight, without delusion or denial. So if he wishes to specialize and its time comes, and his teachers see in him the qualification for it, they take his hand and have him read the longer versions of books, and assist him with every answer in the difficulty of its problems, until he becomes accustomed to them and familiar with them, and becomes a leader in them, and walks through them with a fast gait after a slow one. (1) al-Khabab: swift walking, and al-Kūdana: slow walking with heaviness.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a description of the ideal traditional Islamic educational methodology, from early memorization to specialized study in kalām. The author begins by emphasizing the importance of a nurturing but firm environment, where the student is both encouraged and disciplined. The student first memorizes foundational texts (mutūn) like al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya in grammar, then moves to creedal texts like Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid and al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughra, alongside texts in other sciences (ḥadīth, uṣūl, tajwīd, etc.). After memorization, the student studies the abstract principles (uṣūl al-ʿulūm) and mathematics, then reads Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya after memorizing its base text. This commentary introduces the student to kalām. The learning is based on proofs, not blind imitation. Finally, for specialization, the student reads longer works with teacher guidance until mastery. The footnote explains two terms for speed of progress.

Key Terms
المتون العلمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that memorization is the only goal and that understanding comes later or is secondary.
Correction: The passage shows that understanding is valued; if memorization is too hard, the teacher accepts understanding alone, and many with understanding reached the peak of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the teacher do if the student cannot memorize?
A) The teacher gives up on the student.
B) The teacher is satisfied with understanding.
C) The teacher forces the student to memorize anyway.
D) The teacher sends the student to another field.

Key Takeaway
A nurturing but firm teacher creates an environment where memorization is valued but understanding is also respected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever resisted discipline in your pursuit of knowledge? How does the image of a teacher who rebukes 'with the stick of discipline' challenge your comfort zone, and what will you do about it today?
?
The passage says 'if he is lazy, he prods him with the spur of severity.' Where in your life right now are you being lazy in seeking beneficial knowledge, and who or what could serve as that spur for you?
?
Are you genuinely satisfied with understanding when you cannot memorize, or do you feel inadequate? How will you change your approach to learning based on the truth that understanding alone can lead to the pinnacle of knowledge?

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
From Grammar to Creed: The Early Curriculum
Sanusi enumerates the grammar and creed texts memorized in early education.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sequence of texts a student memorizes in grammar and creed as part of the traditional curriculum.

Core Idea
The student moves from grammar to creed, memorizing texts that build linguistic and theological foundations.

After establishing the learning environment, Sanusi details the specific texts a student memorizes. He starts with grammar (al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya) to train the tongue in Arabic, then moves to creedal poems like Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid and Jawharat al-Tawḥīd. This progression ensures the student has the tools to understand the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammar texts
Find al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya. Understand their roles: the first is memorized with examples, the second later for commentary.
Grammar is the foundation for reading all sciences.
2
Note the creedal texts
Look at the listed creedal poems: Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid, al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā, Manẓūma Ibn ʿĀshir, Jawharat al-Tawḥīd. These are memorized for theological grounding.
These texts summarize core beliefs in poetic form.
3
Consider the alternative path
The passage mentions that if a student does not master Arabic grammar, he may be directed to general sciences like medicine or a trade.
This shows the practicality of the system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
براعة التعبير ، وتُشرِقُ على مِقْوَلِهِ وبَنانِهِ على نعومتها شمس المعاني ؛ فيحسنُ القول باللسان ، ويُرزقُ حدَّةَ الجَنان ، ويُسطّر روائع البيان ثم يصير إلى أيد أمينة ؛ تُقرتُهُ مبادئ العلوم ، وترعاه رعاية الآباء الرشيدة للأبناء ، فتجمعُ له التربية إلى العلم ، وتحنو عليه برفق ، فإن حرنَتْ نفسه زجرته زجر الحريص عليه بعصا التأديب ، وإِن كَسِلَ هُمَزَ بمهماز الشدَّة ؛ حتى تلين قناته ، ويستقيم جذعُهُ فيقرأ المتون العلمية التي انعقدَتْ خناصر المتخصصين على فضلها ، وأثبتت الأيام حُسنَ خَبرِها ومَخْبَرِها ، ويحفظها عن ظهر قلب إن كانت له هذه الملكة ، وإلا فيُمهل ولا يُهمل ، حتى يتمرَّس على ذلك ، في وسط مفعم بالمنافسة والتشجيع ، فإن تعسر عليه رضي منه أستاذه بالفهم ، فكم ممَّنْ قصر باعُهُ في الحفظ وملك الحفظ رضي ناصية العلم فيتقلب الطالب بين ( الآجرومية » و « الألفية » في النحو ، فيحفظ الأولى بأمثلتها ، ويفهم جملها ، ويدرج بحفظ الثانية إلى أن تتهيَّاً له سنوات الشرح عند نضجه ويفاعته ، وبها يدرج لسانه على العربية فمن لم يأخذ بأصول العربية بطرف يمكّنه من قراءة العلوم.. فغالباً ما يُمالُ به إلى علوم عامَّةٍ تنفعُهُ وتنفعُ مجتمعه ؛ كالطب والرياضيات ونحوها ، أو يُسعى به إلى حرفةٍ يسترزقُ الله بها حلالاً ، وتحفظه عن أن يكون عالةً يتكفَّفُ الناس ومن جملة ما يحفظُهُ الطالب : « قواعد العقائد » أو « العقيدة الصغرى » ، أو ( منظومة ابن عاشر » ، أو ( جوهرة التوحيد » وغيرها من المنظومات العقدية ، وقُلْ مثل هذا في عموم المتون العلمية على اختلافها ؛ كـ ( البيقونية » في مصطلح الحديث ، و نظم الورقات » في الأصول ، و الرحبية » في الفرائض ، و ( الجزرية » في التجويد ، و السلَّم المنورق » في المنطق ، و « متن العزّي » في التصريف ، و أرجوزة ابن سينا » في الطب ، في سن طينها لين وطبعها قابل ؛ فمتى ما ولَّتْ ولَّتْ معها ملكة الحفظ لتصير إلى كلفة وعناء ثم يدرس إذا بدأت مخايل النجابة تظهر عليه أصول العلوم ، ويتعلم مجرداتها ؛ من الرياضيات وغيرها ، ويقرأ فيما يقرأ : « شرح المقدمات السنوسية » بعد حفظ متنها ، وبها يتعرف على معالم هذا الفنّ ، فلا يزال يتدرّج فيه ؛ من صغرى صغراه » إلى « كبراه » ، حتى ترسخ في نفسه حقائق العلوم لا عن تقليد ذميم ، ولا عن تعصب أعمى ، بل كلُّ ذلك بالبراهين التي تقصَّتها وتأثرتها عيون أهل الخبرة ، لتسلمَهُ إلى شمس الحقيقة في رابعة النهار ، فلا وَهُم ولا جحد • فإن أراد التخصُّص وآنَ أوانه ، ورأى أساتيذُه فيه التأهل له .. أخذوا بيده فأقرؤوه من الكتب مطولاتها ، وأسعفوه بكل إجابة عند حَزَنِ مشكلاتها ، حتى يريضَ عليها ويألفها ، ويصير رأساً بها . ويمشي بها خبباً بعد كودنة(١) (١) الخبب : السير السريع ، والكودنة : السير البطيء مع تثاقل
The eloquence of expression, and the sun of meanings shines upon his speech and his fingers in their tenderness; so he speaks well with the tongue, and is granted sharpness of mind, and he writes marvels of eloquence. Then it passes to trustworthy hands: they teach him the principles of the sciences, and care for him with the wise care of fathers for sons, combining for him upbringing with knowledge, and be gentle with him. If his soul becomes stubborn, he rebukes him with the rebuke of one eager for him, with the stick of discipline. And if he is lazy, he prods him with the spur of severity until his spear shaft becomes pliable and his trunk straightens. So he reads the scientific texts (al-mutūn al-ʿilmiyya) whose merits the fingers of specialists have grasped, and whose goodness of report and experience days have proven. And he memorizes them by heart if he has this faculty; otherwise he is given time but not neglected, until he becomes practiced in that, in an environment full of competition and encouragement. If it becomes difficult for him, his teacher is satisfied with understanding. For how many whose reach fell short in memorization and [yet] possessed understanding have attained the pinnacle of knowledge! So the student moves back and forth between al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya in grammar (naḥw). He memorizes the first with its examples, and understands its sentences, and proceeds by memorizing the second until the years of commentary become ready for him at his maturity and adolescence. By it his tongue becomes trained in Arabic. So whoever does not take hold of the principles of Arabic with a handle that enables him to read the sciences... then often he is directed toward general sciences that benefit him and his community, like medicine, mathematics, and similar, or is led to a trade by which he may earn lawful provision from God, and which preserves him from being a burden on people, begging from them. Among what the student memorizes: 'The Rules of Creeds' (Qawāʿid al-ʿĀqāʾid) or 'The Small Creed' (al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā), or 'The Poem of Ibn ʿĀshir' (Manẓūma Ibn ʿĀshir), or 'The Jewel of Tawḥīd' (Jawharat al-Tawḥīd), and other creedal poems. Say the same regarding scientific texts in general of various types, like al-Bayqūniyya in the terminology of ḥadīth, Naẓm al-Waraqāt in Uṣūl, al-Raḥbiyya in inheritance, al-Jazariyya in tajwīd, al-Sullam al-Munawraq in logic (manṭiq), Matn al-ʿIzzī in morphology (taṣrīf), and Urjūza Ibn Sīnā in medicine, in an age when their clay is soft and their nature receptive. For whenever the faculty of memorization passes away, it passes away with it, becoming toil and hardship. Then, if signs of excellence begin to appear on him, he studies the foundations of the sciences (uṣūl al-ʿulūm) and their abstracts, including mathematics and others. And among what he reads is 'The Commentary on the Sanūsī Prolegomena' (Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya) after memorizing its text, and by it he learns the landmarks of this art (al-fann). He continues to progress in it from its smallest to its largest until the realities of the sciences become firmly rooted in his soul, not through blameworthy imitation nor blind partisanship, but all through the proofs that the eyes of experts have scrutinized and absorbed, to deliver him to the sun of truth in the daylight, without delusion or denial. So if he wishes to specialize and its time comes, and his teachers see in him the qualification for it, they take his hand and have him read the longer versions of books, and assist him with every answer in the difficulty of its problems, until he becomes accustomed to them and familiar with them, and becomes a leader in them, and walks through them with a fast gait after a slow one. (1) al-Khabab: swift walking, and al-Kūdana: slow walking with heaviness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Method of Learning
beginner
6 min
Memorization Across the Sciences and Entering Kalam
Sanusi lists memorization texts in various sciences and describes the transition to studying uṣūl al-ʿulūm and kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the breadth of texts memorized in various sciences and the transition to studying the foundations of kalam.

Core Idea
After memorizing texts across many sciences, the student studies uṣūl al-ʿulūm and reads Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya to enter the field of kalam.

Sanusi expands the curriculum beyond grammar and creed, listing memorization texts in ḥadīth, uṣūl, inheritance, tajwīd, logic, morphology, and medicine. Then he describes the next stage: when signs of excellence appear, the student studies the foundations of the sciences (including mathematics) and reads the commentary on Sanusi's Prolegomena after memorizing its text. This marks the formal beginning of kalam study, grounded in proofs not imitation.

Guided Path
1
List the additional scientific texts
Identify each text and its field: al-Bayqūniyya (ḥadīth), Naẓm al-Waraqāt (uṣūl), al-Raḥbiyya (inheritance), al-Jazariyya (tajwīd), al-Sullam al-Munawraq (logic), Matn al-ʿIzzī (morphology), Urjūza Ibn Sīnā (medicine).
The variety shows the comprehensive nature of the curriculum.
2
Understand the study of uṣūl al-ʿulūm
After memorization, the student studies the foundations of the sciences and mathematics. This abstract study prepares for kalam.
Uṣūl al-ʿulūm includes principles common to all sciences.
3
Recognize the entry into kalam
The student reads Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya after memorizing its text. This commentary is by Sanusi himself and introduces kalam based on proofs.
This is the first dedicated kalam text in the curriculum.

Source Text

The eloquence of expression, and the sun of meanings shines upon his speech and his fingers in their tenderness; so he speaks well with the tongue, and is granted sharpness of mind, and he writes marvels of eloquence. Commentary
This passage is a description of the ideal traditional Islamic educational methodology, from early memorization to specialized study in kalām. The author begins by emphasizing the importance of a nurturing but firm environment, where the student is both encouraged and disciplined. The student first memorizes foundational texts (mutūn) like al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya in grammar, then moves to creedal texts like Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid and al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughra, alongside texts in other sciences (ḥadīth, uṣūl, tajwīd, etc.). After memorization, the student studies the abstract principles (uṣūl al-ʿulūm) and mathematics, then reads Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya after memorizing its base text. This commentary introduces the student to kalām. The learning is based on proofs, not blind imitation. Finally, for specialization, the student reads longer works with teacher guidance until mastery. The footnote explains two terms for speed of progress.

?

Specialization in Kalam and Mastery
Sanusi describes the final specialization phase in kalam, from teacher-guided reading to mastery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final stage of specialization in kalam, where the student reads longer works and achieves mastery under teacher guidance.

Core Idea
Specialization in kalam requires reading longer books with teacher assistance until the student becomes a leader in the field.

Sanusi concludes the educational journey by describing specialization. When the student is ready and teachers confirm his qualification, he reads longer versions of books (muṭawwalāt) with their guidance. He persists through difficulties until he becomes familiar with the material and eventually a master. The footnote explains two types of pace: quick (khabab) and slow (kūdana).

Guided Path
1
Understand the prerequisites for specialization
The student must be qualified and the teachers must see his readiness. Then they take his hand.
Specialization is not automatic; it requires demonstrated ability.
2
Note the method of advanced study
The student reads longer versions of books and receives answers to difficult problems. This is an interactive process.
The goal is to become accustomed (yarīḍ) and familiar (ya'laf) with the material.
3
Recognize the outcome
The student becomes a leader (ra's) and proceeds quickly after initial slowness.
Mastery is achieved through persistent effort.

الأصول ، و الرحبية » في الفرائض ، و ( الجزرية » في التجويد ، و السلَّم المنورق » في المنطق ، و « متن العزّي » في التصريف ، و أرجوزة ابن سينا » في الطب ، في سن طينها لين وطبعها قابل ؛ فمتى ما ولَّتْ وبها يتعرف على معالم هذا الفنّ ، فلا يزال يتدرّج فيه ؛ من صغرى صغراه » إلى « كبراه » ، حتى ترسخ في نفسه حقائق العلوم لا عن تقليد ذميم ، ولا عن تعصب أعمى ، بل كلُّ ذلك بالبراهين التي تقصَّتها وتأثرتها The eloquence of expression, and the sun of meanings shines upon his speech and his fingers in their tenderness; so he speaks well with the tongue, and is granted sharpness of mind, and he writes marvels of eloquence. Then it passes to trustworthy hands: they teach him the principles of the sciences, and care for him with the wise care of fathers for sons, combining for him upbringing with knowledge, and be gentle with him. If his soul becomes stubborn, he rebukes him with the rebuke of one eager for him, with the stick of discipline. And if he is lazy, he prods him with the spur of severity until his spear shaft becomes pliable and his trunk straightens. So he reads the scientific texts (al-mutūn al-ʿilmiyya) whose merits the fingers of specialists have grasped, and whose goodness of report and experience days have proven. And he memorizes them by heart if he has this faculty; otherwise he is given time but not neglected, until he becomes practiced in that, in an environment full of competition and encouragement. If it becomes difficult for him, his teacher is satisfied with understanding. For how many whose reach fell short in memorization and [yet] possessed understanding have attained the pinnacle of knowledge! So the student moves back and forth between al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya in grammar (naḥw). He memorizes the first with its examples, and understands its sentences, and proceeds by memorizing the second until the years of commentary become ready for him at his maturity and adolescence. By it his tongue becomes trained in Arabic. So whoever does not take hold of the principles of Arabic with a handle that enables him to read the sciences... then often he is directed toward general sciences that benefit him and his community, like medicine, mathematics, and similar, or is led to a trade by which he may earn lawful provision from God, and which preserves him from being a burden on people, begging from them. Among what the student memorizes: 'The Rules of Creeds' (Qawāʿid al-ʿĀqāʾid) or 'The Small Creed' (al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā), or 'The Poem of Ibn ʿĀshir' (Manẓūma Ibn ʿĀshir), or 'The Jewel of Tawḥīd' (Jawharat al-Tawḥīd), and other creedal poems. Say the same regarding scientific texts in general of various types, like al-Bayqūniyya in the terminology of ḥadīth, Naẓm al-Waraqāt in Uṣūl, al-Raḥbiyya in inheritance, al-Jazariyya in tajwīd, al-Sullam al-Munawraq in logic (manṭiq), Matn al-ʿIzzī in morphology (taṣrīf), and Urjūza Ibn Sīnā in medicine, in an age when their clay is soft and their nature receptive. For whenever the faculty of memorization passes away, it passes away with it, becoming toil and hardship. Then, if signs of excellence begin to appear on him, he studies the foundations of the sciences (uṣūl al-ʿulūm) and their abstracts, including mathematics and others. And among what he reads is 'The Commentary on the Sanūsī Prolegomena' (Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya) after memorizing its text, and by it he learns the landmarks of this art (al-fann). He continues to progress in it from its smallest to its largest until the realities of the sciences become firmly rooted in his soul, not through blameworthy imitation nor blind partisanship, but all through the proofs that the eyes of experts have scrutinized and absorbed, to deliver him to the sun of truth in the daylight, without delusion or denial. So if he wishes to specialize and its time comes, and his teachers see in him the qualification for it, they take his hand and have him read the longer versions of books, and assist him with every answer in the difficulty of its problems, until he becomes accustomed to them and familiar with them, and becomes a leader in them, and walks through them with a fast gait after a slow one. (1) al-Khabab: swift walking, and al-Kūdana: slow walking with heaviness.

Commentary
This passage is a description of the ideal traditional Islamic educational methodology, from early memorization to specialized study in kalām. The author begins by emphasizing the importance of a nurturing but firm environment, where the student is both encouraged and disciplined. The student first memorizes foundational texts (mutūn) like al-Ājurrūmiyya and al-Alfiyya in grammar, then moves to creedal texts like Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid and al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughra, alongside texts in other sciences (ḥadīth, uṣūl, tajwīd, etc.). After memorization, the student studies the abstract principles (uṣūl al-ʿulūm) and mathematics, then reads Sharḥ al-Muqaddimāt al-Sanūsiyya after memorizing its base text. This commentary introduces the student to kalām. The learning is based on proofs, not blind imitation. Finally, for specialization, the student reads longer works with teacher guidance until mastery. The footnote explains two terms for speed of progress.

Method of Learning
beginner

The Traditional Method of Learning Islamic Sciences
The author explains the traditional step-by-step method of studying Islamic sciences from memorization to specialization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional step-by-step method of studying Islamic sciences, from memorization of foundational texts to specialization under expert guidance.

Core Idea
Islamic learning progresses from memorizing versified texts to mastering proofs and specialization.

The author outlines the classical curriculum of Islamic education, emphasizing a structured progression. Students begin by memorizing key texts while young, then move to studying commentaries and proofs, and finally specialize with the guidance of teachers. This passage gives you a clear picture of how traditional Islamic sciences were taught and mastered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation. Notice the sequential stages from memorization to specialization.
The passage is dense with lists of texts; don't worry about memorizing them now.
2
Identify the stages of learning
Look for the three main phases: memorization of mutun, study of fundamentals and commentaries, and specialization with longer books. Underline key phrases that mark transitions.
The phrase 'فإن أراد التخصُّص' signals the specialization stage.
3
Understand the role of memorization
The author stresses that memorization should occur when the mind is receptive (young age). If delayed, it becomes difficult. This is not mere rote; it is the foundation for later understanding.
The metaphor 'soft clay' emphasizes the importance of early training.
4
Trace the progression from Sughra to Kubra
The student starts with the smallest elementary work (sughra) and progresses to the largest (kubra). This gradual increase ensures solid grounding before advanced study.
This principle applies within each science, especially kalam.
5
Reflect on the ultimate goal
The passage says truths become ingrained through proofs, not through imitation or partisanship. The end result is clear certainty, leading to specialization where the student becomes an authority.
Notice the contrast between 'taqlid dhamim' and proofs of experts.

The 'Sughra' or 'Nazm Ibn Ashir' or 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' and other theological versifications, and say the like regarding all scientific texts despite their differences; like 'al-Bayquniyya' in hadith terminology, and 'Nazm al-Waraqat' in usul, and 'al-Rahbiyya' in inheritance, and 'al-Jazariyya' in tajwid, and 'al-Sullam al-Munawraq' in logic, and 'Matan al-Izzi' in morphology, and 'Urjuzat Ibn Sina' in medicine, while their clay is soft and their nature receptive; for whenever the memorization faculty leaves, it leaves with it, becoming a toil and hardship. Then he studies, when signs of excellence begin to appear on him, the fundamentals of the sciences, and learns their abstracts; from mathematics and others, and among what he reads is: 'Sharh al-Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya' after memorizing its text, and through it he becomes acquainted with the landmarks of this art. He continues to progress in it from its smallest 'Sughra' to its 'Kubra', until the truths of the sciences become ingrained in his soul, not through blameworthy imitation nor through blind partisanship, but all of that by the proofs which the eyes of the experts have tracked and absorbed, so that it delivers him to the sun of truth in broad daylight, so there is no delusion nor denial. Then if he wishes to specialize and his time has come, and his teachers see in him qualification for it, they take his hand and make him read from the longer books, and assist him with every answer at the difficulty of its problems, until he becomes accustomed to them and familiar with them, and becomes a leader in them, and walks briskly in them after plodding.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage describes the traditional method of learning Islamic sciences, presenting it as a structured progression. It begins by listing foundational versified texts (mutun) across various disciplines—theology, hadith, usul, inheritance, tajwid, logic, morphology, and medicine—emphasizing that students should memorize them while young (soft clay, receptive nature). The author warns that if the memorization faculty is not exercised early, it becomes difficult later. Once signs of intelligence appear, the student moves to studying the fundamentals and abstract sciences, including mathematics. A key text mentioned is 'Sharh al-Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya', studied after memorizing its original. Through this commentary, the student learns the landmarks of this art (kalam) and progresses from the smallest elementary work to the largest. The goal is for truths to become ingrained through proofs, not through blameworthy imitation (taqlid) or blind partisanship. The proofs cited are those established by past experts. This leads to clear certainty. Then, if the student wishes to specialize and is deemed ready, teachers guide him through longer books, helping him overcome difficulties until he masters the subject and becomes an authority, moving quickly after initial slowness.

شرح المقدمات السنوسية
تقليد
براهين
التخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Memorization is only rote learning without understanding.
Correction: The author shows memorization is followed by studying commentaries and proofs, leading to true understanding and mastery.

Checkpoint

A) To criticize the original text.
B) To become acquainted with the landmarks of this art (kalam).
C) To memorize it as well.
D) To prepare for specialization immediately.

Key Takeaway
The traditional Islamic curriculum progresses from memorization of foundational texts, through study of commentaries and proofs, to specialization under expert guidance, aiming for certainty through evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your 'clay' is still soft—what foundational text are you committing to memory right now? If none, what is holding you back, and how will you start before the capacity fades?
?
Which of your religious beliefs are based on proof, and which on imitation? Identify one area where you have accepted something without evidence—will you seek out the proofs as described in this passage?
?
Are you ready to submit to qualified teachers who will 'take your hand' through difficulties? Where is pride blocking you from seeking guidance and persevering until you become a leader? What step will you take this week?

Critique of Modern Secular Education
The author criticizes modern education for neglecting memorization and producing graduates without real knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of modern secular education, specifically his condemnation of the neglect of Arabic, lack of memorization, and the emptiness of modern certificates.

Core Idea
Modern certificates often testify to ignorance, not knowledge.

In this passage, the author launches a scathing critique of contemporary education, contrasting it with the traditional emphasis on memorizing foundational texts (mutun) and preserving the Arabic language. He argues that students waste years learning irrelevant languages and emerge with degrees that prove their ignorance. As you read, notice the emotional language and the use of Quranic verse and poetry to reinforce the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then consult the English translation to ensure you understand the author's main points.
The Arabic uses strong, vivid language.
2
Identify the specific criticisms
List the three main targets of the author's critique: foreign language learning, lack of memorization, and the certificate system.
3
Examine the use of textual evidence
Notice the Quranic verse and the quote from al-Majd. How do they bolster the argument?
The Quranic verse warns against exchanging what is better for what is lower.
4
Reflect on the final outcome
The author concludes that modern education wastes years and produces empty credentials. Consider whether his critique applies to your own educational context.

وأيامنا العفنة يتعلَّم الطفل فيها رطانة الأعجمية العربية ، ويكتب بحرفٍ معوجٌ حروفَ لغته إلى لغات لا تتصل به ولا يتصل بها ، وتطولُ به الأيام وهو على هذه السبيل ؛ لينقطع إلى يوم أسود ، يرى فيه لغته الأم لغةَ ذُلُّ ومهانة ، ويرى ویری تخنثه بشوبها ببضع ألفاظ أجنبيّة عنها زينةً له وللسانه الألكَن ، فيصدق فيه قول المجد : ( ولا يشنأ هذه اللغة الشريفة إلا مَنِ اهْتافَ به ريحُ الشقاء ، ولا يختارُ عليها إلا مَنِ اعتاضَ السافية من الشَّحْواء ((۱) ثم هو لا يحفظ من المتون شيئاً ، بل لا يحفظ شيئاً ، إنما هي ثرثرة عن علوم لا تسمن ولا تغني من جوع ، ﴿ أَتَسْتَبْدِلُونَ الَّذِى هُوَ أَدْنَ بِالَّذِي هُوَ خَيْرُ ﴾ [البقرة : ٦١] ، يخرج الطالب بعدها يحمل أوراقاً تدعى بالشهادات ، تشهد له بأنه خاوي الوفاض ، رقيع المعلومات تائه المستقبل ، مشتَّتُ البال ، ضاعت منه زهرة عمره في قيل وقال إن سُئلَ تبالة ، وإن أجاب أضحك ! . ثم ينضم بعد ذلك إلى قطيع أمثاله ممَّنْ سبقه ، لاهثاً وراء لقمة العيش في غير ما أنفق من سنوات ؛ إذ يتعلم شيئاً ويعمل آخر ، فترى أيامنا شرقاً وغرباً تُضيَّعُ أعمارَ البشر في ثقافة يمكن تحصيلها عند الوعي والنضج بيسير أيام ، وتسرقُ جهودهم وأعمارهم بطيات الأوراق والألقاب ، ويحسبون أنَّهم على شيء .
And our rotten days, the child in them learns the gibberish of non-Arabic Arabic, and writes with a crooked letter the letters of his language to languages that have no connection to him nor he to them, and the days become long for him while he is on this path; so that he ends up in a black day, in which he sees his mother tongue as a language of humiliation and degradation, and he sees his effeminacy in mixing it with a few foreign words as an adornment for him and his lisping tongue. Thus the saying of al-Majd applies truly to him: 'Only he whom the wind of wretchedness has thrown will hate this noble language, and only he who has exchanged the dust-laden wind for the abundant well will choose another over it.' Then he does not memorize any texts at all—rather he memorizes nothing—it is merely chatter about sciences that neither nourish nor avail against hunger, (as Allah says: 'Would you exchange that which is better for that which is lower?' [al-Baqara 2:61]). The student then emerges carrying papers called certificates, which testify that he is empty-handed, a patchwork of knowledge, lost of future, scattered of mind, the flower of his life wasted in gossip; if asked he is confused, and if he answers he makes people laugh! Then he joins the herd of his peers who preceded him, panting after a livelihood in something other than what he spent years on; for he learns one thing and works another. So you see our days, east and west, wasting human lives in a culture that could be acquired when one is conscious and mature in a few days, and robbing their efforts and lives in layers of papers and titles, while they think they are upon something.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a critical evaluation of modern education, contrasting it with the traditional method described earlier. The author begins by characterizing contemporary times as 'rotten', where children learn 'gibberish of non-Arabic Arabic'—likely a mix of foreign language and poor Arabic—and write Arabic letters crookedly while learning foreign languages that have no connection to them. This continues for a long time, leading to a 'black day' where the child sees his own native language as a mark of humiliation and considers mixing it with foreign words as a decoration. The author quotes al-Majd (al-Fayruzabadi) criticizing those who despise Arabic and choose another language. Then he notes that such a student does not memorize any texts (mutun) at all; instead he engages in empty chatter about sciences that are useless, citing Quran 2:61 ('Do you exchange what is better for what is lower?'). The outcome is that the student graduates with certificates (shahadat) that testify to his emptiness: he has shallow knowledge, a lost future, and a scattered mind. His youth is wasted in pointless talk. After graduation, he joins the herd of job-seekers, working in a field unrelated to his studies, having wasted years. In conclusion, the author laments that modern education wastes human lives in a culture that could be learned quickly after maturity, while people are robbed of their efforts and lives through paper degrees and titles, yet they think they have achieved something.

شهادات
خاوي الوفاض
متون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects all foreign language learning.
Correction: The author criticizes the neglect of Arabic and Islamic sciences in favor of foreign languages that are poorly taught and irrelevant to the student's life. He does not oppose learning foreign languages per se, but condemns the prioritization of them over the student's own linguistic and religious heritage.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about certificates (شهادات) in modern education?
A) They are valuable and prove the student's knowledge.
B) They testify that the student is empty-handed and lacking real knowledge.
C) They are a necessary step for specialization.
D) They are the same as the traditional ijazah system.

Key Takeaway
Modern education, according to the author, prioritizes foreign languages and empty chatter over memorizing foundational texts, resulting in graduates whose certificates testify to their lack of real knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever valued a certificate or degree more than the actual knowledge it represents? Are there areas of your life where you are chasing credentials instead of substance? What is one step you can take today to prioritize true learning over empty credentials?
?
How do you personally view the Arabic language? Do you mix foreign words into your speech without thinking? In what specific way can you honor Arabic starting today, whether by memorizing a text, learning a new word, or simply avoiding unnecessary English phrases?
?
Think back on years of schooling: how much of what you learned truly 'nourished' your soul or prepared you for the akhira? Are there any subjects or habits you have carried into adulthood that are mere 'chatter'? What can you remove from your schedule this week to focus on what truly avails against the hunger of the grave?

Critique of Contemporary Education: Language and Memorization
Author criticizes modern education's neglect of Arabic and memorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of modern education, specifically the neglect of Arabic language and memorization, and the consequences he describes.

Core Idea
Modern education trades deep learning for shallow certificates, abandoning language and memory.

In this passage, the author laments the state of contemporary education, where children learn foreign gibberish instead of Arabic and memorize nothing of substance. He sees this as a recipe for wasted years and empty achievements. As you read, notice how he uses vivid imagery and quotes to drive his point home.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without stopping. Let the author's passionate critique sink in before analyzing.
2
Identify the two main complaints
Find where the author criticizes language teaching (first part) and where he criticizes lack of memorization (second part).
The transition is marked by 'ثم هو لا يحفظ'.
3
Examine the quotes used
The author quotes al-Majd and Quran 2:61. Consider what these add to his argument.
The quote from al-Majd emphasizes the nobility of Arabic.
4
Analyze the portrayal of the graduate
The author describes the graduate as empty, foolish, and lost. Notice the vivid imagery and the final consequence of chasing a livelihood unrelated to studies.
5
Reflect on the author's implied alternative
What does the author suggest education should prioritize? Consider his lament about wasted time on culture that could be acquired quickly.
He implies a return to Arabic mastery and memorization of texts.
