(۱) قاله في مقدمة ( القاموس المحيط » ، واهتاف : رمى ، والسافية : الرياح « المتربة ، والشحواء : البئر الواسعة العامرة الماء
And our rotten days: the child learns therein the gibberish of the non-Arabic language, and writes with a crooked letter the letters of his language into languages that neither connect to him nor does he connect to them, and the days prolong for him while he is on this path, until he is cut off to a black day in which he sees his mother tongue as a language of humiliation and degradation, and sees his effeminacy in mixing it with a few foreign words as an adornment for him and his stammering tongue. So the saying of al-Majd is verified in him: 'None despises this noble language except one whom the wind of wretchedness has cast upon, and none chooses another over it except one who has exchanged the dust-laden wind for a well full of water.' Then he does not memorize any texts, rather he does not memorize anything; it is only prattle about sciences that neither nourish nor avail against hunger. {Would you exchange that which is higher for that which is lower?} [al-Baqarah 2:61]. The student emerges after that carrying papers called certificates, bearing witness that he is empty-handed, shallow in knowledge, lost in the future, distracted in mind; the flower of his life has been wasted in idle talk—if asked, he is foolish, and if he answers, he makes people laugh! Then he joins the herd of his likes who preceded him, panting after a livelihood in a way different from what he spent of years; for he learns one thing and works another. So you see our days, east and west, wasting the lives of humans in a culture that could be acquired at the age of awareness and maturity in a few days, and they steal their efforts and lives in the folds of papers and titles, and they think that they are upon something. (1) He said it in the introduction to (al-Qamus al-Muhit), and 'ihtafa' means 'cast', and 'al-safiya' is the wind carrying dust, and 'al-shahwa' is the wide well full of water.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a critique of contemporary education by the author. It begins by lamenting that children learn the gibberish of a foreign language instead of Arabic, writing their own language with distorted letters. The author states that this prolongs their path until they end up in a dark day where they see their mother tongue as humiliating. He quotes al-Majd (likely al-Fayruzabadi) to emphasize that only the wretched despise Arabic. Then the author criticizes the lack of memorization: students memorize no texts, only prattle about useless sciences. He quotes the Quran (al-Baqarah 2:61) to rebuke exchanging the superior for the inferior. The student graduates with certificates that attest to his emptiness, foolishness, and confusion. After graduation, he joins the herd chasing a livelihood unrelated to his studies, having wasted years. The author concludes that our time squanders lives on culture that could be acquired quickly in maturity, and people think they are accomplished while they are not. The footnote explains the source of the quote and clarifies the meanings of obscure words.

The Lie of Specialization: A Critique of Superficial Learning
Author criticizes specialization and lists areas of ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of superficial specialization, especially among kalam professors, and why mastering fundamentals is essential.

Core Idea
True expertise requires mastering the basics first.

The author launches a sharp critique of specialization, calling it a 'lie' because many who claim expertise today lack even basic knowledge of their field. He gives the example of kalam professors who are ignorant of foundational definitions and advanced concepts. This sets the stage for why a proper foundation is needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main accusation
Read the first sentence where the author calls specialization a 'lie'. Notice how he describes the gap between claim and reality.
He uses the word 'ukdhuuba (أُكذوبة) which is a strong term.
2
Examine the examples of ignorance
Look at the list of things the professors do not know: definitions of substance, accident, attributes, etc. See how the list progresses from basics to advanced topics.
The terms like al-ahadiyya and al-wahidiyya are technical theological concepts.
3
Reflect on the author's tone
Notice the author's frustration and sarcasm ('these have become like puzzles'). Consider why he feels this is a serious problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك تأتيك أُكذوبة التخصُّص ، وحديث الأستاذية والعالمية ، فترى مَنْ يتخصَّص في العلوم اليوم لا يُتقن أبجدياتها ! ضَعفُ ظاهر ودعوى عريضة ، وتعال وانتفاخ ونفوس مريضة وعلى سبيل المثال بما يناسب الحال : أساتيذُ علم الكلام اليوم إن سألتهم عن حقائق الأشياء وأسباب العلم . . جهلوها ، وعن حدود الجوهر والعرض والصفات والتعلقات .. لم يعرفوها ، دع الأحديَّة والواحديَّة ، والاثنينية والتثليث ، وعن الجوامع عنك السؤال عن الأربعة ، والوجودات الأربعة ، والأكوان الأربعة ، والمطالب السبعة ، والمقولات العشرة ، فهذه أضحت لهم كالألغاز ! ولكنَّ الخير بحمد الله وفضله ، ثم بفضل علمائنا أدام الله نفعهـم ، ولا حرمنا الله عطفَهم .. باقٍ إلى أن يرثَ الله الأرض ومن عليها ؛ فأُمتنا أمة مباركة . والكتابُ الذي بين يديك سبق وأخبرتك أنه مما يطالعُه الطلاب لعلم الكلام في سنّ اليفاعة ، ويستفتحون به الطريقة ؛ فيتعرفون به على ما لا بدَّ لهم من الإحاطة به ، لا في الاعتقاد فحسب ، بل في عموم علوم الأصول والنظر العقلي ، وقلب النظر فيه لترى مصداق ذلك ، فكان من أوجب الواجب العناية به .
Then after that comes the lie of specialization, and talk of professorship and world-renowned scholarship. So you see one who specializes in sciences today does not master their alphabets! Apparent weakness and broad claim, and haughtiness, bloating, and sick souls. And for example, as appropriate to the situation: the professors of kalam (ilm al-kalam) today—if you ask them about the realities of things and the causes of knowledge, they are ignorant of them; and about the definitions of substance, accident, attributes, and relations, they do not know them; let alone oneness (al-ahadiyya) and individuality (al-wahidiyya), duality (al-ithnayniyya) and trinity (al-tathlith), and about the universals—do not ask about the four, the four existences, the four modes, the seven demands, and the ten categories—these have become for them like puzzles! But goodness, by the praise of Allah and His grace, and then by the virtue of our scholars—may Allah perpetuate their benefit, and may Allah not deprive us of their kindness—remains until Allah inherits the earth and whoever is upon it; for our nation is a blessed nation. And the book that is in your hands—I have already informed you that it is among what students of kalam read in the age of youth, and they open the method with it; so they become acquainted through it with what they must encompass, not only in belief (i'tiqad), but in all the sciences of principles (usul) and rational speculation (nazar 'aqli). And turn your consideration in it to see the truth of that. So it was among the most obligatory duties to take care of it.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a critique of specialization followed by an introduction to the book. The author begins by calling specialization a 'lie', noting that those who specialize today do not even master the basics. He then gives an example: professors of kalam are ignorant of fundamental topics like the realities of things, definitions of substance and accident, and higher concepts like al-ahadiyya and al-wahidiyya (aspects of divine oneness), and various classifications (the four, seven demands, ten categories). Despite this, the author asserts that goodness remains through Allah's grace and the scholars. Then he introduces the book: it is read by young students of kalam, they begin their method with it, and it teaches them what they must know—not only in creed but in all sciences of principles and rational speculation. He urges the reader to examine the book to see this truth, concluding that taking care of it is an obligation.

The Book as a Remedy: Introduction to the Study of Kalam
Author introduces the book as a remedy and describes its content

Learning Objective

Core Idea
This book equips beginners with essential knowledge in creed and rational speculation.

After the critique, the author shifts to a hopeful tone: goodness remains through Allah's grace and scholars. He then introduces the book, describing it as a beginner's text for kalam that covers necessary topics not only in belief but also in principles and rational speculation. This passage shows the book's importance and purpose.

Then after that comes the lie of specialization, and talk of professorship and world-renowned scholarship. So you see one who specializes in sciences today does not master their alphabets! Apparent weakness and broad claim, and haughtiness, bloating, and sick souls. And for example, as appropriate to the situation: the professors of kalam (ilm al-kalam) today—if you ask them about the realities of things and the causes of knowledge, they are ignorant of them; and about the definitions of substance, accident, attributes, and relations, they do not know them; let alone oneness (al-ahadiyya) and individuality (al-wahidiyya), duality (al-ithnayniyya) and trinity (al-tathlith), and about the universals—do not ask about the four, the four existences, the four modes, the seven demands, and the ten categories—these have become for them like puzzles! But goodness, by the praise of Allah and His grace, and then by the virtue of our scholars—may Allah perpetuate their benefit, and may Allah not deprive us of their kindness—remains until Allah inherits the earth and whoever is upon it; for our nation is a blessed nation. And the book that is in your hands—I have already informed you that it is among what students of kalam read in the age of youth, and they open the method with it; so they become acquainted through it with what they must encompass, not only in belief (i'tiqad), but in all the sciences of principles (usul) and rational speculation (nazar 'aqli). And turn your consideration in it to see the truth of that. So it was among the most obligatory duties to take care of it.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a critique of specialization followed by an introduction to the book. The author begins by calling specialization a 'lie', noting that those who specialize today do not even master the basics. He then gives an example: professors of kalam are ignorant of fundamental topics like the realities of things, definitions of substance and accident, and higher concepts like al-ahadiyya and al-wahidiyya (aspects of divine oneness), and various classifications (the four, seven demands, ten categories). Despite this, the author asserts that goodness remains through Allah's grace and the scholars. Then he introduces the book: it is read by young students of kalam, they begin their method with it, and it teaches them what they must know—not only in creed but in all sciences of principles and rational speculation. He urges the reader to examine the book to see this truth, concluding that taking care of it is an obligation.

Key Terms
الاعتقاد
الأصول
النظر العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The book only covers basic beliefs (i'tiqad).
Correction: The author says it covers not only belief but also principles (usul) and rational speculation (nazar 'aqli).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The book covers belief, principles, and rational speculation. Which of these three areas have you neglected in your own Islamic education? What is one concrete action you will take today to begin addressing that gap?

Sanusi's Personal Joy and His Teachers' Engagement
Describes Sanusi's satisfaction and his teachers' extraction of 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi witnessed the acceptance of his works by students and his own teachers, leading to the production of 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'.

Core Idea
Sanusi saw his works embraced even by his teachers before his death.

Before passing away, Imam al-Sanusi had the satisfaction of seeing his writings gain traction. Students eagerly studied them, and some of his own teachers engaged deeply, even producing a derived work called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. This lesson explores that moment of recognition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the sequence: Sanusi's comfort at students, then his teachers' involvement.
The passage uses 'بل' to add a stronger point about teachers.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions two groups that engaged with Sanusi's works: students of knowledge and his own teachers. Observe that the teachers went further by extracting 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: what does it mean that Sanusi's teachers, who were his seniors, engaged with his works to create a new text? This shows immediate scholarly validation.
It is rare for teachers to derive works from their student's writings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبل رحيل إمامنا السنوسي عن هذه الدنيا الفانية . . قرت عينه حينما رأى مؤلفاته - وعلى رأسها « سنوسياته » ـ قد أقبل عليها طلبة العلم ، بل إنَّ بعض شيوخه نظر فيها واستخرج ما سُمِّي بكتاب حقائق السنوسي . أمَّا الناظر في كتب فهارس المخطوطات فسيرى الأثر الكبير الذي أحدثته « السنوسيات » ؛ إذ أعملت العلماء أقلامها في شرح متونها والتحشية على شروحها ، ولا سيما « الصغرى » و « شرحها » ؛ فقد كانت لهذه العقيدة تحديداً عناية فائقة ندر أن ترى مثلها لكتاب في رحاب المكتبة الإسلامية.
And before the departure of our Imam al-Sanusi from this fleeting world... his eyes were comforted when he saw his works – headed by his 'Sanusiyyat' – that students of knowledge had turned to them. Rather, some of his teachers examined them and extracted what was called the book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. As for the one who looks in the books of manuscript indices, he will see the great impact that the 'Sanusiyyat' produced; for scholars employed their pens in commenting on their texts and writing glosses on their commentaries, especially 'al-Sughra' and its commentary; for this creed specifically received such extraordinary attention that one rarely sees its like for a book in the expanse of the Islamic library.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage describes the enthusiastic reception of Sanusi's writings. First, it notes that even before his death, Sanusi was gratified to see students eagerly studying his works, especially his 'Sanusiyyat' series. Moreover, some of his own teachers examined these works and produced a derived text called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi' (The Realities of al-Sanusi), showing that even senior scholars engaged with them. The author then emphasizes that anyone consulting manuscript catalogs will see the immense influence of the Sanusiyyat: scholars wrote extensive commentaries and glosses on them, particularly on al-Sughra (the Smaller) and its commentary. He concludes that this creedal work received a level of attention rarely seen for any book in Islamic libraries.

Correction: The passage states that they 'examined' his works and 'extracted' a book from them, showing active scholarly engagement.

C) They ignored them.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi saw his works accepted by students and even his teachers, who derived a new work from them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Extraordinary Scholarly Attention to the Sanusiyyat
Emphasizes the extensive commentaries and glosses on Sanusi's works, especially 'al-Sughra'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the immense scholarly impact of Sanusi's 'Sanusiyyat', as evidenced by commentaries and glosses, especially on 'al-Sughra'.

Core Idea
Sanusi's creedal works received unprecedented scholarly attention.

The influence of Sanusi's writings extended far beyond his lifetime. Manuscript catalogs reveal a vast corpus of commentaries and glosses, particularly on his 'al-Sughra' creed. This lesson highlights the extraordinary reception that made his works stand out in Islamic library history.

Al-Sughra is a concise creedal text by Sanusi.

The Interlocking Method of the Sanusiyyat
Describing the unique interlocking composition of Sanusi's works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique interlocking method of Imam al-Sanusi's Sanusiyyat and why each work in the series remains indispensable for scholars.

Core Idea
Sanusi's works interlock—each adds unique value while sharing common foundations.

This lesson explores the remarkable composition of Imam al-Sanusi's trilogy of creeds. Rather than simply repeating himself, Sanusi crafted each work to interlock with the others, offering fresh insights, varied proofs, and unique points. We will see how scholars rely on all three, not just one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice how Sanusi's method is described as 'interlocking composition' (al-ta'lif al-mutarakib).
Pay attention to the contrast between repetition and newness.
2
Identify the key elements of the method
Look for what makes the composition 'interlocking': repetition of general features, but with new style, diverse evidence, and unique additions in each work.
There are three aspects mentioned: style, evidence, and unique content.
3
Understand the practical implication
The passage concludes by showing how scholars cite all three commentaries. Why? Because each contains material not found in the others.
This shows the necessity of studying the whole series.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think the repetition is wasteful. The passage corrects this: it is intentional and each work adds value.
This is a common misunderstanding the passage guards against.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبديع في منهج السنوسي في « سنوسياته » المباركة هو التأليف المتراكب الذي لا بد فيه من جديد مفيد ; فالمتون على تفاوت حجمها ترى فيها تكراراً في معالمها العامة ، ولكن مع جديد في أسلوب العرض ، وتنويع في الدليل والمثال ، وزيادة تنفرد بها كل « سنوسية » لا توجد في غيرها ، وكم ترى للعلماء نقولاً وتحريرات عن إمامنا السنوسي يختمونها بقولهم : كما في ( شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ، أو شرح العقيدة الوسطى » ، أو « شرح العقيدة الصغرى » ، ولا يمكنهم الاكتفاء بـ ( شرح العقيدة الكبرى » مثلاً عن غيرها.
And the marvelous aspect of al-Sanusi's methodology in his blessed 'Sanusiyyat' is the interlocking composition in which there must be something new and beneficial. For the texts, despite their varying sizes, you see repetition in their general features, but with newness in the style of presentation, diversity in evidence and example, and an addition unique to each 'Sanusiyya' that is not found in others. And how often you see scholars quoting and editing from our Imam al-Sanusi, concluding their words with: 'as in the commentary on the Great Creed,' or 'the commentary on the Middle Creed,' or 'the commentary on the Smaller Creed,' and they cannot content themselves with, for example, the 'commentary on the Great Creed' over others.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage explains the distinctive compositional method of Sanusi's Sanusiyyat series. The author calls it 'interlocking composition' (al-ta'lif al-mutarakib), where each work must contain something new and beneficial. Although the texts vary in length, they share common general outlines, but each one adds novelty in style, variety in proofs and examples, and unique points not found elsewhere. The author then illustrates this by noting that scholars frequently cite Sanusi's commentaries—on the Great Creed (al-Kubra), the Middle Creed (al-Wusta), and the Smaller Creed (al-Sughra)—and they cannot rely solely on one, because each contains distinct material. This shows the value of the entire series.

Key Terms

شروح السنوسي

العقيدة الوسطى
العقيدة الصغرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The repetition in the Sanusiyyat means they are redundant and one work suffices.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while general features repeat, each work offers unique content in style, evidence, and exclusive points, making each indispensable.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Sanusiyyat are designed as an interlocking trilogy where each work contributes unique elements, making all three essential for a complete understanding of Sanusi's theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage shows that scholars cannot rely on the largest commentary alone. Is there an area of your faith where you assume you have 'enough' knowledge? What would it cost you to ignore the other voices? Commit to reading one additional work on that topic this month.

Sanusi designed his works so that each adds 'new and beneficial' content. How often do you approach learning with the same intention—to find what is new and beneficial rather than merely repeating what you already know? What change in your study habits would reflect this mindset?

Sanusi's Diverse Audience and Accessible Style
Sanusi's audience-conscious methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi tailored his discourse to different audiences while maintaining clarity and elegance.

Core Idea
Sanusi wrote for everyone — commoner and specialist — with clarity, not complexity.

This lesson explores a distinctive feature of Sanusi's methodology: his conscious effort to address a wide range of readers. The passage highlights his intention to rescue those who blindly follow (muqallidin) by using language that prioritizes understanding over ornamentation. Despite this simplicity, his works are described as both sweet and elegant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the author's claim about Sanusi's diverse discourse.
Pay attention to the pairs of audiences mentioned.
2
Identify the audience groups
The passage lists pairs: commoner/specialist, young/old, nation/child. This shows Sanusi's intention to reach everyone.
Consider why all these groups are mentioned.
3
Reflect on the balance
Think about how Sanusi achieved clarity without sacrificing elegance. The final sentence indicates his works were both sweet and elegant.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ناظر يعلم تنوع الخطاب الذي روعي فيه تنوع المخاطب ; فقد كان للسنوسي نية حسنة في استنقاذ المقلدين من جميع الشرائح ; فتراه يخاطب العامي والمختص ، والصغير والكبير ، والأمة والصبي ، بلغة حريصة على التفهيم والبيان ، دون اشتغال بتنميق عبارة ، ومع هذا كله جاءت سنوسياته ( حلوة الكلمات ، رائقة العبارات .
And every observer knows the diversity of discourse in which the diversity of the addressee was taken into account. For al-Sanusi had a good intention in rescuing blind followers from all segments of society; so you see him addressing the commoner and the specialist, the young and the old, the nation and the child, in a language careful to make understood and clarify, without occupying himself with embellishing expressions. And with all this, his Sanusiyyat came forth with sweet words and elegant phrases.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses Sanusi's audience-conscious method. The author states that anyone examining his works notices the variety of discourse corresponding to the variety of readers. Sanusi's noble intention was to rescue blind followers (muqallidin) from all social strata. Consequently, he addresses the layperson and the expert, the young and the old, the entire community and even children, using language focused on explanation and clarity rather than rhetorical ornamentation. Despite this simplicity, the result was that his Sanusiyyat are described as having 'sweet words and elegant phrases' – i.e., they are both accessible and beautiful.

Correction: It means he aimed to elevate them to informed belief, not that he rejected all taqlid.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's method was to communicate clearly with all levels of society, rescuing them from blind following, without sacrificing beauty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Sufi Elements and Poetic Grace in the Sanusiyyat
Describes the inclusion of Sufi references, prayers, and poems in the Sanusiyyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi deliberately integrated Sufi allusions, prayers, and poetry into the Sanusiyyat to enhance spiritual engagement and pedagogical effectiveness.

Core Idea
The Sanusiyyat blends Sufi spirituality with creedal instruction to nourish both mind and heart.

This passage highlights a distinctive feature of the Sanusiyyat: the strategic incorporation of gnostic references, heartfelt supplications, and poetic maxims. Rather than being a dry theological manual, al-Sanusi's work intentionally combines rational argument with spiritual subtlety. As you read, consider how this method reflects the author's concern for the holistic formation of the believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the three elements mentioned: isharat ma'rifiyya (gnostic allusions), ad'iya raqiqa (delicate supplications), and ash'ar/hikam (poems and sayings).
The passage is from a commentary, not the original matn.
2
Identify the purposes given
Find the purposes listed: bringing comfort and reminding (al-inas wa al-tadhkir), sharpening determination and seeking success (shahdh al-himam wa istilham al-tawfiq), and moistening dryness and softening roughness.
Each element serves a distinct pedagogical role.
3
Consider the author’s praise
The commentator praises this feature, noting its good effect and the tranquility it brings. He also mentions that Imam al-Sanusi himself indicated the sufficiency and blessedness of this method.
4
Reflect on the method
Think about how this combination of kalam and tasawwuf connects mind and heart. Why would al-Sanusi deliberately include Sufi elements?
This relates to the broader debate on the relationship between theology and mysticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الملامح البديعة في هذه السلسلة الموفقة : المزج الهادف والمتعمد لبعض الإشارات المعرفية على طريقة السادة الصوفية ، ولبعض الأدعية الرقيقة التي يختم بها فصوله على سبيل الإيناس والتذكير ، وشحذ الهمم واستلهام التوفيق ، ولبعض الأشعار والحكم والأقوال التي ترطب جفاف النص وتطرّي من خشونته ، وكم لهذا الملمح من أثر طيب ، وطمأنينة تسري في النفوس ، فلله دره من عالم تحرير موفق ! وقد لوّح الإمام السنوسي بوفاء هذه الطريقة في عرض الاعتقاد بالمقصود ، وبركتها وسلامتها من كدر الخوض فيما لا يعني ; فقد
Then, among the marvelous features of this blessed series is the purposeful and deliberate blending of certain gnostic allusions in the manner of the Sufi masters, and of some delicate supplications with which he concludes his chapters by way of bringing comfort and reminding, sharpening determination and seeking success, and of some poems, wise sayings, and aphorisms that moisten the dryness of the text and soften its roughness. And how great is the good effect of this feature, and the tranquility that permeates souls. So excellent is he, a successful scholar of refined expression! And Imam al-Sanusi hinted at the sufficiency of this method in presenting the intended belief, and its blessing and freedom from the trouble of delving into what does not concern; for...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage highlights further features of the Sanusiyyat: the deliberate inclusion of Sufi gnostic references (isharat ma'rifiyya), delicate supplications at the end of chapters, and poems, maxims, and sayings. The purpose is to provide comfort, remind the reader, sharpen resolve, and seek divine success, while also moistening the dryness of the text and softening its roughness. The author praises this feature, noting its positive impact and the tranquility it brings. Finally, he mentions that Sanusi himself indicated that this method sufficiently conveys the intended creed and is blessed and free from the trouble of irrelevant discussions. The sentence is cut off at 'for...' (faqad), likely continuing on the next page.

Key Terms

الأدعية
الأشعار
الحكم
الإيناس
التذكير
شحذ الهمم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inclusion of Sufi elements means al-Sanusi improperly mixed tasawwuf with kalam, diluting rational theology.
Correction: The passage shows the blending was deliberate, purposeful, and even endorsed by al-Sanusi as sufficient and blessed. It was meant to enhance spiritual engagement, not compromise theological rigor.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of including delicate supplications in the Sanusiyyat?
A) To lengthen the text.
B) To bring comfort and remind, sharpen determination and seek success.
C) To replace theological arguments.
D) To criticize Sufi practices.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi deliberately enriched his creedal work with Sufi allusions, prayers, and poetry to nurture both intellect and spirit, a method he himself affirmed as sufficient and blessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study 'aqidah, do you treat it as a dry intellectual exercise, or do you actively seek to let it soothe your heart and sharpen your resolve? What one practice from this lesson will you adopt this week to bring spiritual moisture to your learning?
?
Al-Sanusi ended his chapters with delicate supplications. Do you end your own sessions of religious learning with prayer and reflection, or do you rush off to the next task? What supplication will you commit to saying after your next study session?
?
The commentator praises the tranquility that the blend of creed, poetry, and prayer produces. Is your heart tranquil when you contemplate the foundational beliefs of Islam? If not, what specific element from this lesson could you begin to incorporate to address that?

The Unique Features of the Sanusiyyat Series
Author enumerates features of the Sanusiyyat series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive features that make each Sanusiyya work unique despite their common foundation.

Core Idea
Sanusi's works blend repetition with innovation, tailored for every audience.

Imam al-Sanusi authored a series of creedal works known as the Sanusiyyat. Though they share core content, each has unique features in style, proofs, and supplementary elements. This lesson explores those features and Sanusi's deliberate method of addressing diverse audiences with clarity and spiritual depth.

Guided Path
1
Observe the pattern of repetition and newness
Notice that the passage first acknowledges repetition in general features but then highlights newness in presentation and variety in proofs.
This shows the series is not mere duplication.
2
Identify the diverse audience addressed
Look at how Sanusi specifically intended to rescue muqallids (followers) from all segments, including commoners and specialists.
His language is clear and explanatory, not ornate.
3
List the three embellishing features
The passage mentions blending Sufi allusions, including supplications at chapter ends, and incorporating poems and wisdoms. Note how these soften the text.
These features bring tranquility.
4
Reflect on the overall goal
Consider how these features serve to make the theological content accessible and spiritually uplifting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترى فيها تكراراً في معالمها العامة ، ولكن مع جديد في أسلوب العرض ، وتنويع في الدليل والمثال ، وزيادة تنفرد بها كل « سنوسية » لا توجد في غيرها ، وكم ترى للعلماء نقولاً وتحريرات عن إمامنا السنوسي يختمونها بقولهم : كما في ( شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ، أو شرح العقيدة الوسطى » ، أو « شرح العقيدة الصغرى » ، ولا يمكنهم الاكتفاء بـ ( شرح العقيدة الكبرى » مثلاً عن غيرها . وكل ناظر يعلم تنوع الخطاب الذي روعي فيه تنوع المخاطب ؛ فقد كان للسنوسي نية حسنة في استنقاذ المقلدين من جميع الشرائح ؛ فتراه يخاطب العامي والمختص ، والصغير والكبير ، والأمة والصبي ، بلغة حريصة على التفهيم والبيان ، دون اشتغال بتنميق عبارة ، ومع هذا كله جاءت سنوسياته ( حلوة الكلمات ، رائقة العبارات . ثم من الملامح البديعية في هذه السلسلة الموفقة : المزج الهادف والمتعمد لبعض الإشارات المعرفية على طريقة السادة الصوفية ، ولبعض الأدعية الرقيقة التي يختم بها فصوله على سبيل الإيناس والتذكير ، وشحذ الهمم واستلهام التوفيق ، ولبعض الأشعار والحكم والأقوال التي ترطب جفاف النص وتطرّي من خشونته ، وكم لهذا الملمح من أثر طيب ، وطمأنينة تسري في النفوس ، فلله دره من عالم تحرير موفق ! وقد لوّح الإمام السنوسي بوفاء هذه الطريقة في عرض الاعتقاد بالمقصود ، وبركتها وسلامتها من كدر الخوض فيما لا يعني ؛ فقد قال في طالعة كتابه ( المنهج السديد ( المعروف بـ ( شرح الجزائرية ) وهو يتحدث عن « منظومة الإمام أبي العباس الجزائري » : ( إذ هو منظوم مشتمل على طريقي هداية الخواص والعوام ؛ لأنه قد ضم فيه إلى حلاوة النظم المستميلة للطباع تقرير الأدلة البرهانية للعقائد على التمام ، ثم وشّحها بخطابات تصوفية تهزُّ النفوس النائمة لتعظيم جناب الحق ويدخل بها الضعيف مع القوي في سلك الانتظام ، وتلك سنة الله تعالى في تقرير الأدلة في كتابه العزيز ، ثم سنة مصطفاه الرسول وما أبركها من طريقة ، طريقة ، وأنصحها من دلالة ! لتضمنها الهداية العامة ، وإنالة البغية لكل موفق يروم إلى الحق الوصول )(۱)
You see in them repetition in their general features, but with newness in the style of presentation, and variety in proof and example, and an increase unique to each 'Sanusiyya' not found in others. How often you see scholars' quotations and corrections about our Imam al-Sanusi, concluding them with their saying: 'as in (Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra), or Sharh al-'Aqida al-Wusta, or Sharh al-'Aqida al-Sughra,' and they cannot suffice with (Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra) for example, beyond others. And every observer knows the diversity of discourse in which the diversity of the addressee was taken into account; for al-Sanusi had a good intention in rescuing the muqallids from all segments; so you see him addressing the commoner and the specialist, the young and the old, the slave woman and the boy, in a language keen on clarification and explanation, without preoccupation with embellishing expression. And with all this, his Sanusiyyat came (sweet words, luminous expressions). Then among the beautiful features of this well-guided series: the purposeful and deliberate blending of some gnostic allusions in the manner of the Sufi masters, and some delicate supplications with which he concludes his chapters by way of comfort and reminder, and sharpening of resolve and seeking of success, and some poems, wisdoms, and sayings that moisten the dryness of the text and soften its roughness. How great an effect this feature has, and tranquility spreading in souls. So wonderful is he as a successful scholar! And Imam al-Sanusi hinted at the fulfillment of this method in presenting belief with the intended goal, and its blessedness and safety from the turbidity of delving into what does not concern. For he said in the preface of his book al-Minhaj al-Sadid (known as Sharh al-Jaza'iriyya) while speaking about 'the poem of Imam Abi al-'Abbas al-Jaza'iri': (It is a poem comprising the two paths of guidance for the elite and the commoners; because he included in it, along with the sweetness of verse that attracts natures, the presentation of demonstrative proofs for beliefs in full. Then he adorned them with Sufi discourses that shake the sleeping souls to magnify the majesty of the Truth, and thereby the weak enters with the strong into the ranks of order. And that is the way of Allah Almighty in presenting the proofs in His Mighty Book, then the way of His chosen Messenger. How blessed is this way, a way, and how sincere a guide! For it includes general guidance, and obtains the objective for every successful one who seeks the truth.)
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage describes the distinctive features of Imam al-Sanusi's series of creedal works (the Sanusiyyat). The author begins by noting that although there is repetition in general outlines, each work offers new presentation style, variety in proofs and examples, and unique additions. He highlights that scholars often reference Sanusi's works, showing their importance. The author emphasizes Sanusi's intention to benefit all levels of people, from the commoner to the specialist, using clear language without ornate style. The Sanusiyyat are described as sweet and luminous. Then the author lists three additional features: (1) blending of Sufi gnostic allusions, (2) inclusion of tender supplications at chapter endings, and (3) inclusion of poems, wisdoms, and sayings that soften the text. This method brought tranquility. Then the author quotes Sanusi's own preface from al-Minhaj al-Sadid, where Sanusi praises the poem of al-Jaza'iri for combining both rational proofs and Sufi discourses, making it suitable for both elite and commoners, following the method of the Quran and Sunnah.

مقلدين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sanusi's works are purely theological without Sufi elements.
Correction: The passage shows he deliberately blended Sufi gnostic allusions to soften the text and appeal to the soul.

Sanusi's Sanusiyyat combine clear theological exposition with spiritual elements like Sufi allusions and supplications to reach all audiences.

?

Sanusi's Method: Rational Proofs and Sufi Discourses
Sanusi quotes his own preface explaining the method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's method of combining rational proofs with Sufi discourses, as illustrated in his preface to Sharh al-Jaza'iriyya.

Core Idea
Sanusi's method mirrors the Quran and Sunnah: rational proofs plus spiritual awakening.

In the preface to his Sharh al-Jaza'iriyya, Imam al-Sanusi praises the poem of al-Jaza'iri for combining two essential paths: rational demonstration and Sufi discourse. This lesson examines that quote and its significance for his overall methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the quote from Sanusi's preface
Focus on the passage where Sanusi describes the poem as containing both demonstrative proofs and Sufi discourses.
Note the phrase 'including general guidance'.
2
Identify the two paths mentioned
Sanusi mentions two paths: one for the elite (khawass) and one for commoners ('awamm). Discuss how these correspond to rational proofs and Sufi discourses.
This is a key feature of his method.
3
Notice the Quranic and Prophetic precedent
Sanusi grounds this method in the Quran and Sunnah, which also combine clear evidence with spiritual motivation.
This shows his approach is rooted in tradition.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Consider how this method aims to include both the strong and weak in the ranks of order, providing general guidance.

The Warning Against Over-Reliance on a Single Text
Caution against absolutizing the Sanusiyya works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct attitude towards authoritative texts like the Sanusiyya, avoiding both under- and over-reliance.

Core Idea
No single book is everything in a vast science.

The author relates a criticism from Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar in which he laments that students and authors have made al-Sughra the sole reference, ignoring other verified knowledge. This is a caution against absolutizing any one text, not an attack on the text itself. The lesson helps you navigate the balance between honoring authoritative works and maintaining intellectual openness.

Guided Path
1
Read al-‘Attar's quote
Carefully read Hasan al-‘Attar's words where he wishes that the speaker had lived to see how al-Sughra has become the exclusive authority. Notice his concern that even decisive proofs are rejected if they contradict what is in that book.
2
Understand the author's interpretation
The author clarifies that al-‘Attar is not attacking the Sanusiyya but warning against making them the sole measure of truth. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
The author uses 'فحاشا أن يفهم منه الطعن' to emphasize this.
3
Apply to contemporary challenges
The author extends the criticism to modern students who rely on quick summaries and defective works. Consider how this applies to your own study habits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بلغ شأن السنوسيات عموماً وشرح الصغرى خصوصاً شأواً جعل بعض محققي العلماء يتضجّر من ذلك، لا من باب الاعتراض على ما حوته من علم جم، بل أن يُخطَّأ ما سواها لمجرد مخالفتها، حتى قال العلامة الشيخ حسن العطار في «حاشيته على شرح المحلي لجمع الجوامع»: (ليت هذا القائل عاش حتى الآن؛ ليرى ما يقوله المدرسون في دروسهم، بل ما ينقله المؤلفون في عصرنا ممن يتعلَّق بعلم الكلام؛ فإنهم اتخذوا «الصغرى» وما كتب عليها من الحواشي والشروح عمدة وإماماً، ولم تطمح نفوسهم بما قرره محققو هذا الفن في كتبهم، حتى إنه لو أُتي لواحد منهم بنقل ساطع أو برهان قاطع.. لم يعدل عمَّا استقر في ذهنه مما يخالف الصواب، وقال: لا أعدل عمَّا رأيته في ذلك الكتاب). وهذه الكلمة من شيخ الأزهر العلامة العطار يقدر قدرها العالم والموفق، فحاشا أن يفهم منه الطعن في السنوسيات، بل هو تنبيه على ألا تجعل –لا سيما «الصغرى» و «شروحها»– كل شيء في هذا العلم المترامي الأطراف؛ فقد ترى تحقيقاً في مسألة أو أكثر لا تجده أو تجد خلافه فيها. ولو أنَّ العلامة العطار عاش حتى الآن، ورأى حال طلبة علم العقائد والكلام، وما هم عليه من اهتمام بالوجبات العلمية السريعة، والاختصارات المحدثة المخلة وغير المحققة، والتأليفات الهشة التي داهنَ فيها مؤلفوها أهل البدع وجانبوا المداراة، وأخرى تعصّب فيها أصحابها فضيقوا واسعاً، وجعلوا الجنةَ وَقْفاً على أهل السنة دون غيرهم، ورأى إلى ذلك أحوال مدرّسي هذا العلم العظيم، والذين عامتهم تلقلقُ ألسنتهم بمصطلحات هذا العلم دون دراية، وبعضُهم قد أضمر الخصومة له وهو يدرسه محتسباً بزعمه ليصحح الأخطاء ويرد البدع التي انتشرت فيه! ولا يعلم أنه قد ضل بفعله وأضلَّ.. لرجا المولى تعالى أن تعود تلك العصبيَّة لهذه المدرسة العريقة، وأن يؤول الأمر إلى ما كان عليه، والشكوى إلى المولى العظيم. وإليك هذه الذكرى في صفات المتعلّم بل والمتصدر لتدريس علم التوحيد والعقائد، وهي صفات ذكرها حجة الإسلام إمامنا الغزالي في كتابه (قواعد العقائد) من كتب «إحياء علوم الدين» حيث قال: (العالم به ينبغي أن يخصِّصَ بتعليم هذا العلم مَنْ فيه ثلاث خصال: إحداها: التجرد للعلم والحرص عليه؛ فإنَّ المحترف يمنعه الشغل عن الاستتمام وإزالة الشكوك إذا عرضت. والثانية: الذكاء والفطنة والفصاحة؛ فإنَّ البليد لا ينتفع بفهمه، والفدم لا ينتفع بحجاجه، فيخاف عليه من ضرر الكلام، ولا يرجى فيه نفعه. والثالثة: أن يكون في طبعه الصلاح والديانة والتقوى، ولا تكون الشهوات غالبة عليه؛ فإنَّ الفاسق بأدنى شبهة ينخلع عن الدين؛ فإن ذلك يحلُّ عنه الحجر ويرفع السد بينه وبين الملاذ). وهذه الثالثة يدخل فيها الفاسق بالاعتقاد كما لا يخفى.
And the status of the Sanusiyyat in general and the Sharh al-Sughra in particular reached a level that caused some meticulous scholars to be annoyed, not by way of objecting to what they contain of abundant knowledge, but rather that anything else is deemed mistaken merely for contradicting them, until the erudite scholar Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar said in his gloss (Hashiya) on al-Mahalli's commentary on Jam‘ al-Jawami‘: 'I wish this speaker had lived until now, to see what the teachers say in their lessons, rather what the authors in our time who occupy themselves with kalam transmit; for they have taken al-Sughra and the glosses and commentaries written upon it as chief and imam, and their souls have not aspired to what the investigators of this art have established in their books, to the extent that if one of them were presented with a brilliant transmission or a decisive proof, he would not turn away from what had settled in his mind that contradicts the truth, and he would say: I will not turn away from what I have seen in that book.' This statement from the Shaykh of al-Azhar, the erudite al-‘Attar, is appreciated at its worth by the scholar and the successful one; far be it that one understand from it an attack on the Sanusiyyat; rather it is a warning that one should not make—especially al-Sughra and its commentaries—everything in this far-reaching science; for you might find a verification in a certain issue or more that you do not find in them, or you find its opposite therein. And if the erudite al-‘Attar had lived until now, and seen the condition of the students of the science of creed and kalam, and what they are upon of interest in quick scientific items, and the new defective and unverified abridgments, and the flimsy compositions in which their authors flattered the people of innovation and avoided courteous treatment, and others in which their partisans narrowed the broad, and made Paradise restricted to Ahl al-Sunna to the exclusion of others, and also seen the conditions of the teachers of this great science, most of whom move their tongues with the terminology of this science without understanding, and some of them have hidden hostility towards it while studying it, reckoning himself to be seeking reward in order to correct errors and refute innovations that have spread in it! And he does not know that he has gone astray and led others astray… he would have beseeched the Exalted Lord that that devotion to this noble school should return, and that the matter should revert to what it was, and complaint is to the Great Lord. And this reminder concerning the qualities of the learner, indeed of the one who sets himself up to teach the science of tawhid and creed: they are qualities mentioned by the Proof of Islam, our Imam al-Ghazali, in his book Qawa‘id al-‘Aqa‘id from the books of Ihya‘ ‘Ulum al-Din, where he said: 'The scholar of it should assign to teaching this science only one in whom there are three characteristics: first, devotion to knowledge and eagerness for it; for the preoccupied person is prevented by his occupation from completing and removing doubts when they arise. Second, intelligence, sagacity, and eloquence; for the dull person does not benefit from his understanding, and the stupid person does not benefit from his argument, so it is feared that harm from kalam will befall him, and no benefit from it is hoped for. Third, that there be in his nature righteousness, religiosity, and piety, and that desires not be dominant over him; for the immoral person, at the slightest doubt, is stripped of religion; for that removes the barrier from him and lifts the screen between him and pleasures.' And this third quality includes the person who is immoral in belief, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an editorial reflection on the reception and study of the Sanusiyya works, specifically the Umm al-Barahin (al-Sughra) and its commentaries. The author begins by noting that the Sanusiyyat have attained such high esteem that some scholars are troubled—not because they object to the valuable knowledge contained in them, but because many people now consider any work contrary to them to be automatically mistaken. He then quotes Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar, who laments that students and authors of his time treat al-Sughra and its commentaries as the ultimate reference, ignoring the findings of other great investigators, to the point that they stubbornly reject any evidence that contradicts what they have become accustomed to. Al-‘Attar's remark is reinterpreted not as criticism of the Sanusiyya themselves, but as a warning against making them the sole standard in a vast science. The author then imagines what al-‘Attar would say if he saw contemporary students preoccupied with superficial summaries and defective works, some authors flattering heretics and others becoming overly sectarian, and teachers who merely parrot terminology without understanding, some even harboring hostility to the subject while teaching it in a mistaken effort to 'correct' it. The passage concludes by quoting al-Ghazali's three essential qualities for a teacher of tawhid and kalam: (1) total devotion to knowledge and freedom from worldly preoccupations; (2) intelligence, sagacity, and eloquence; (3) righteousness, religiosity, and piety, with dominance over desires. Al-Ghazali warns that an immoral person may easily abandon religion at the slightest doubt. The editor adds that this third quality includes moral corruption in belief (i.e., heresy).

Key Terms
السنوسيات
شرح الصغرى
العطار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-‘Attar's quote is an attack on the Sanusiyya works themselves.
Correction: Al-‘Attar is not attacking the Sanusiyya; he is warning against making them the sole criterion for truth. The author explicitly states 'فحاشا أن يفهم منه الطعن في السنوسيات' (far be it that one understand from it an attack on the Sanusiyyat).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main point of Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar's criticism?
A) The Sanusiyya works contain errors and should be abandoned.
B) Students and authors have made al-Sughra the sole authority, ignoring other verified knowledge.
C) The Sanusiyya are too difficult for students.
D) Al-Sughra's commentaries are more important than the original.

Key Takeaway
While the Sanusiyya are highly valuable, no single text should be the absolute measure of truth in a vast science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung so tightly to a particular teacher, book, or school that you dismissed evidence that contradicted it? What would it take for you to change your mind if decisive proof were presented to you?
?
When you study religious sciences, do you rush through quick summaries and shortcuts, or do you genuinely seek out the depth of the great scholars? What would al-‘Attar say about your current approach?
?
Examine your intention: when you study kalam or other Islamic sciences, is there any hidden hostility or pride in your heart—perhaps thinking you are defending the truth while actually dismissing valid perspectives? How can you ensure your learning purifies your soul rather than inflating your ego?

Al-Ghazali's Three Conditions for Teaching Kalam
Al-Ghazali's enumeration of three conditions for teaching kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three qualities that al-Ghazali requires for a teacher of tawhid and kalam.

Core Idea
Teaching kalam requires devotion, intelligence, and piety.

After a long reflection on the state of kalam education, the author presents al-Ghazali's timeless criteria for who should teach this science. These three qualities—dedication to knowledge, intellectual competence, and moral-religious integrity—ensure that the teacher is both capable and sincere. This lesson details each condition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ghazali's three conditions
Read the passage carefully and identify the three conditions: devotion to knowledge, intelligence and eloquence, and righteousness and piety.
2
Understand each condition in depth
For each condition, note the reason al-Ghazali gives. Why is devotion necessary? Why intelligence? Why piety? The passage explains the dangers of lacking each.
3
Reflect on the inclusion of belief immorality
The author adds that the third condition includes those who are immoral in belief (heretics). Consider why this is important for teaching kalam.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of al-Ghazali's three conditions for teaching kalam?
A) Devotion to knowledge
B) Intelligence and eloquence
C) Wealth and social status
D) Righteousness and piety

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali teaches that only a devoted, intelligent, and righteous person should teach kalam, ensuring both competence and integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three qualities do you lack most when you study 'aqidah? What is one specific step you can take this week to strengthen it?
?
Al-Ghazali says the immoral person is stripped of religion at the slightest doubt. Look at your own heart: when a doubt about your faith arises, does your piety hold you steady, or do you feel the barrier weaken? What does that tell you about your need for taqwa?
?
If you were to teach someone else about tawhid today, which of these conditions would you fall short in? What would you do to prepare yourself?

Background and Significance of the Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya
Editor introduces the Muqaddimat and its commentary, explaining its significance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the background and significance of the Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya as both a key to kalam terminology and a full creedal work by Imam al-Sanusi.

Core Idea
The Muqaddimat is both a glossary for kalam and a full creedal work.

Sanusi's Muqaddimat holds a unique place in Islamic theology. It serves as a crucial introduction to the technical terms of kalam, yet it is also itself a complete creedal text. This lesson explores how the editor introduces this work and its commentary, highlighting its importance for students of theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main points
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Look for three key ideas: the Muqaddimat as a key to kalam terminology, its benefit for students, and its status as one of al-Sanusi's creedal works.
Notice the quotes from al-Malali, al-Shafshawni, and al-Bannani.
2
Understand the purpose of the commentary
The editor explains that the commentary helps students by simplifying dense concepts and making unfamiliar terms accessible. Consider how this aids in building a strong foundation.
3
Recognize the Muqaddimat as a full creed
The passage emphasizes that the Muqaddimat is not just a glossary but a complete creedal work. Note the listing of al-Sanusi's five creeds.
This corrects a common misconception.

قال الشيخ العلامة الملالي المؤرخ الأكبر لسيرة الإمام السنوسي يتحدث عن تأليفه: (ومنها «المقدمة» التي وضعها مبينة لعقيدته الصغرى، وهي قريبة منها في الجرم). وهذه «المقدمة»، أو «المقدمات» على ما هو الأشهر الغالب في المخطوطات.. هي متن هذا الشرح الذي بين أيدينا والتي هي على ما ذكر الشيخ الملالي مفتاح تعريف لأهم الاصطلاحات الدائرة في كتب الكلام عموماً، وكتاب «العقيدة الصغرى» خصوصاً. غير أنَّ هذا الشرحَ لا تقف حدود فائدته عند بيان تلك الاصطلاحات فقط، بل تُعد قراءته خير معين لطلاب الأصول وعلم الكلام، خصوصاً في مرحلة التمهيد، تلك المرحلة التي يتشكّى فيها الطالبُ ويتململ من جديد مفردات يغلب عليها الغموض في الدلالة فأسهم هذا التأليف في تلطيف الكثيف وتأنيس الغريب، ليدخل طالب العقائد بكل ثقة إلى ميدانِهِ، ويبني صرحه على أساس معرفي متين. ولكن يجب أن تعلم: أنَّ متن «المقدمات» ـ أو «المقدمة» - يُعد برأسه واحداً من العقائد التي ألفها إمامنا السنوسي، وشرحها واحد من شروحاتها، ولهذا المعنى قال العلامة المؤرخ الشفشاوني في الإمام السنوسي ذاكراً تأليفه العقدية التي وضع أصلها وفرعها: (وعقائده الخمس وشروحاتها من أفضل ما أُلْفَ في الإسلام؛ وهي: «المقدمة»، و«الصغرى»، و«صغرى الصغرى»، و«الوسطى»، و«الكبرى»). وممَّا يؤكِّدُ أنَّها داخلة في سلسلة العقائد السنوسية ما قاله العلامة أبو إسحاق السرقسطي البناني في شرح المقدمات السنوسية: (لمَّا قصرت الهمم ونفرت في هذا الزمان مما فيه تطويل.. سألني بعض الإخوان أن أختصر له «شرح العقيدة» المسماة بـ «المقدمات»، لسيدنا ومولانا شيخ الإسلام، ومصباح الأنام؛ أبي عبد الله محمد بن يوسف السنوسي الحسني، نفعنا الله به آمين؛ لما رآني أهلاً لذلك، وإن كنت لست هنالك بذلك، وجمعت)
The scholar, the historian, the greatest biographer of the life of Imam al-Sanusi, Sheikh al-Malali said, speaking about his authorship: (And among them is 'al-Muqaddima' which he composed explaining his minor creed, and it is close to it in size). And this 'al-Muqaddima', or 'al-Muqaddimat' as is more common and prevalent in manuscripts, is the text of this commentary which is before us, and which, as Sheikh al-Malali mentioned, is a key to defining the most important terminologies circulating in books of kalam in general, and the book 'al-Aqida al-Sughra' in particular. However, the benefit of this commentary is not limited to explaining only those terminologies, but rather its reading is considered an excellent aid for students of usul and kalam, especially in the introductory stage, that stage in which the student complains and becomes restless due to new vocabulary that is often ambiguous in meaning, and thus this work contributed to softening the dense and familiarizing the strange, so that the student of creeds may enter his field with full confidence and build his structure on a solid epistemological foundation. But you must know that the text of 'al-Muqaddimat' – or 'al-Muqaddima' – is by itself one of the creeds that our Imam al-Sanusi authored, and this commentary is one of its commentaries. For this reason, the historian scholar al-Shafshawni said about Imam al-Sanusi, mentioning his creedal writings for which he established both the original and the branch: (And his five creeds and their commentaries are among the best things written in Islam; they are: 'al-Muqaddima', 'al-Sughra', 'Sughra al-Sughra', 'al-Wusta', and 'al-Kubra'). And confirming that it is included among the Sanusi creedal series is what the scholar Abu Ishaq al-Saraqusti al-Bannani said in his commentary on al-Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya: (When aspirations fell short and they turned away in this time from that which is lengthy, some brothers asked me to abbreviate for them 'Sharh al-Aqida' titled 'al-Muqaddimat', of our master and lord, the Shaykh of Islam, and the lamp of mankind, Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Yusuf al-Sanusi al-Hasani, may Allah benefit us with him, Amen; because they considered me fit for that, although I am not up to that task, and I compiled...).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction to the Sharh al-Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya. The editor begins by quoting the historian al-Malali, who describes the Muqaddima as a work by al-Sanusi explaining his minor creed (al-Aqida al-Sughra) and close to it in size. The editor then clarifies that this Muqaddima (or Muqaddimat, as it is commonly called in manuscripts) is the base text for the present commentary. According to al-Malali, this text is a key to the most important terminologies used in books of kalam, especially al-Sanusi's al-Aqida al-Sughra. However, the commentary's usefulness goes beyond defining terms; it serves as an excellent aid for students of usul and kalam, particularly at the introductory stage. The editor describes how students often struggle with unfamiliar and ambiguous vocabulary, and this work helps by simplifying dense concepts and making strange terms familiar, allowing the student to enter the field of creedal study with confidence and build on a solid knowledge foundation. The editor then emphasizes a crucial point: the Muqaddimat is itself one of the creedal works authored by al-Sanusi, and this commentary is one of its explanations. To support this, the editor quotes the historian al-Shafshawni, who lists al-Sanusi's five creeds (al-Muqaddima, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra) and states that these works and their commentaries are among the best in Islam. Finally, the editor cites another scholar, Abu Ishaq al-Saraqusti al-Bannani, who in his own commentary on the Muqaddimat explains that he abbreviated his explanation at the request of some brothers, indicating that this text was indeed part of the Sanusi creedal corpus being taught and commented upon.

Correction: The passage clearly states that the Muqaddimat is itself one of al-Sanusi's creedal works, and it is included in his series of five creeds.

Key Takeaway
The Muqaddimat serves a dual purpose: it introduces key kalam terminology while standing as a complete creedal text authored by Imam al-Sanusi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you encounter difficult terminology in your study of faith, do you turn away in frustration or lean in with patience? What does the example of the Muqaddimat, which was written to 'soften the dense,' invite you to change about your own approach to learning?
?

?
The editor says that reading this commentary helps the student 'enter his field with full confidence.' Is your confidence in your creedal knowledge genuine, or do you rely on borrowed certainty? What would it take for you to truly own your faith?

Al-Muqaddimat as One of Sanusi's Creedal Works: Evidence from al-Shifshawni and al-Banani
Al-Shifshawni and al-Banani confirm al-Muqaddimat as a Sanusi creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Muqaddimat is one of the five creedal works of Imam al-Sanusi, as attested by historians and commentators.

Core Idea
Al-Muqaddimat is one of Sanusi's five creeds, not just an introduction.

This lesson presents evidence that the text known as al-Muqaddimat (or al-Muqaddima) is not merely an introduction but stands as one of Imam al-Sanusi's five major creedal works. We will examine citations from two scholars who include it in the list of Sanusi's creedal writings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
The passage opens with a remark about clarifying terms for students, then makes a key claim about al-Muqaddimat being a creedal work. Pay attention to the shift from general observation to specific claim.
2
Identify the two authorities cited
The author quotes al-Shifshawni and al-Banani. Note what each says about al-Muqaddimat.
Al-Shifshawni lists the five creeds; al-Banani mentions al-Muqaddimat as the creed he commented on.
3
Consider the significance
Why is it important to know that al-Muqaddimat is a creedal work? It places it among the primary sources of Sanusi's theology.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might think it is just an introduction; the passage shows it is explicitly counted among the five creeds.

The student becomes restless again due to new terms that are mostly ambiguous in meaning. So this work helped in refining the dense and familiarizing the strange, so that the student of creeds enters his field with full confidence, and builds his edifice on a solid knowledge foundation. But you must know that the text of "al-Muqaddimat" — or "al-Muqaddima" — is considered in itself one of the creeds that our Imam al-Sanusi authored, and its commentary is one of their commentaries. For this meaning, the historian scholar al-Shifshawni said about Imam al-Sanusi, mentioning his creedal writings whose original and branch he laid down: "And his five creeds and their commentaries are among the best authored in Islam; they are: al-Muqaddima, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra." (1) And among what confirms that they are included in the series of Sanusi creeds is what the scholar Abu Ishaq al-Saraqusti al-Banani said in his commentary "Sharh al-Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya": "When aspirations fell short and people turned away in this time from that which involves prolongation... some brothers asked me to abbreviate for him 'Sharh al-Aqida' called 'al-Muqaddimat', for our master and lord, the Shaykh of Islam, and the lamp of mankind, Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Yusuf al-Sanusi al-Hasani, may Allah benefit us with him, amen; since he saw me as fit for that, although I am not at that level, and I compiled what effects the solving of the expressions of 'al-Aqida', and perhaps I add to that a beneficial increase from other than him related to the context, so that benefit is obtained." (1)
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the author's assertion and supporting citations. The author begins by noting that the work (likely referring to the commentary on al-Muqaddimat) helps the student by clarifying unclear terms. Then he makes a key claim: the text of "al-Muqaddimat" (or "al-Muqaddima") is itself one of the creedal works authored by Imam al-Sanusi, and its commentary is one of the commentaries on those works. To support this, he quotes two authorities. First, the historian al-Shifshawni, who in discussing al-Sanusi's creedal writings, lists them as five: al-Muqaddima, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra. This explicitly places al-Muqaddima among the five. Second, he quotes Abu Ishaq al-Saraqusti al-Banani from his commentary "Sharh al-Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya", where he states that he was asked to abbreviate the commentary on al-Aqida called al-Muqaddimat. This shows that later scholars treated al-Muqaddimat as a creedal work. The quote also reveals that al-Banani compiled explanations of the expressions of al-Aqida, reinforcing its status as a creedal text. Thus, the author provides two textual proofs that al-Muqaddimat belongs to the series of Sanusi's creedal works.

Defending al-Muqaddimat as a Creedal Work
The author answers the objection that al-Muqaddimat does not resemble a creedal work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the classification of al-Muqaddimat as a creedal work by analogy with Ibn Khaldun's classification of literary works.

Core Idea
Classification of books depends on their foundational role, not superficial form.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection: that al-Muqaddimat and its commentary do not look like typical creedal works. He acknowledges the difference but offers a clever analogy drawn from Ibn Khaldun's classification of literary works to show that foundational works can differ in form from their genre's typical examples. This lesson explores the objection and the author's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector questions how al-Muqaddimat can be creedal. Notice the two reasons given: (1) many creedal topics are absent, (2) the form and arrangement differ.
The objector is not attacking the work, but questioning its classification.
2
Identify the answer
Read the author's citation of Ibn Khaldun's classification of four foundational literary books. Pay attention to what they are: Adab al-Katib, al-Kamil, al-Bayan wa al-Tabyin, and al-Nawadir.
Ibn Khaldun states that these four are the foundations; all other literary books are subsidiary.
3
Apply the analogy
Consider how Ibn Khaldun includes Adab al-Katib despite it resembling a dictionary more than typical literary works. Reflect on how this answers the objection: classification can be based on foundational importance, not surface similarity.
Think about other disciplines where foundational works differ from typical examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيف تكون » المقدمات » و « شرحها » من كتب العقيدة ؟ الناظر في ( المقدمات » و « شرحها » لا يكادُ يَقْنَعُ بكونها من جملة الكتب العقدية ؛ لغياب كثير من أبحاث الاعتقاد عنها ، ولتباينها عنها في صورة التأليف والترتيب ، فكيف تُضمُّ إلى سلسلة الإمام السنوسي في العقيدة ؟ وكيف يعدُّها الشفشاوني وغيره منها ؟ ولا أجد ما يقرِّبُ هذا العدَّ إلى ذهن المعترض على ذلك ككلمة العلامة المؤرّخ ابن خلدون ؛ إذ قال ضمن حديثه عن أمَّات الكتب الأدبية : ( وسمعنا من شيوخنا في مجالس التعليم : أن أصول هذا الفن وأركانه أربعة دواوين ؛ وهي : ( أدب الكاتب » لابن قتيبة ، وكتاب ( الكامل » للمبرد ، وكتاب ( البيان والتبيين » للجاحظ ، وكتاب ( النوادر » لأبي علي القالي البغدادي ، وما سوى هذه الأربعة فتبع لها وفروع عنها ) (۲) أفلا يسأل سائل ويقول : ما شأن « أدب الكاتب » ينظم في سلسلة أمَّات كتب الأدب وما أشبهه بمعجم من معاجم المعاني والصيغ الصرفية ؟ فأين هو من كتب الأدب ؟ (۱) انظر ( المواهب اللدنية في شرح المقدمات السنوسية » ( ص ٢ ) . (۲) انظر ( مقدمة ابن خلدون » ( ص ٦٤٧ )
How can "al-Muqaddimat" and its commentary be among the books of creed? The observer of "al-Muqaddimat" and its commentary is hardly convinced that they are among the creedal books, due to the absence of many creedal topics from them, and due to their difference from them in the form of authorship and arrangement. So how are they included in the series of Imam al-Sanusi in creed? And how does al-Shifshawni and others count them among them? And I find nothing that brings this counting closer to the mind of the objector than the statement of the historian scholar Ibn Khaldun, for he said within his speech about the primary literary books: "We heard from our teachers in educational sessions that the foundations of this art and its pillars are four books: they are: 'Adab al-Katib' by Ibn Qutayba, the book 'al-Kamil' by al-Mubarrad, the book 'al-Bayan wa al-Tabyin' by al-Jahiz, and the book 'al-Nawadir' by Abu Ali al-Qali al-Baghdadi. Whatever is besides these four is subsidiary to them and branches from them." (2) Does not a questioner ask and say: What business does 'Adab al-Katib' have being organized in the series of primary literary books while it resembles a dictionary of meanings and morphological forms? So where is it from literary books? (1) See (al-Mawahib al-Laduniyya fi Sharh al-Muqaddimat al-Sanusiyya, p. 2). (2) See (Muqaddimat Ibn Khaldun, p. 647).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is straightforward: how can "al-Muqaddimat" and its commentary be considered creedal books? The objector points out that they lack many of the usual discussions about creed and differ in their structure and arrangement from standard creedal works. The author then acknowledges this difficulty and proposes an answer by analogy. He cites the historian Ibn Khaldun, who listed four foundational literary books: "Adab al-Katib", "al-Kamil", "al-Bayan wa al-Tabyin", and "al-Nawadir". Ibn Khaldun's list includes "Adab al-Katib", which is essentially a dictionary of meanings and morphological forms, not a typical work of literary criticism or prose. Yet it is counted among the primary sources of the art of literature. The author implies that just as "Adab al-Katib" is included despite its apparent difference, so "al-Muqaddimat" can be included among creedal works despite its different form. The passage ends with a rhetorical question: "What business does Adab al-Katib have being in the series of primary literary books while it resembles a dictionary? So where is it from literary books?" This drives home the point that the classification of books is not always based on superficial resemblance but on their foundational role. Thus, the objection is answered.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author argues that classification is based on foundational importance, not surface similarity, using the example of Adab al-Katib among literary works.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to defend the classification of al-Muqaddimat as a creedal work?
A) The analogy of Ibn Khaldun's classification of literary books
B) The analogy of al-Shifshawni's list
C) The analogy of al-Banani's commentary
D) The analogy of a dictionary

Key Takeaway
The classification of a text depends on its foundational role in a discipline, not on its superficial resemblance to other works in that discipline.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a book, a lesson, or a person because it didn't look like what you expected from 'true' Islamic knowledge? What in your heart made you do that? Will you now ask Allah to give you the wisdom to see foundational value beyond appearances?
?
If someone challenged your understanding of a core belief because you couldn't cite the 'right' book or the 'right' style, would you have the courage and insight to draw an analogy from another field like the author did? What holds you back from thinking this way?
?
This passage reveals that even a dictionary can be a pillar of literature. What 'dictionary-like' acts of worship or knowledge in your daily life have you neglected because they seem too simple or not 'spiritual' enough? Will you repent and start taking them seriously as foundations of your faith?

Defending the Inclusion of Preliminary Works in the Sanusi Series
Editor defends inclusion of al-Muqaddimat in Sanusi Aqeedah series using Ibn Khaldun's literary analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why preparatory works like al-Muqaddimat are considered part of the Sanusi Aqeedah series, through the analogy of Ibn Khaldun's classification of foundational literary books.

Core Idea
Every science has foundational works that prepare the ground, even if they don't directly discuss its topics.

This passage is an editor's defense of including al-Muqaddimat and its commentary in the Sanusi Aqeedah series. The editor anticipates the objection that these works do not contain standard discussions of belief and differ in format. He responds by invoking Ibn Khaldun's classification of the four foundational literary works, showing that foundational books can be preparatory in nature. As you read, notice how the editor builds his argument using analogy and authority.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The editor raises a hypothetical objection: how can al-Muqaddimat be considered a book of Aqeedah when it lacks many typical discussions and looks different?
2
Examine Ibn Khaldun's analogy
Read the quote from Ibn Khaldun about the four foundational adab books. The editor uses this to draw a parallel.
3
Grasp the editor's explanation
The editor argues that every science has preparatory works (mumahhidat) and interconnections (shawabik). A science's canon is not limited to direct expositions.
4
Apply to Aqeedah series
Understand that the inclusion of al-Muqaddimat serves as a foundation for the Sanusi Aqeedah series, similar to how Ibn Qutaybah's Adab al-Katib is foundational for adab.
5
Reflect on the quote from Ibn Qutaybah
The passage ends with Ibn Qutaybah's warning that his book is for those seeking true mastery, not superficial students. This underscores the editor's point about the purpose of preparatory works.
