ما يحصل به حل ألفاظ « العقيدة » ، وربما أزيد على ذلك زيادة مفيدة من غيره تتعلق بالمقام ؛ لتحصل الفائدة )(١) كيف تكون « المقدمات » و « شرحها » من كتب العقيدة ؟ الناظر في « المقدمات » و « شرحها » لا يكادُ يَقْنَعُ بكونها من جملة الكتب العقدية ؛ لغياب كثير من أبحاث الاعتقاد عنها ، ولتباينها عنها في صورة التأليف والترتيب ، فكيف تُضمُّ إلى سلسلة الإمام السنوسي في العقيدة ؟ وكيف يعدُّها الشفشاوني وغيره منها ؟ ولا أجد ما يقرِّبُ هذا العدَّ إلى ذهن المعترض على ذلك ككلمة العلامة المؤرّخ ابن خلدون ؛ إذ قال ضمن حديثه عن أمَّات الكتب الأدبية : ( وسمعنا من شيوخنا في مجالس التعليم : أن أصول هذا الفن وأركانه أربعة دواوين ؛ وهي : « أدب الكاتب » لابن قتيبة ، وكتاب « الكامل » للمبرد ، وكتاب « البيان والتبيين » للجاحظ ، وكتاب « النوادر » لأبي علي القالي البغدادي ، وما سوى هذه الأربعة فتبع لها وفروع عنها )(٢) أفلا يسأل سائل ويقول : ما شأن « أدب الكاتب » ينظم في سلسلة أمَّات كتب الأدب وما أشبهه بمعجم من معاجم المعاني والصيغ الصرفية ؟ فأين هو من كتب الأدب ؟ وهذا السائل هنا وهناك جهل أن التأسيس في علم من العلوم لا يقتصر على سردِ أبحاثه موجزة تارة ومسهبة أخرى ، وأن تتراوح بين المتون والشروح ، بل لكلِّ علم ممهدات تشي بمقاصده وتعرفُ بما يدور فيه ، وشوابكُ تصله بغيره من العلوم ، ومن قصر طلبه على مألوف أهل زمانه ، ولم تهفُ همَّتُهُ للحاق بركب العلماء المهرة ، وأهل المكنة الخيرة .. فليقنع بأن يكون قعيد بيتِ التقليد ، وحِلْس دار العاجزين ؛ الذين أتبعوا نفسهم هواها ، وتمنوا على الله تعالى فطالبُ الأدب ليحترف حرفة الأدب ، لا ليبهج نفسَهُ بأخبار الأدباء وحكاياهم ، ويزركش كلامَهُ بقبس من نخب أقوالهم .. لا بدَّ له من معارف خاصة لا تُلفَى في كتب الأدب في الأغلب ، بل تقصد غيرها ، وهذا الذي نبّه له شيوخ العلامة ابن خلدون ؛ حين نظموا « أدب الكاتب » في سلك الكتب الأدبية الرفيعة ، مع أن الناظر فيه للوهلة الأولى قد يصفه بالكلمات السابقة ، ولكن أيكون أديباً من لا يقوى على صناعة أسطر دون لحن ، ولا يضع الكلم في مواضعه ؟! إنَّ الذي يعرف مفاتح خزائن الألفاظ ، ويعرف معانيها ومواطن التلفظ بها .. لينوء بثقل أحمال المعاني التي تتموّج فلا يُعرف لها حد ، فكيف بالجاهل بذلك كله ؟! ولهذا قال عريفُ الأدباء ابن قتيبة في مقدمة كتابه « أدب الكاتب » منبهاً : ( وليسَتْ كُتبنا هذه لمَنْ لم يتعلق من الإنسانية إلا بالجسم ومن الكتابة إلا بالاسم ، ولم يتقدَّم من الأداة إلا بالقلم والدواة )
What achieves the resolution of the terms of 'al-ʿAqīdah', and perhaps I add to that a useful increase from other [sources] related to the context; so that benefit is obtained. How are 'al-Muqaddimāt' and its commentary among the books of ʿAqīdah? The one who looks at 'al-Muqaddimāt' and its commentary will hardly be satisfied that they are among the doctrinal books, due to the absence of many discussions of belief from them, and due to their difference from them in the form of authorship and arrangement. So how are they included in the series of Imam al-Sanūsī on ʿAqīdah? And how does al-Shafshāwī and others count them among them? And I find nothing that brings this enumeration closer to the mind of the objector to that except the statement of the eminent historian Ibn Khaldūn; for he said in his discussion of the foundational literary books: 'We heard from our teachers in the educational circles that the principles of this art and its pillars are four collections: Adab al-Kātib by Ibn Qutaybah, Kitāb al-Kāmil by al-Mubarrad, Kitāb al-Bayān wa al-Tabyīn by al-Jāḥiẓ, and Kitāb al-Nawādir by Abū ʿAlī al-Qālī al-Baghdādī. What is besides these four is subordinate to them and branches off from them.' Does not a questioner ask and say: What business does 'Adab al-Kātib' have being organized in the series of foundational books of adab, when it resembles a lexicon of meanings and morphological forms? So where is it among the books of adab? This questioner here and there is ignorant that the establishment of a science is not limited to listing its discussions, sometimes brief and sometimes detailed, and that it ranges between texts (mutūn) and commentaries (shurūḥ). Rather, every science has preparatory matters that hint at its purposes and make known what revolves around it, and interconnections that connect it to other sciences. Whoever confines his pursuit to what is familiar to the people of his time, and his aspiration does not soar to catch up with the ranks of skilled scholars and people of good capability... let him be content to be a squatting occupant of the house of blind imitation, a clinging occupant of the house of the incapable; those who followed their own whims and wished upon Allah the Exalted. So the seeker of adab, to practice the craft of adab, not to delight his soul with the news of literary men and their tales, and to embellish his speech with borrowed sparks from the choicest of their sayings... he inevitably needs special knowledge that is not found in books of adab for the most part, but rather is sought elsewhere. This is what the teachers of the eminent Ibn Khaldūn alerted to, when they organized 'Adab al-Kātib' within the series of refined literary books, even though the one who looks at it at first glance may describe it with the previous words. But can a person be an adīb who cannot compose a line without grammatical error, nor place words in their proper positions? Indeed, the one who knows the keys to the treasuries of words, and knows their meanings and the contexts of their utterance... he certainly staggers under the weight of the loads of meanings that surge so that no limit is known for them. So how about the ignorant of all that?! For this reason, the dean of literary men, Ibn Qutaybah, said in the introduction to his book 'Adab al-Kātib', warning: 'These books of ours are not for the one who has taken from humanity nothing but the body, and from writing nothing but the name, and has advanced from tools nothing but the pen and the inkwell.'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an editorial defense of including the work 'al-Muqaddimāt' and its commentary within the series of Imam al-Sanūsī on ʿAqīdah. The editor begins by stating his goal to clarify terms and add beneficial material. He then raises a hypothetical objection: How can these works be considered books of ʿAqīdah when they lack many standard discussions of belief and differ in format and arrangement? The editor answers by invoking a parallel from the field of adab (literature). He quotes Ibn Khaldūn, who reports that the foundational books of adab are four specific works, and others are subordinate. The objector might similarly question why 'Adab al-Kātib' by Ibn Qutaybah is counted among those foundational literary books when it looks more like a lexicon. The editor explains that such objections stem from ignorance of how sciences are established. Establishment is not merely listing topics in summaries and commentaries; each science has preparatory works (mumahhidāt) that hint at its purposes and show its scope, as well as interconnections (shawābik) linking it to other sciences. He criticizes those who limit themselves to familiar contemporary works and fail to aspire to join the ranks of skilled scholars. Such persons are content with blind imitation and wishful thinking. The editor then elaborates on the seeker of adab: his goal should be to master the craft, not just to collect anecdotes and decorate speech. For that, he needs special knowledge not usually found in adab books, sought elsewhere. This is why Ibn Khaldūn's teachers included 'Adab al-Kātib' among refined literary works, despite its initial appearance. The editor affirms that true literary skill requires more than avoiding grammatical errors; it requires deep knowledge of word meanings and contexts. He concludes by quoting Ibn Qutaybah from the introduction of that work, stating that his book is not for those who have only taken the physical aspect of humanity and the name of writing, and only the pen and inkwell as tools. Thus, the editor defends the inclusion of preparatory works in a series by arguing that they fulfill essential foundational roles akin to those of 'Adab al-Kātib' in adab.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A book must directly discuss the core topics of a science to be considered part of that science's canon.
Correction: Preparatory works that build foundational knowledge are also essential parts of a science's canon, as exemplified by Ibn Khaldun's classification and the inclusion of al-Muqaddimat.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the editor use to defend the inclusion of 'al-Muqaddimāt' in the Sanusi Aqeedah series?
A) The analogy of Ibn Khaldūn's classification of four foundational adab books.
B) The analogy of the four schools of jurisprudence.
C) The analogy of the primary sources of fiqh.
D) The analogy of grammatical works in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
Preparatory works are integral to a science's canon, even if they do not directly cover its topics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you approach a new subject, do you have the discipline to study its preparatory works first, or do you jump straight into the 'main' content? What does your answer reveal about your patience and seriousness in seeking knowledge?
?
The editor warns that those who limit themselves to familiar works and refuse preparatory studies are content with blind imitation. Is there any area in your study of aqeedah where you have dismissed a text as 'not relevant'? What might you be missing by that dismissal?
?
Ibn Qutaybah said his book is not for those who only take the physical aspects of writing. In your own learning, have you ever been satisfied with just the outer form of knowledge (titles, names, summaries) without the deeper understanding that comes from foundational study? What will you do about it starting today?

The Muqaddimat as an Independent Creed
Explains the independent status of the Muqaddimat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Sanusi's Muqaddimat is an independent creedal primer that can stand alone, not just an introduction to the Aqida al-Sughra.

Core Idea
The Muqaddimat stands alone as a complete creedal foundation.

This passage clarifies the intended role of Sanusi's Muqaddimat. While it was written to explain the Aqida al-Sughra, it is not merely an appendix. As al-Mallali states, it is an independent creed that every student of knowledge can rely on. Let's see what he says.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the tone and the key claim.
The passage uses a rhetorical question to start.
2
Understand the connection
The passage first notes the obvious link between the Muqaddimat and its commentary, and with the Sughra. But then it introduces a crucial nuance.
The link is not the whole story.
3
Focus on al-Mallali's statement
Al-Mallali, a student of Sanusi, says the Muqaddima was composed to clarify the Sughra. However, he immediately adds that it remains an independent creed (عقيدة مفردة). This is the key point.
He uses 'but despite this' (لكن هذا مع) to contrast.
4
Consider the implication for study
The Muqaddimat can be used as a standalone primer for any Sunni Aqeedah text. It is not mandatory to read the Sughra afterward. It is a general introduction for every student.
This gives the Muqaddimat broader utility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماذا في « شرح المقدمات » ؟ لا تخفى العلقة بین » المقدمات » و شرحها » وكتاب « العقيدة الصغرى » و « شرحها » ؛ ويكفينا كلمة العلامة الملالي تلميذ الإمام السنوسي حين قال عند ذكر تأليفه : ( ومنها « المقدمة » التي وضعها مبينة لـ « عقيدته الصغرى » (١) ، ولكن هذا مع بقي هذا الكتاب عقيدةً مفردة ، يمكن اعتمادها لقراءة أيّ كتاب في الاعتقاد على طريقة أهل السنة ، وليس بالضرورة أن يُقراً بعده « العقيدة الصغرى » ، بل هو تمهيد عام يُعنى به كل طالب علم
What is in the 'Commentary on the Muqaddimat'? The connection between the Muqaddimat and its commentary, and the book 'Al-Aqida al-Sughra' and its commentary is not hidden. And sufficient for us is the statement of the scholar al-Mallali, the student of Imam al-Sanusi, when he said, mentioning his authorship: 'Among them is the Muqaddima which he composed, clarifying his 'Aqida al-Sughra' (1), but despite this, this book remains an independent creed, which can be relied upon for reading any book on belief according to the way of Ahl al-Sunna, and it is not necessary that 'Al-Aqida al-Sughra' be read after it; rather, it is a general introduction that every student of knowledge should attend to.'
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature and purpose of Sanusi's 'Al-Muqaddimat' (The Introductions). It begins by noting the evident connection between the Muqaddimat and its commentary, as well as with Sanusi's other work 'Al-Aqida al-Sughra' (The Small Creed) and its commentary. The author then cites a statement from al-Mallali, a student of Sanusi, who says that the Muqaddima was written to explain 'Al-Aqida al-Sughra'. However, al-Mallali immediately qualifies this: despite this purpose, the Muqaddimat stands as an independent creedal text. It can be used on its own as a foundation for studying any book on Aqeedah according to the Sunni method. It is not mandatory to then read 'Al-Aqida al-Sughra'; rather, the Muqaddimat is a general preliminary for all students of sacred knowledge. Thus, the passage asserts the autonomy and broader utility of the Muqaddimat beyond being a mere introduction.

Correction: Al-Mallali explicitly states that the Muqaddimat remains an independent creed (عقيدة مفردة) that can be relied upon for any book on Sunni belief, not just the Sughra.

The Order of the Eight Prefaces in the Muqaddimat
Al-Bannani enumerates the eight prefaces and explains their order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight prefaces of Sanusi's Muqaddimat and the rational order designed by al-Bannani to build knowledge and refute errors.

Core Idea
The eight prefaces are arranged with deliberate wisdom, not arbitrarily.

Al-Bannani does not merely list the eight prefaces; he reveals the underlying logic connecting them. This lesson explores each preface and the pedagogical and refutational reasons for their sequence, showing that the author's arrangement is intentionally crafted for effective learning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of al-Bannani's enumeration and explanation come through.
The passage begins with a list of eight prefaces, then poses a question about their order.
2
Identify the eight prefaces
Locate each preface by its Arabic name: judgments, sects, types of polytheism, foundations of disbelief, existent things, possible things, eternal attributes, and the trustworthiness of messengers.
These are the building blocks of the Muqaddimat.
3
Notice the question-and-answer format
Al-Bannani asks: 'What is the wisdom in giving precedence to the preface on judgments over others, and in linking the rest to it?' The answer provides the rationale for each sequential connection.
This Q&A highlights that the order is purposeful.
4
Follow the rationale for each link
Trace the reasoning: judgments first because by it others are known; sects because of shared number three; types of polytheism because of shared connection with Qadariyya; foundations of disbelief due to general-specific relation; existent things as refutation of Christian error.
Each link builds on the previous to create a coherent argumentative flow.
5
Reflect on the pedagogical and refutational goals
Consider how this order serves both teaching (starting with foundational concepts) and refutation (ending with a response to Christian tenets). This shows the author's wisdom.
The arrangement is not accidental but designed for maximum clarity and impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال العلامة البناني واصفاً هذه « المقدمات » ومفصلاً القول فيها : ( وعدد مقدماتها ثمانية : الأولى : مقدمة الأحكام ، والثانية : مقدمة المذاهب ، والثالثة : مقدمة أنواع الشرك ، والرابعة : مقدمة أصول الكفر ، والخامسة : مقدمة الموجودات ، والسادسة : مقدمة الممكنات ، والسابعة : مقدمة الصفات الأزلية ، والثامنة : مقدمة الأمانة في حق الرُّسُل عليهم الصلاة والسلام ) (۲) وقد بين العلامة البناني أيضاً وجه ترتيب هذه المقدمات على النحو المذكور ، ولم يرضَ أن يكون هذا محض اتفاق ؛ إذ أفعالُ الحكيم حكيمة محكمة ؛ فقال : ) فإن قلت : ما الحكمة في تقديم الأحكام على غيرها ، وفي عطف باقيها عليها على الترتيب المشاهد ؟ فالجواب : إنما قدَّمَ مقدمة الأحكام على غيرها ؛ لأنَّ بها يُعرف ما عداها وعطف مقدمة المذاهب على مقدمة الأحكام ؛ لاشتراكهما في ثلاثة كما أن الأحكام ثلاثة ، وقيل : المناسبة بينهما لأنه العدد ؛ وهي ختم الأحكام بالجائز ، والجائز فعل ، فعطف الفعل على الفعل وعطف مقدمة أنواع الشرك على مقدمة المذاهب ؛ لاشتراكهما مع مذهب القدرية وعطف مقدمة أصول الكفر على مقدمة أنواع الشرك ؛ لأنَّ بينهما عموماً وخصوصاً من وجه ؛ فيشتركان في جُلَّها ، وينفرد الشرك في السادس ، وينفرد الكفر في الإيجاب الذاتي وعطف مقدمة الموجودات على مقدمة أصول الكفر ؛ لما فيه من شبه البرهان بعد الدعوى ؛ وذلك أنه لما ختم الأصول بالجهل بالقواعد العقلية ، وهو متضمن لمذهب النصارى في جعلهم الإله صفة تعالى الله عن قولهم .. أتى بالموجودات ردّاً عليهم ، والله أعلم .
And the scholar al-Bannani said, describing these 'Muqaddimat' and detailing the discussion of them: 'The number of its prefaces is eight: the first: the preface on judgments, the second: the preface on sects, the third: the preface on types of polytheism, the fourth: the preface on the foundations of disbelief, the fifth: the preface on existent things, the sixth: the preface on possible things, the seventh: the preface on eternal attributes, and the eighth: the preface on the trustworthiness with regard to the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them.' (2) And al-Bannani also explained the wisdom behind the arrangement of these prefaces in the mentioned manner, and he was not content that this be mere coincidence, for the actions of the Wise are wise and precise. He said: 'If you say: What is the wisdom in giving precedence to the preface on judgments over others, and in linking the rest to it in the observed order? The answer: He only gave precedence to the preface on judgments over others because by it what comes after it is known. And he linked the preface on sects to the preface on judgments because of their sharing in three, just as the judgments are three. And it is said: the appropriateness between them is the number; and it is the ending of the judgments with the permissible, and the permissible is an act, so an act is linked to an act. And he linked the preface on types of polytheism to the preface on sects because of their sharing with the doctrine of the Qadariyya. And he linked the preface on the foundations of disbelief to the preface on types of polytheism because between them there is a general and specific relationship from one aspect; they share in most of it, and polytheism is unique in the sixth, and disbelief is unique in the self-obligation. And he linked the preface on existent things to the preface on the foundations of disbelief because there is in it a semblance of proof after the claim; that is because when he ended the foundations with ignorance of intellectual principles, and it includes the doctrine of the Christians in making God an attribute—exalted is God above their saying—he brought the existent things as a refutation of them. And God knows best.'
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents al-Bannani's enumeration and explanation of the eight prefaces of Sanusi's 'Al-Muqaddimat'. It begins by listing them: 1) judgments (al-ahkam), 2) sects (al-madhahib), 3) types of polytheism (anwa' al-shirk), 4) foundations of disbelief (usul al-kufr), 5) existent things (al-mawjudat), 6) possible things (al-mumkinat), 7) eternal attributes (al-sifat al-azaliyya), and 8) the trustworthiness of messengers (al-amana fi haqq al-rusul). Then al-Bannani argues that this order is not accidental; it is divinely wise because the author is a wise man (al-Hakim refers to Sanusi as a wise person, or ultimately God). He then poses a question: why this specific order? The answer provides a rationale for each sequential link. The preface on judgments comes first because it provides foundational knowledge for later prefaces. The preface on sects is linked because both involve the number three (judgments are three: obligatory, possible, impossible; sects? perhaps three sects? but the text says اشتراكهما في ثلاثة). Then there is a note that the judgments end with 'permissible' which is an act, so linking act to act with the sects (which involve actions). The preface on types of polytheism follows because both share a connection with the Qadariyya sect (which may involve shirk). The preface on foundations of disbelief comes next because it has a general-specific relationship with types of polytheism: they overlap in most aspects but shirk uniquely has a sixth point (undefined) and kufr uniquely involves self-obligation (al-ijab al-dhati). Then the preface on existent things is placed after the foundations of disbelief because the latter ends with ignorance of intellectual principles (al-jahl bi al-qawa'id al-'aqliyya), which includes the Christian error of making God an attribute (i.e., the Son as attribute). So the preface on existent things serves as a refutation by presenting the categories of existence. The passage ends with 'And God knows best', indicating the author's humility. Throughout, al-Bannani shows that the arrangement is purposeful, matching pedagogical needs and doctrinal refutations.

مقدمة الموجودات
مقدمة الممكنات
مقدمة الصفات الأزلية
مقدمة الأمانة في حق الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of the prefaces is arbitrary or coincidental.
Correction: Al-Bannani demonstrates that the order is deliberately designed for pedagogical and refutational purposes, reflecting the wisdom of the author.

Checkpoint
Why is the preface on judgments placed first according to al-Bannani?
A) Because judgments are the most important topic
B) Because by it what comes after it is known
C) Because it is the longest preface
D) Because it refutes the Christians

Key Takeaway
The arrangement of the eight prefaces is not arbitrary but follows a logical order that builds foundational knowledge and refutes specific errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you ever stop to consider the wisdom behind the order of topics? Or do you just follow the text without reflecting? This lesson shows that even the sequence is deliberate. What might you be missing by not pondering the structure?
?
The preface on existent things was placed to refute the Christian error of making God an attribute. Are you aware of the theological errors circulating in your own time? Are you equipped to respond to them with knowledge, or do you remain passive?
?
Al-Bannani did not accept that the order was mere coincidence. Do you sometimes assume that the arrangement of Islamic texts is arbitrary or outdated? How can you train yourself to trust the wisdom of the scholars and seek out the reasons behind their choices?

Conjunction of the Three Prefaces
Sanusi explains the reasons for conjoining three prefaces in his work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationships that justify the conjunction of three key prefaces in Sanusi's theological introduction.

Core Idea
The conjunctions between prefaces are not arbitrary but based on shared content and logical necessity.

Sanusi's prefaces are not merely a random collection; they are carefully ordered and conjoined based on conceptual relationships. In this passage, he explicitly explains why three particular prefaces are placed together. Understanding these reasons reveals the systematic structure of his theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three conjunctions
The passage presents three conjunctions. For each, note what is being conjoined and the reason given.
The conjunctions are labeled explicitly: عطف مقدمة الممكنات على مقدمة الموجودات, عطف مقدمة الصفة الأزلية على مقدمة الممكنات, and عطف مقدمة الأمانة على الصدق.
3
Reflect on the logical basis
Ask yourself why these particular conjunctions are highlighted. Consider how they reflect the deeper structure of kalam reasoning.
This passage shows Sanusi’s attention to pedagogical clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعطف مقدمة الممكنات على مقدمة الموجودات ؛ لما بينهما من الاشتراك ؛ فيشتركان في الأجرام وأعراضها ، وتنفرد الموجودات بذات مولانا ، وتنفرد الممكنات بالجائز المعدوم ، فتأمله وعطف مقدمة الصفة الأزلية على مقدمة الممكنات ؛ من باب إتيان الطالب في أثر المطلوب ؛ وذلك أن القدرة الأزلية طالبة لتعلُّقها بالممكنات ، وهي مطلوبة . وعطف مقدمة الأمانة - وهي الثامنة - على الصدق المندرج تحت مقدمة الصفات ؛ لما بينهما من الاشتراك والتلازم ، وهذا من منح العلم ، فاعرفه فإنه نفيس )(۱)
And the conjunction of the preface of possible things (مقدمة الممكنات) with the preface of existing things (مقدمة الموجودات) due to what they share; they share in bodies and their accidents, but existing things are unique with the essence of our Lord, and possible things are unique with the possible non-existent, so reflect. And the conjunction of the preface of the eternal attribute (مقدمة الصفة الأزلية) with the preface of possible things is from the category of the seeker following the sought; because the eternal power seeks to attach to possible things, and it is sought. And the conjunction of the preface of trust (مقدمة الأمانة) – which is the eighth – with truthfulness (الصدق) which is included under the preface of attributes; due to what they share and their mutual necessity, and this is from the gifts of knowledge, so know it for it is precious.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of three specific conjunctions (عطف) in Sanusi's famous prefaces (مقدمات). The author states the reason for each conjunction. First, the preface of possible things is conjoined with the preface of existing things because they share bodies and accidents, but differ in that existing things include the essence of God while possible things include the possible non-existent. Second, the preface of the eternal attribute is conjoined with the preface of possible things because the eternal power seeks to attach to possible things, making it a case of the seeker following the sought. Third, the preface of trust (the eighth preface) is conjoined with truthfulness (under the preface of attributes) because they share and are mutually necessary. The author emphasizes that this is a gift of knowledge and worth understanding.

Key Terms
مقدمة
ممكنات
موجودات
صفة أزلية
أمانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conjunctions between prefaces are arbitrary.
Correction: They are actually based on logical relationships such as shared content and mutual necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the reason given for conjoining the preface of the eternal attribute with the preface of possible things?
A) They share in bodies.
B) Because the eternal power seeks to attach to possible things.
C) Because they are both prefaces.
D) Because of mutual necessity.

Key Takeaway
The three conjunctions of the prefaces are based on conceptual overlap and logical necessity, reflecting the systematic structure of Sanusi's theological method.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you view your theological beliefs as a coherent system with logical connections, or as isolated facts? How would Sanusi's method of conjoining prefaces challenge you to seek deeper relationships between what you believe?
?
The author calls this knowledge a 'gift.' Have you ever experienced the beauty of understanding the structure of your faith? If not, ask yourself: are you willing to invest time in systematic study to see those connections, even if it means reorganizing your current understanding?
?
Sanusi connects truthfulness (sidq) with trust (amana) as mutually necessary. Examine your own life: where does lacking one of these weaken the other in your character? What specific step can you take today to strengthen both?

Learning Objective

Arabic sciences are the foundation of correct kalam; ignorance leads to mistakes.

This lesson focuses on a concise but powerful warning from Sanusi: errors in theological reasoning often stem not from weak logic but from weak Arabic. Guided Path

Core Idea
Ignorance of Arabic grammar and rhetoric can lead to disbelief.

Sanusi's warning is not about mere eloquence—it is about theological safety. Errors in understanding the Qur'an and Sunnah often stem from linguistic misunderstanding. This lesson highlights the critical role of Arabic sciences in preserving correct creed.

Sanusi's Principle: Why Literalism Without Detail Can Lead to Disbelief
Commentary notes Sanusi's statement that adhering to literal texts without qualification can be kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's principle that taking the outward meanings of scripture without qualified interpretation is a root of disbelief, and why this nuance is crucial in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Blind literalism without detail is a dangerous foundation.

Sanusi, a master of Islamic theology, formulated a striking principle: adhering to the literal texts of the Quran and Sunna without proper qualification can itself be a source of disbelief. This may sound shocking, but it highlights the importance of interpretative method. In this lesson, we examine his formulation, the reaction it provoked, and the essential distinction between holding the scripture and misusing it.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle as stated by Sanusi
Carefully read the Arabic sentence where Sanusi articulates his law (qanun). Note the key terms: ظواهر الكتاب والسنة, أصول الكفر, تفصيل.
2
Understand the rationale behind the principle
The commentator explains that this principle is affirmed by reason and transmitted texts. Reflect on why taking the outward meaning without detail could be problematic.
3
Identify the common misinterpretation
Some critics twisted Sanusi's statement to claim he said 'adhering to Quran and Sunna is kufr.' Recognize the distortion and the commentator's response.
4
Apply the principle to your own understanding
Consider how this principle guards against extreme literalism and encourages a balanced, detailed approach to scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- صياغته البديعة لقانون: (الأخذ بظواهر الكتاب والسنة من غير تفصيل.. من أصول الكفر)، هذا القانون الذي أقرته العقول والنقول قد غاظ كثيراً من العقول الصماء التي حجرَتِ الشريعة في قمقم الظاهر، وجاءت بأخذها بهذا بضلالات مخزية، وقد أظهر بعض العلماء المتأخرين هذا الذي قنَّنهُ الإمام، فقام عليه بعضُ من يدعي الغيرة على الكتاب والسنة؛ ليمخرق على العامة بقوله: صار التمسك بالكتاب والسنة من أصول الكفر! ولم يدر المسكين أن...
- His brilliant formulation of a principle: (Taking the outward meanings (zawahir) of the Book and the Sunna without detail is among the roots of disbelief). This principle, which reason and transmitted texts have affirmed, angered many hard hearts that confined the Shari'a in the flask of the outward, and they came, by taking it, with shameful deviations. Some later scholars manifested this which the Imam codified, but some who claim zeal for the Book and Sunna rose against him to deceive the common people by saying: 'Clinging to the Book and Sunna has become among the roots of disbelief!' And the poor fellow did not know that...
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary note about a principle formulated by Sanusi: 'Taking the outward meanings (zawahir) of the Quran and Sunna without specification or detail is among the roots of disbelief (usul al-kufr).' The commentator explains that this principle, supported by both reason and transmitted evidence, angered rigid-minded people who insisted on literal interpretations. Some later scholars made this principle known, causing criticism from those who claimed to defend the Quran and Sunna, accusing Sanusi of saying that adhering to the primary sources is kufr. The commentator says such critics misinterpreted Sanusi's intent and did not understand the nuance. The passage cuts off in mid-sentence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi said that adhering to the Quran and Sunna is a root of disbelief.
Correction: Sanusi said that taking outward meanings without detail is a root of disbelief, not the act of holding to scripture itself.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi's principle state?
A) Following the outward meaning is always obligatory
B) Taking outward meanings without detail is a root of disbelief
C) The Quran and Sunna should be abandoned
D) Details are unnecessary in understanding scripture

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's principle warns that unqualified literalism can lead to disbelief, stressing the need for detailed, methodical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Sanusi's Purpose: Explaining the Premises with Brevity
Sanusi states his intention for writing the Muqaddimat succinctly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's stated purpose for writing the Muqaddimat: to explain the foundational premises in a concise manner, seeking divine success.

Core Idea
Sanusi’s work aims to concisely explain the premises of Islamic theology.

Before diving into theological arguments, Sanusi clarifies his own purpose. In this brief statement, he tells us that he has divided his work into three parts and intends to explain the foundational premises with brevity. He concludes with a prayer for guidance, reminding us that the pursuit of truth depends on divine help.

وكل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة وهو [الشرق؟] قصدت بها شرح ما وضعته من المقدمات الجبيل الاختصار ومنه سبحانه أسأله التوفيق للحق والصواب في الأقوال والاعتصام
And each one of these three sections, which is [the east?], I intended by it to explain what I have set down of the premises, the great conciseness, and from Him, glory be to Him, I ask success to truth and correctness in statements and adherence.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a statement of purpose. The author declares that he has divided his work into three sections (الأقسام الثلاثة) and that his goal is to explain the premises (المقدمات) he has laid down in a concise manner (الاختصار). He concludes by praying to God for success (التوفيق) in attaining truth (الحق) and correctness (الصواب) in his statements (الأقوال) and adherence to the truth (الاعتصام). The word 'الشرق' is unclear; it may refer to the type of division or a specific section, but the overall meaning is the author's intent to clarify the foundational matters of the discipline with brevity.

The Definition of Hukm and Its Threefold Division
Sanusi defines al-ḥukm and divides it into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general definition of al-ḥukm and its three primary divisions in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Core Idea
All judgments fall into three categories: divine law, custom, and reason.

Before diving into the specifics of legal rulings, Sanusi establishes a foundational framework. He defines the term 'ḥukm' and then classifies it into three broad types. This lesson introduces that essential classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic and translation of the definition of al-ḥukm. Note that it is simply affirmation or negation of something.
This is a broad, philosophical definition.
2
Identify the three divisions
Identify the three divisions: sharʿī, ʿādī, and ʿaqlī. Understand that they represent different sources of judgment.
These divisions are fundamental in both kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Sanusi begins with this classification. It sets the stage for the rest of the lesson.
This is a methodological starting point.

فَالشَّرْعِيُّ: خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِأَفْعَالِ الْمُكَلَّفِينَ بِالطَّلَبِ أَوِ الإِبَاحَةِ، أَوِ الْوَضْعِ لَهُمَا

وَالتَّحْرِيمُ: وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا جَازِمًا؛ كَشُرْبِ الْخَمْرِ وَالزِّنَا وَنَحْوِهِمَا
وَالْكَرَاهَةُ: وَهِيَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا غَيْرَ جَازِمٍ؛ كَقِرَاءَةِ الْقُرْآنِ فِي الرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ مَثَلاً
وَأَمَّا الْإِبَاحَةُ: فَهِيَ إِذْنُ الشَّرْعِ فِي الْفِعْلِ وَالتَّرْكِ مَعًا مِنْ غَيْرِ تَرْجِيحٍ لِأَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ؛ كَالنِّكَاحِ وَالْبَيْعِ وَنَحْوِهِمَا

وَالْمَانِعُ: مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْعَدَمُ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ؛ كَالْحَيْضِ لِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَادِيُّ: فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ إِثْبَاتِ الرَّبْطِ بَيْنَ أَمْرٍ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُودًا أَوْ عَدَمًا بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُّرِ، مَعَ صِحَّةِ التَّخَلُّفِ وَعَدَمِ تَأْثِيرِ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ الْبَتَّةَ
وَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ:
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And may Allah bless our master Muhammad and his family and companions and grant them peace.
The First Introduction: On Judgments
Judgment (al-ḥukm) is: affirmation of a thing or negation of it.
And it divides into three divisions: legal (sharʿī), customary (ʿādī), and rational (ʿaqlī).
As for the legal (sharʿī): it is the address of Allah the Exalted pertaining to the actions of responsible persons (mukallafīn) by way of demand (ṭalab) or permission (ibāḥa), or the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both.
And included in demand are four: obligation (ījāb), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (taḥrīm), and disapproval (karāha).
Obligation: it is the demand to do an act with a decisive demand; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam.
Recommendation: it is the demand to do an act with a non-decisive demand; like the dawn prayer and the like.
Prohibition: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a decisive demand; like drinking wine and adultery and the like.
Disapproval: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a non-decisive demand; like reciting the Quran in bowing and prostration, for example.
As for permission (ibāḥa): it is the permission of the Law (sharʿ) to do or to leave, without preference of one over the other; like marriage and sale and the like.
As for the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both: it is an expression for the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign (amāra) for one of those five judgments.
And they are: the cause (sabab), the condition (sharṭ), and the impediment (māniʿ).
The cause: that from whose existence existence necessarily follows, and from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the setting of the sun for the obligation of the noon prayer, for example.
The condition: that from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the completion of the year for the obligation of zakāh.
The impediment: that from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose non-existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like menstruation for the obligation of prayer.
As for the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī): it is an expression for the affirmation of a connection between two things, in existence or non-existence, by way of repeated occurrence (takarrur), while allowing for exception and with no influence of one on the other at all.
And its divisions are four:
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of al-ḥukm (judgment) in the context of uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām methodology. Sanusi begins by defining ḥukm broadly as the affirmation or negation of something. He then divides it into three fundamental types: sharʿī (legal), ʿādī (customary), and ʿaqlī (rational). The rest of the passage elaborates on the first two.

First, he explains al-ḥukm al-sharʿī (legal judgment) as the address (khiṭāb) of Allah pertaining to the actions of mukallafīn (legally responsible persons). This address can be of three kinds: demand (ṭalab), permission (ibāḥa), or disposition (waḍʿ) for both demand and permission.

Under demand, he lists four subcategories: (1) ījāb (obligation) – a decisive demand to do an act, exemplified by faith and the five pillars; (2) nadb (recommendation) – a non-decisive demand to do an act, like the supererogatory dawn prayer; (3) taḥrīm (prohibition) – a decisive demand to refrain from an act, like drinking wine and adultery; (4) karāha (disapproval) – a non-decisive demand to refrain from an act, like reciting Quran during bowing and prostration.

Next, he defines ibāḥa (permission) as the Law’s permission to either do or leave an act, with no preference for one over the other, illustrated by marriage and sale.

Then he discusses waḍʿ (disposition), which is the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign or indicator (amāra) for one of those five legal rulings (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapproval, permission). He lists three types of such signs: sabab (cause), sharṭ (condition), and māniʿ (impediment). He defines each: sabab is something whose existence necessitates existence (of the ruling) and whose non-existence necessitates non-existence, e.g., the setting of the sun causing the obligation of ẓuhr prayer; sharṭ is something whose non-existence necessitates non-existence but whose existence does not necessitate either existence or non-existence, e.g., the completion of one year for the obligation of zakāh; māniʿ is something whose existence necessitates non-existence but whose non-existence does not necessitate either, e.g., menstruation preventing the obligation of prayer.

Finally, Sanusi turns to al-ḥukm al-ʿādī (customary judgment), which he defines as affirming a connection between two things, either in existence or non-existence, based on repeated occurrence (custom), while recognizing that the connection can be broken (exception) and that one thing has no real influence on the other. He then mentions that its divisions are four, but the text cuts off before listing them.

Key Terms
حكم
شرعي
عادي
عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the three categories are mutually exclusive or that all judgments are equally divine.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes them by their source: sharʿī comes from divine revelation, ʿādī from repeated observation, and ʿaqlī from pure reason.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the definition of al-ḥukm?
A) A divine command
B) Affirmation or negation of a thing
C) A rational necessity
D) A customary practice

Key Takeaway
The term 'ḥukm' broadly means affirmation or negation, and it is divided into three categories based on source: legal, customary, and rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you unconsciously prioritize customary judgments (what everyone does) over the rulings of the Shari'ah? Consider a specific choice you made today—was your judgment based on custom, reason, or divine law? Which one should have been primary according to this lesson?
?
The passage divides judgment into three types. Which type of judgment do you most frequently rely on? Is that type the most authoritative in matters of faith? What does that reveal about your priorities?
?
Reflect on a time when your rational judgment conflicted with a legal ruling in the Shari'ah. How did you resolve it? What does this lesson teach you about the scope and limits of each type of judgment?

The Legal Judgment and the Five Rulings
Sanusi defines legal judgment and enumerates the five rulings: four from demand and permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of legal judgment (sharʿī) and the five categories of rulings: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapproval, and permission.

Core Idea
Legal rulings are divine addresses that demand or permit actions in five ways.

Having defined judgment broadly, Sanusi now focuses on the first category: legal judgment (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī). He explains that it is the address of Allah related to human actions, and he enumerates five types of rulings. This lesson breaks down these rulings with examples.

Guided Path
1
Define legal judgment
Read the definition: it is the address of Allah pertaining to the actions of responsible persons via demand, permission, or disposition.
Note that 'disposition' (waḍʿ) is treated separately later.
2
Identify the four types of demand
List the four types: obligation (ījāb), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (taḥrīm), and disapproval (karāha). Understand the decisive/non-decisive distinction.
Obligation and prohibition are decisive; recommendation and disapproval are non-decisive.
3
Examine permission
Read the definition of ibāḥa: it is permission to do or leave without preference.
Permission is not a demand but a license.
4
Review examples
Look at the examples given for each ruling and relate them to familiar acts of worship or daily life.
Examples help cement the concepts.

The First Introduction: On Judgments
Judgment (al-ḥukm) is: affirmation of a thing or negation of it.
And it divides into three divisions: legal (sharʿī), customary (ʿādī), and rational (ʿaqlī).
As for the legal (sharʿī): it is the address of Allah the Exalted pertaining to the actions of responsible persons (mukallafīn) by way of demand (ṭalab) or permission (ibāḥa), or the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both.
And included in demand are four: obligation (ījāb), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (taḥrīm), and disapproval (karāha).
Obligation: it is the demand to do an act with a decisive demand; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam.
Recommendation: it is the demand to do an act with a non-decisive demand; like the dawn prayer and the like.
Prohibition: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a decisive demand; like drinking wine and adultery and the like.
Disapproval: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a non-decisive demand; like reciting the Quran in bowing and prostration, for example.
As for permission (ibāḥa): it is the permission of the Law (sharʿ) to do or to leave, without preference of one over the other; like marriage and sale and the like.
As for the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both: it is an expression for the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign (amāra) for one of those five judgments.
And they are: the cause (sabab), the condition (sharṭ), and the impediment (māniʿ).
The cause: that from whose existence existence necessarily follows, and from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the setting of the sun for the obligation of the noon prayer, for example.
The condition: that from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the completion of the year for the obligation of zakāh.
The impediment: that from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose non-existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like menstruation for the obligation of prayer.
As for the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī): it is an expression for the affirmation of a connection between two things, in existence or non-existence, by way of repeated occurrence (takarrur), while allowing for exception and with no influence of one on the other at all.
And its divisions are four:
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of al-ḥukm (judgment) in the context of uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām methodology. Sanusi begins by defining ḥukm broadly as the affirmation or negation of something. He then divides it into three fundamental types: sharʿī (legal), ʿādī (customary), and ʿaqlī (rational). The rest of the passage elaborates on the first two.

First, he explains al-ḥukm al-sharʿī (legal judgment) as the address (khiṭāb) of Allah pertaining to the actions of mukallafīn (legally responsible persons). This address can be of three kinds: demand (ṭalab), permission (ibāḥa), or disposition (waḍʿ) for both demand and permission.

Under demand, he lists four subcategories: (1) ījāb (obligation) – a decisive demand to do an act, exemplified by faith and the five pillars; (2) nadb (recommendation) – a non-decisive demand to do an act, like the supererogatory dawn prayer; (3) taḥrīm (prohibition) – a decisive demand to refrain from an act, like drinking wine and adultery; (4) karāha (disapproval) – a non-decisive demand to refrain from an act, like reciting Quran during bowing and prostration.

Next, he defines ibāḥa (permission) as the Law’s permission to either do or leave an act, with no preference for one over the other, illustrated by marriage and sale.

Then he discusses waḍʿ (disposition), which is the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign or indicator (amāra) for one of those five legal rulings (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapproval, permission). He lists three types of such signs: sabab (cause), sharṭ (condition), and māniʿ (impediment). He defines each: sabab is something whose existence necessitates existence (of the ruling) and whose non-existence necessitates non-existence, e.g., the setting of the sun causing the obligation of ẓuhr prayer; sharṭ is something whose non-existence necessitates non-existence but whose existence does not necessitate either existence or non-existence, e.g., the completion of one year for the obligation of zakāh; māniʿ is something whose existence necessitates non-existence but whose non-existence does not necessitate either, e.g., menstruation preventing the obligation of prayer.

Finally, Sanusi turns to al-ḥukm al-ʿādī (customary judgment), which he defines as affirming a connection between two things, either in existence or non-existence, based on repeated occurrence (custom), while recognizing that the connection can be broken (exception) and that one thing has no real influence on the other. He then mentions that its divisions are four, but the text cuts off before listing them.

Key Terms

طلب
إيجاب

تحريم
كراهة
إباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think that permission (ibāḥa) is a type of demand, but it is separate.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes demand (ṭalab) from permission (ibāḥa); permission is not a demand but a free choice.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a prohibitive judgment (taḥrīm)?
A) Faith in Allah
B) Dawn prayer
C) Drinking wine
D) Marriage

Key Takeaway
Legal rulings are divine addresses that either demand an action or refrain decisively or non-decisively, or permit it without preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made today. Can you identify which of the five rulings applied to it? If you are unsure, what does that reveal about your awareness of the divine address in your daily life? Commit to examining at least one action each day this week and categorizing it under these rulings.
?
The non-decisive rulings (recommendation and disapproval) are often neglected because they are not enforced. When did you last deliberately act on a recommendation (e.g., praying sunnah or giving extra charity) or avoid a disliked act solely out of consciousness of the Sharia? What one recommendation can you resolve to practice consistently starting today?
?
Permission (ibaha) covers many neutral actions like eating, sleeping, or buying. Is your heart neutral toward these actions, or have you attached excessive desires or preferences to them? How can you train yourself to treat permissible matters with the same indifference prescribed by the Lawgiver, using them only as means to fulfill obligations?

5 min
The Dispositional Judgment: Cause, Condition, and Impediment
Sanusi defines dispositional judgment and lists its three types: cause, condition, impediment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of dispositional judgment (waḍʿ) and the three types of indicators: cause, condition, and impediment.

Core Idea
Dispositional rulings are signs established by the Lawgiver to indicate other rulings.

After covering demand and permission, Sanusi turns to the third component of legal judgment: disposition (waḍʿ). This refers to signs set by the Lawgiver that correlate with the five rulings. He introduces three types: cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), and impediment (māniʿ). This lesson clarifies their logical relationships.

Guided Path
1
Define disposition
Read the definition: waḍʿ is the Lawgiver's establishing a sign (amāra) for one of the five rulings.
These signs are not rulings themselves but indicators.
2
Examine the three types
Read the definitions of sabab, sharṭ, and māniʿ. Notice the logical implications: cause necessitates existence, condition is necessary but not sufficient, impediment prevents existence.
Examples help differentiate them.
3
Compare and contrast
Compare sabab, sharṭ, and māniʿ using their logical formulas: cause: existence→existence, non-existence→non-existence; condition: non-existence→non-existence, existence doesn't guarantee; impediment: existence→non-existence.
Memorizing these formulas is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد وعلى آله وصحبه وسلم
المقدمة الأولى: في الأحكام
الْحُكْمُ: إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ
وَيَنْقَسِمُ إِلَىٰ ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ: شَرْعِيٌّ، وَعَادِيٌّ، وَعَقْلِيٌّ
فَالشَّرْعِيُّ: خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِأَفْعَالِ الْمُكَلَّفِينَ بِالطَّلَبِ أَوِ الإِبَاحَةِ، أَوِ الْوَضْعِ لَهُمَا
وَيَدْخُلُ فِي الطَّلَبِ أَرْبَعَةٌ: الإِيجَابُ، وَالنَّدْبُ، وَالتَّحْرِيمُ، وَالْكَرَاهَةُ
فَالإِيجَابُ: وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا جَازِمًا؛ كَالإِيمَانِ بِاللَّهِ وَرُسُلِهِ، وَكَقَوَاعِدِ الْإِسْلَامِ الْخَمْسِ
وَالنَّدْبُ: وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا غَيْرَ جَازِمٍ؛ كَصَلاةِ الْفَجْرِ وَنَحْوِهَا
وَالتَّحْرِيمُ: وَهُوَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا جَازِمًا؛ كَشُرْبِ الْخَمْرِ وَالزِّنَا وَنَحْوِهِمَا
وَالْكَرَاهَةُ: وَهِيَ طَلَبُ الْكَفِّ عَنِ الْفِعْلِ طَلَبًا غَيْرَ جَازِمٍ؛ كَقِرَاءَةِ الْقُرْآنِ فِي الرُّكُوعِ وَالسُّجُودِ مَثَلاً
وَأَمَّا الْإِبَاحَةُ: فَهِيَ إِذْنُ الشَّرْعِ فِي الْفِعْلِ وَالتَّرْكِ مَعًا مِنْ غَيْرِ تَرْجِيحٍ لِأَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ؛ كَالنِّكَاحِ وَالْبَيْعِ وَنَحْوِهِمَا
وَأَمَّا الْوَضْعُ لَهُمَا: فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ نَصْبِ الشَّارِعِ أَمَارَةً عَلَى حُكْمٍ مِنْ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ الْخَمْسَةِ
وَهِيَ: السَّبَبُ، وَالشَّرْطُ، وَالْمَانِعُ
فَالسَّبَبُ: مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْوُجُودُ، وَمِنْ عَدَمِهِ الْعَدَمُ لِذَاتِهِ؛ كَزَوَالِ الشَّمْسِ لِوُجُوبِ الظُّهْرِ مَثَلاً
وَالشَّرْطُ: مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ الْعَدَمُ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ؛ كَتَمَامِ الْحَوْلِ لِوُجُوبِ الزَّكَاةِ
وَالْمَانِعُ: مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْعَدَمُ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ؛ كَالْحَيْضِ لِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَادِيُّ: فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ إِثْبَاتِ الرَّبْطِ بَيْنَ أَمْرٍ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُودًا أَوْ عَدَمًا بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُّرِ، مَعَ صِحَّةِ التَّخَلُّفِ وَعَدَمِ تَأْثِيرِ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ الْبَتَّةَ
وَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ:
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And may Allah bless our master Muhammad and his family and companions and grant them peace.
The First Introduction: On Judgments
Judgment (al-ḥukm) is: affirmation of a thing or negation of it.
And it divides into three divisions: legal (sharʿī), customary (ʿādī), and rational (ʿaqlī).
As for the legal (sharʿī): it is the address of Allah the Exalted pertaining to the actions of responsible persons (mukallafīn) by way of demand (ṭalab) or permission (ibāḥa), or the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both.
And included in demand are four: obligation (ījāb), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (taḥrīm), and disapproval (karāha).
Obligation: it is the demand to do an act with a decisive demand; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam.
Recommendation: it is the demand to do an act with a non-decisive demand; like the dawn prayer and the like.
Prohibition: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a decisive demand; like drinking wine and adultery and the like.
Disapproval: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a non-decisive demand; like reciting the Quran in bowing and prostration, for example.
As for permission (ibāḥa): it is the permission of the Law (sharʿ) to do or to leave, without preference of one over the other; like marriage and sale and the like.
As for the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both: it is an expression for the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign (amāra) for one of those five judgments.
And they are: the cause (sabab), the condition (sharṭ), and the impediment (māniʿ).
The cause: that from whose existence existence necessarily follows, and from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the setting of the sun for the obligation of the noon prayer, for example.
The condition: that from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the completion of the year for the obligation of zakāh.
The impediment: that from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose non-existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like menstruation for the obligation of prayer.
As for the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī): it is an expression for the affirmation of a connection between two things, in existence or non-existence, by way of repeated occurrence (takarrur), while allowing for exception and with no influence of one on the other at all.
And its divisions are four:
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of al-ḥukm (judgment) in the context of uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām methodology. Sanusi begins by defining ḥukm broadly as the affirmation or negation of something. He then divides it into three fundamental types: sharʿī (legal), ʿādī (customary), and ʿaqlī (rational). The rest of the passage elaborates on the first two.

First, he explains al-ḥukm al-sharʿī (legal judgment) as the address (khiṭāb) of Allah pertaining to the actions of mukallafīn (legally responsible persons). This address can be of three kinds: demand (ṭalab), permission (ibāḥa), or disposition (waḍʿ) for both demand and permission.

Under demand, he lists four subcategories: (1) ījāb (obligation) – a decisive demand to do an act, exemplified by faith and the five pillars; (2) nadb (recommendation) – a non-decisive demand to do an act, like the supererogatory dawn prayer; (3) taḥrīm (prohibition) – a decisive demand to refrain from an act, like drinking wine and adultery; (4) karāha (disapproval) – a non-decisive demand to refrain from an act, like reciting Quran during bowing and prostration.

Next, he defines ibāḥa (permission) as the Law’s permission to either do or leave an act, with no preference for one over the other, illustrated by marriage and sale.

Then he discusses waḍʿ (disposition), which is the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign or indicator (amāra) for one of those five legal rulings (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapproval, permission). He lists three types of such signs: sabab (cause), sharṭ (condition), and māniʿ (impediment). He defines each: sabab is something whose existence necessitates existence (of the ruling) and whose non-existence necessitates non-existence, e.g., the setting of the sun causing the obligation of ẓuhr prayer; sharṭ is something whose non-existence necessitates non-existence but whose existence does not necessitate either existence or non-existence, e.g., the completion of one year for the obligation of zakāh; māniʿ is something whose existence necessitates non-existence but whose non-existence does not necessitate either, e.g., menstruation preventing the obligation of prayer.

Finally, Sanusi turns to al-ḥukm al-ʿādī (customary judgment), which he defines as affirming a connection between two things, either in existence or non-existence, based on repeated occurrence (custom), while recognizing that the connection can be broken (exception) and that one thing has no real influence on the other. He then mentions that its divisions are four, but the text cuts off before listing them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Students often confuse cause and condition; cause necessitates the ruling's existence, while condition only makes the ruling possible but not guaranteed.
Correction: Cause: existence ensures ruling; condition: existence does not ensure ruling.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a condition (sharṭ)?
A) Setting of the sun
B) Completion of the year for zakāh
C) Menstruation
D) Drinking wine

Key Takeaway
Dispositional judgments are signs (cause, condition, impediment) that logically correlate with the five rulings without being rulings themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the causes and conditions of your religious duties as mere formalities, or do you see them as signs from the Lawgiver that demand your mindfulness? What would change if you saw the setting of the sun as a direct reminder from Allah?
?
Reflect on a time when you neglected a condition (like purity) or encountered an impediment (like menstruation). Did you view it as a mere obstacle or as a divine sign with wisdom? How can you cultivate a deeper awareness of Allah's wisdom in these rulings?
?
Consider how the Lawgiver uses causes, conditions, and impediments to structure your life of worship. Are you grateful for these signs or do you find them burdensome? Ask Allah to transform your understanding.

The Customary Judgment
Sanusi defines customary judgment and states it has four divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of customary judgment based on repeated occurrence and its distinction from legal and rational judgments.

Core Idea
Customary judgments are empirical regularities that can sometimes be broken.

Sanusi moves from legal to customary judgment. Unlike legal rulings that come from divine command, customary judgments arise from observed patterns in the world. He defines it as a connection between two things based on repetition, yet allowing for exceptions. This lesson explains that concept and mentions its four divisions, though the text cuts off before listing them.

Guided Path
1
Define customary judgment
Read the definition: it is the affirmation of a connection between two things by repeated occurrence, with the possibility of exception and no real causal influence.
This is based on observation, not necessity.
2
Understand the key features
Note three features: (1) comes from repetition (takarrur), (2) allows for exceptions (ṣiḥḥat al-takhalluf), and (3) one thing has no actual influence on the other.
This aligns with the Ashʿarī occasionalist view.
3
Mention the four divisions
Sanusi says its divisions are four, but the passage ends. Reflect on what these might be based on the definition.
This leads to further study.

وَالشَّرْطُ: مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ الْعَدَمُ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ؛ كَتَمَامِ الْحَوْلِ لِوُجُوبِ الزَّكَاةِ
وَالْمَانِعُ: مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ الْعَدَمُ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ؛ كَالْحَيْضِ لِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَادِيُّ: فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ إِثْبَاتِ الرَّبْطِ بَيْنَ أَمْرٍ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُودًا أَوْ عَدَمًا بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُّرِ، مَعَ صِحَّةِ التَّخَلُّفِ وَعَدَمِ تَأْثِيرِ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ الْبَتَّةَ
وَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ:
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And may Allah bless our master Muhammad and his family and companions and grant them peace.
The First Introduction: On Judgments
Judgment (al-ḥukm) is: affirmation of a thing or negation of it.
And it divides into three divisions: legal (sharʿī), customary (ʿādī), and rational (ʿaqlī).
As for the legal (sharʿī): it is the address of Allah the Exalted pertaining to the actions of responsible persons (mukallafīn) by way of demand (ṭalab) or permission (ibāḥa), or the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both.
And included in demand are four: obligation (ījāb), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (taḥrīm), and disapproval (karāha).
Obligation: it is the demand to do an act with a decisive demand; like faith in Allah and His messengers, and like the five pillars of Islam.
Recommendation: it is the demand to do an act with a non-decisive demand; like the dawn prayer and the like.
Prohibition: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a decisive demand; like drinking wine and adultery and the like.
Disapproval: it is the demand to refrain from an act with a non-decisive demand; like reciting the Quran in bowing and prostration, for example.
As for permission (ibāḥa): it is the permission of the Law (sharʿ) to do or to leave, without preference of one over the other; like marriage and sale and the like.
As for the disposition (waḍʿ) for them both: it is an expression for the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign (amāra) for one of those five judgments.
And they are: the cause (sabab), the condition (sharṭ), and the impediment (māniʿ).
The cause: that from whose existence existence necessarily follows, and from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the setting of the sun for the obligation of the noon prayer, for example.
The condition: that from whose non-existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the completion of the year for the obligation of zakāh.
The impediment: that from whose existence non-existence necessarily follows, but from whose non-existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like menstruation for the obligation of prayer.
As for the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī): it is an expression for the affirmation of a connection between two things, in existence or non-existence, by way of repeated occurrence (takarrur), while allowing for exception and with no influence of one on the other at all.
And its divisions are four:
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of al-ḥukm (judgment) in the context of uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām methodology. Sanusi begins by defining ḥukm broadly as the affirmation or negation of something. He then divides it into three fundamental types: sharʿī (legal), ʿādī (customary), and ʿaqlī (rational). The rest of the passage elaborates on the first two.

First, he explains al-ḥukm al-sharʿī (legal judgment) as the address (khiṭāb) of Allah pertaining to the actions of mukallafīn (legally responsible persons). This address can be of three kinds: demand (ṭalab), permission (ibāḥa), or disposition (waḍʿ) for both demand and permission.

Under demand, he lists four subcategories: (1) ījāb (obligation) – a decisive demand to do an act, exemplified by faith and the five pillars; (2) nadb (recommendation) – a non-decisive demand to do an act, like the supererogatory dawn prayer; (3) taḥrīm (prohibition) – a decisive demand to refrain from an act, like drinking wine and adultery; (4) karāha (disapproval) – a non-decisive demand to refrain from an act, like reciting Quran during bowing and prostration.

Next, he defines ibāḥa (permission) as the Law’s permission to either do or leave an act, with no preference for one over the other, illustrated by marriage and sale.

Then he discusses waḍʿ (disposition), which is the Lawgiver’s establishing a sign or indicator (amāra) for one of those five legal rulings (obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapproval, permission). He lists three types of such signs: sabab (cause), sharṭ (condition), and māniʿ (impediment). He defines each: sabab is something whose existence necessitates existence (of the ruling) and whose non-existence necessitates non-existence, e.g., the setting of the sun causing the obligation of ẓuhr prayer; sharṭ is something whose non-existence necessitates non-existence but whose existence does not necessitate either existence or non-existence, e.g., the completion of one year for the obligation of zakāh; māniʿ is something whose existence necessitates non-existence but whose non-existence does not necessitate either, e.g., menstruation preventing the obligation of prayer.

Finally, Sanusi turns to al-ḥukm al-ʿādī (customary judgment), which he defines as affirming a connection between two things, either in existence or non-existence, based on repeated occurrence (custom), while recognizing that the connection can be broken (exception) and that one thing has no real influence on the other. He then mentions that its divisions are four, but the text cuts off before listing them.

Key Terms
عادي
تكرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think customary judgments are necessary laws of nature; Sanusi clarifies they are based on repetition and can be broken.
Correction: Customary judgments are not necessary; they are patterns set by Allah that can be interrupted by divine will.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of al-ḥukm al-ʿādī according to Sanusi?
A) Divine command
B) Rational necessity
C) Repeated occurrence (custom)
D) Logical deduction

Key Takeaway
Customary judgment is an empirical regularity that can be broken, with no real causal influence between the connected things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a regular pattern in your life—like the sun rising or a seed growing—do you instinctively believe that the first event causes the second? How does this lesson’s claim that there is 'no influence' challenge your default assumption? Will you now pause to remind yourself that Allah alone is the Creator of each event?
?
Have you ever felt disappointed when a reliable habit failed—like a good deed not bringing a desired outcome? This lesson says the connection can be broken. How does that truth reshape your trust in Allah rather than in the patterns themselves?
?
If everything in life is a customary judgment that can be broken, what pattern are you most afraid of being broken? Name it honestly. Are you willing to surrender that fear and rely on Allah, the One who breaks and sustains all customs at His will?

Seven Key Theological Errors and Prerequisites
Sanusi enumerates seven definitions of theological errors and prerequisites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of seven key theological errors and prerequisites in Sanusi's framework.

Core Idea
Theological errors are rooted in flawed reasoning, imitation, and ignorance.

Sanusi begins by defining several terms that are critical for identifying and avoiding errors in theology. These definitions cover everything from how we think about God's actions to how we interpret scripture. Understanding these concepts is essential for sound theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read each definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: each term is introduced with 'wa-' (and) followed by a colon and a definition.
The first item is a fragment from a previous definition.
2
Categorize the definitions
Identify which definitions relate to reasoning about God (items 1-2), which to following others (item 3), which to causality (item 4), which to knowledge (item 5), and which to scripture and method (items 6-7).
3
Reflect on the Ash'ari perspective
Consider why each definition is considered an error in Ash'ari theology. For example, 'rationalist teleology' is rejected because God's actions are not bound by created purposes.
The commentary will help clarify.
4
Distinguish similar concepts
Compare 'blameworthy imitation' (following out of zeal) with 'compound ignorance' (ignorant of one's ignorance). How do they differ?

- عَلَى سَبِيلِ التَّعْلِيلِ أَوِ الطَّبْعِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اخْتِيَارٍ
- وَالتَّحْسِينُ الْعَقْلِيُّ : وَهُوَ كَوْنُ أَفْعَالِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَأَحْكَامِهِ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَقْلاً عَلَى الْأَغْرَاضِ ; وَهِيَ : جَلْبُ الْمَصَالِحِ ، وَدَرْهُ الْمَفَاسِدِ

- وَالرَّبْطُ الْعَادِيُّ : وَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ الثَّلازُمِ بَيْنَ أَمْرٍ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُوداً أَوْ عَدَماً بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُرِ
- وَالْجَهْلُ الْمُرَكَّبُ : وَهُوَ أَنْ يَجْهَلَ الْحَقَّ ، وَيَجْهَلَ جَهْلَهُ بِهِ .
- وَالتَّمَسُّكُ فِي عَقَائِدِ الإِيمَانِ بِمُجَرَّدِ ظَوَاهِرِ الْكِتَابِ وَالسُّنَّةِ : مِنْ غَيْرِ تَفْصِيلِ بَيْنَ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ ظَاهِرُهُ مِنْهَا وَمَا لا يَسْتَحِيلُ .
- وَالْجَهْلُ بِالْقَوَاعِدِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ : الَّتِي هِيَ الْعِلْمُ بِوُجُوبِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ ، وَجَوَازِ الْجَائِزَاتِ ، وَاسْتِحَالَةِ الْمُسْتَحِيلاتِ ، وَبِاللِّسَانِ الْعَرَبِيِّ : الَّذِي هُوَ عِلْمُ اللُّغَةِ وَالإعْرَابِ وَالْبَيَانِ .
On the basis of causation or nature without choice. And rationalist teleology: it is that the actions of God Most High and His rulings are rationally dependent on purposes, which are: obtaining benefits and repelling harms. And blameworthy imitation: it is following others for the sake of zeal and partisanship without seeking truth. And habitual correlation: it is affirming necessary connection between two things, in existence or non-existence, through repetition. And compound ignorance: it is to be ignorant of truth and ignorant of one's ignorance of it. And clinging in the doctrines of faith to mere apparent meanings of the Book and the Sunnah without distinguishing between what is impossible in its apparent meaning and what is not. And ignorance of rational principles, which are knowledge of the necessity of necessary things, possibility of possible things, impossibility of impossible things, and of the Arabic language, which is the science of lexicology, syntax, and rhetoric.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a list of definitions of several erroneous concepts in theology and prerequisites for proper reasoning. The author defines seven items:

1. The first item (fragment) likely defines something like 'causation' or 'nature' as occurring without choice — possibly a description of determinism or natural compulsion. This is incomplete.

2. Rationalist teleology (التحسين العقلي) is the view that God's actions and rulings are rationally tied to purposes like obtaining benefits and repelling harms. This is rejected in Ashʿarī theology.

3. Blameworthy imitation (التقليد الرديء) is following others out of zeal and partisanship, not in pursuit of truth.

4. Habitual correlation (الربط العادي) is affirming a necessary connection between two things based on repeated observation, a critique of natural causality.

5. Compound ignorance (الجهل المركب) is being ignorant of truth and unaware of one's ignorance.

6. Clinging to literal meanings of scripture without distinguishing literal senses that are impossible (for God) from those that are not — a warning against anthropomorphism.

7. Ignorance of rational principles (the three rational judgments: necessity, possibility, impossibility) and of the Arabic language (lexicon, syntax, rhetoric) — these are essential tools for correct theology.

Key Terms
التحسين العقلي
التقليد الرديء
الربط العادي
الجهل المركب
التمسك بظواهر الكتاب والسنة
الجهل بالقواعد العقلية
اللسان العربي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rationalist teleology means ethical objectivism in general.
Correction: It specifically means God's actions must have purposes — which Ashʿarīs reject.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is 'compound ignorance' (الجهل المركب)?
A) Ignorance of the Arabic language.
B) Being ignorant of truth and aware of one's ignorance.
C) Being ignorant of truth and ignorant of one's ignorance.
D) Denying necessary truths.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's definitions provide a diagnostic tool for identifying common theological errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you guilty of compound ignorance in any area of your faith? In what specific belief are you certain you are right, yet you have never seriously questioned your own certainty? Will you today ask someone you trust to point out a blind spot in your understanding?
?
Which of these seven errors do you recognize in yourself most clearly? Be honest: is it blind imitation of a scholar or teacher, or relying on habitual explanations of God's actions without deeper reflection? What will you do this week to uproot it?
?
The lesson warns against clinging to literal meanings of scripture without distinguishing possibility. Is there a verse or hadith you have always taken literally that might actually require interpretation? What is your heart's resistance to re-examining that understanding?
