The Definition of Hukm and the Division of Knowledge
Sanusi defines al-hukm and distinguishes tasawwur from tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-hukm (judgment) and the distinction between tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) as foundational concepts in Islamic logic and theology.

Core Idea
Knowledge either merely conceptualizes a meaning or adds a judgment of affirmation or negation.

Before diving into theological arguments, Sanusi clarifies the basic building blocks of reasoning. He defines what a judgment (hukm) is and shows that all knowledge reduces to two types: mere conceptualization or conceptualization with a judgment. This passage sets the stage for everything that follows.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Definition of Hukm
Read the opening definition: 'Al-Hukm is the affirmation or negation of a matter.' Notice that hukm is an act of the mind that either affirms or negates something about something else.
Hukm is not just any statement but specifically a judgment with truth-value.
2
Distinguish Tasawwur from Tasdiq
Sanusi explains that when you perceive a matter, either you only grasp its meaning (tasawwur) or you also affirm/negate it of something (tasdiq). Work through the example of 'occurrence' to see the difference.
Tasawwur is a necessary precondition for tasdiq; you cannot judge without first conceptualizing.
3
Connect to Broader Purpose
The marginal note by Banani states that this introduction is placed first because it is key to understanding later topics. Reflect on why defining these basic terms is essential for theological reasoning.
This lesson is the foundation for all subsequent discussions in 'Aqeedah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحُكْمُ : إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ . يعني : أَنَّ مَنْ أدرك أمراً مِنَ الأمورِ : فإما أن يتصوَّرَ معناه فقط ولم يحكم بثبوته ولا نفيه، فهذا الإدراكُ يُسمَّى في الاصطلاحِ : تصوراً؛ كإدراكنا مثلاً أنَّ معنى الحدوثِ : الوجود بعدَ عدمِ، ولم نثبته لأمرٍ ولا نفيناهُ عنهُ. وإِمَّا أن يتصوَّرَ معَ ذلكَ ثبوت ذلك المعنى لأمر أو نفيه عنه، فهذا الإدراكُ يُسمَّى في الاصطلاح : تصديقاً، ويُسمى أيضاً : حُكْماً؛ كإثباتنا الحدوث مثلاً - بعد تصورِنا لمعناه ـ للعوالم؛ وهي ما سوى المولى تبارك وتعالى، فنقولُ : العوالم حادثةٌ، أو نفيناهُ عمَّنَ وجَبَ قدمه؛ وهو مولانا تبارك وتعالى، فنقولُ: مولانا جل وعزَّ ليسَ بحادث. فإثبات أمرٍ لأمرٍ أو نفيه عنه هو المُسمَّى حُكْماً، وبالله تعالى التوفيق. (۱) إنما قدَّم مقدمة الأحكام على غيرها؛ لأن بها يعرف ما عداها. «بناني» (ص).
Al-Hukm (judgment) is the affirmation or negation of a matter. Meaning: whoever perceives a matter among things: either he conceptualizes its meaning only and does not judge its affirmation or negation, and this perception is called in terminology: tasawwur (conception); like our perception, for example, that the meaning of occurrence (al-huduth) is existence after non-existence, and we neither affirmed it for a thing nor negated it from it. Or he conceptualizes along with that the affirmation of that meaning for a thing or its negation from it, and this perception is called in terminology: tasdiq (assent), and it is also called: hukm (judgment); like our affirmation of occurrence, for example – after our conceptualization of its meaning – for the worlds (al-‘awalim), which are everything other than the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, so we say: the worlds are contingent (haditha), or our negation of it from Him whose eternity is necessary, which is our Lord, Blessed and Exalted, so we say: our Lord, Mighty and Majestic, is not contingent. So the affirmation of a thing for a thing or its negation from it is what is called judgment. And with Allah Almighty is success. (1) He only presented the introduction of judgments before others because by it what comes after is known. – Banani (p.).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage provides a foundational definition of al-hukm (judgment) and explains the division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). The author begins by defining hukm as the affirmation (ithbat) or negation (nafy) of a matter. He then explains that when someone perceives something, either they only grasp its meaning without judging it (positive or negative) – this is called tasawwur in terminology; for example, understanding the meaning of 'occurrence' as existence after non-existence without applying it to anything. Or they combine that conceptualization with a judgment of affirmation or negation – this is called tasdiq (or hukm); for example, after conceptualizing occurrence, we affirm it of the 'worlds' (everything other than Allah), thus judging them as contingent, or we negate it of Allah, who is necessarily eternal. The key point is that the judgment itself is the act of affirming or negating one thing of another. The marginal note by Banani explains why this introduction on judgments is placed first: because it is essential for understanding everything that follows.

Key Terms
حكم
تصور
تصديق
إثبات
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that tasawwur involves some form of judgment or that it is incomplete without judgment.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that tasawwur is purely conceptual and does not include any affirmation or negation; it is a distinct cognitive act.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is 'tasdiq'?
A) Conceptualizing the meaning of a thing without judgment
B) Affirming or negating a matter after conceptualizing it
C) A type of rational judgment
D) The same as hukm in all contexts

Key Takeaway
The most basic division of knowledge is into tasawwur (conception without judgment) and tasdiq (assent with judgment), and every judgment is either affirmation or negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know the difference between tasawwur (mere conceptualization) and tasdiq (judgment with affirmation or negation). Reflect: In your own heart, do you only intellectually understand that Allah is not contingent, or have you truly affirmed it with such certainty that it shapes your awe and love for Him? What would change if you moved from tasawwur to tasdiq in every attribute you study?
?
Sanusi uses the example of 'the worlds are contingent' as an act of tasdiq. Have you personally made this judgment your own, or is it just a borrowed phrase? When you say 'Allah is not contingent,' do you feel the weight of negating any possibility of change or need in Him? If not, what is blocking your heart from reaching tasdiq?
?
This lesson teaches that every judgment is either affirmation or negation. Think of one belief you hold about Allah that is based only on cultural habit or hearsay, not on conscious affirmation. Will you today transform that into a conscious tasdiq by affirming or negating it with evidence? Write down the exact statement and commit to verifying it.

The Definition of Legal Ruling
Sanusi states the definition of legal ruling and explains the term 'address'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a legal ruling (ḥukm sharʿī) as Allah's address concerning the actions of the morally responsible, and the nature of that address.

Core Idea
A legal ruling is divine address about human actions.

Sanusi begins by laying out the foundational definition of the legal ruling. He carefully unpacks each component to clarify exactly what qualifies as a sharʿī ruling. This first part focuses on the core definition and the meaning of 'address' (khiṭāb), including a key dispute about whether Allah's eternal speech can be called address before addressees exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Start by reading the Arabic definition of the legal ruling and its English translation. Let the words of Sanusi sink in before analysis.
2
Focus on the explanation of 'address'
Sanusi explains 'address' (khiṭāb) as the genus. Notice how he defines its reality and introduces a scholarly disagreement about whether it requires an existing addressee.
This disagreement connects to the nature of Allah's eternal speech.
3
Understand the exclusion of other addresses
Sanusi clarifies that only Allah's address is considered a legal ruling. The address of messengers counts because they convey it infallibly. Other beings' speech is excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد الحكم الشرعي
فَالشَّرْعِيُّ : خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِأَفْعَالِ الْمُكَلَّفِينَ بِالطَّلَبِ أَوِ الإِبَاحَةِ ، أَوِ الْوَضْعِ لَهُمَا
قوله : ( خِطاب ) كالجنس في الحدَّ
وحقيقة الخطاب : الكلامُ الذي يُقصد بهِ مَنْ هو أهل للفهم . واختُلِفَ : هل مِنْ شرطِ التسمية به وجود المخاطَب أم لا ؟ وعلى ذلك جرى الخلافُ في كلام الله تعالى : هل يُسمَّى في الأَزْلِ خطاباً قبل وجودِ المخاطَبينَ أم لا ؟(۱) والأصح : جواز إطلاقه؛ لأن تعلقات الكلام قديمة عند الإمام الأشعري واختار القاضي الباقلاني أنه لا يُسمَّى كلام الله في الأزل خطاباً ، وانظر حاشية العطار على شرح جمع الجوامع ) ( ١/ ٦٧ ) ، و رفع الحاجب عن مختصر ابن الحاجب » ( ٤٩٠/١ )
والمراد بالخطابِ هنا : المخاطَبُ بهِ ؛ مِنْ إطلاق المصدرِ على اسم المفعول .
وإضافة الخطاب إلى الله تبارك وتعالى يُخرِجُ خطابَ غيرِهِ ؛ كالملوك والآباء والأمهات والمشايخ .
وبالجملة : يخرجُ بهذا القيدِ خطابُ مَنْ سوى اللهِ تعالى ؛ مِنَ الملائكة والإنس والجن ، فلا يُسمَّى خطاب هؤلاء كلّهم حُكْماً شرعيّاً ، وإنَّما يُسمَّى خطابُ الرُّسُلِ بالتكاليف حُكْماً شرعياً ؛ لأنَّهم مُبلِّغونَ عن الله تعالى ، معصومونَ في تبليغهم مِنَ الكذب عمداً وسهواً .
وقوله : ( المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين ( يُخرِجُ أربعة أشياء : الأول : خطابُهُ تعالى المتعلَّق بذاتِهِ العليَّةِ : نحو : ﴿ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [الصافات : ٣٥]
•
الثاني : الخطاب المتعلّق بفعلِهِ : نحو : ﴿ اللَّهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ [الرعد : ١٦]
الثالثُ : الخطاب المتعلّق بالجماداتِ : نحو : ﴿ وَيَوْمَ نُسَيِّرُ الْجِبَالَ [الكهف : ٤٧]
•
الرابع : الخطاب المتعلّق بذواتِ المكلَّفين : نحو : ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْتَكُمْ ثُمَّ صَوَّرْنَكُمْ﴾ [الأعراف : ١١] .
)
والمراد بفعل المكلَّف : ما يصدر منه ؛ ليشمل القول والنيَّةَ . والمكلَّفُ : هو البالغ العاقل ، ومن هنا يُعلَمُ : أَنَّ الصبي لا يتعلَّق به حكم ، هكذا قيلَ ، وانظر هذا معَ ما ذُكِرَ في الأصولِ مِنَ الخلافِ في الأمر بالأمر بالشيء (۱) : هل هو أمر بذلك الشيء أم لا ؟
(۱) كما في الحديث الذي رواه أبو داود ( ٤٩٥ ) عن سيدنا عبد الله بن عمرو رضي الله عنهما مرفوعاً : « مُرُوا أولادكم بالصلاة وهم أبناء سبع سنينَ )
The definition of the legal ruling. So the legal ruling is: the address of Allah the Exalted that is connected to the actions of the morally responsible (mukallafīn) by way of demand, permission, or a disposition (al-waḍʿ) for them. His saying: (address) is like the genus in the definition. The reality of address is: speech that is intended for one who is capable of understanding. And there is disagreement: is the existence of the addressee a condition for calling it address or not? Upon that depends the disagreement concerning the speech of Allah the Exalted: is it called in pre-eternity 'address' before the existence of the addressees or not? (1) And the correct view is the permissibility of applying it, because the attachments of speech are eternal according to Imam al-Ashʿarī, but al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī chose that it is not called the speech of Allah in pre-eternity 'address'; see the gloss of al-ʿAṭṭār on Sharḥ Jamʿ al-Jawāmiʿ (1/67) and Rafʿ al-Ḥājib ʿan Mukhtaṣar Ibn al-Ḥājib (1/490). And what is meant by 'address' here is the thing addressed (al-mukhāṭab bihi), by way of using the verbal noun for the passive participle. And the attribution of address to Allah the Blessed and Exalted excludes the address of other than Him, such as kings, fathers, mothers, and shaykhs. In summary, by this qualification is excluded the address of anyone other than Allah the Exalted, from the angels, humans, and jinn; so the address of all of these is not called a legal ruling. Rather, only the address of the messengers concerning obligations (takālīf) is called a legal ruling, because they are conveyers from Allah the Exalted, protected in their conveyance from lying intentionally or inadvertently. And His saying: (connected to the actions of the morally responsible) excludes four things: First: His address connected to His own lofty Essence, such as: {There is no god but Allah} [al-Ṣāffāt: 35]. Second: the address connected to His action, such as: {Allah is the Creator of everything} [al-Raʿd: 16]. Third: the address connected to inanimate objects, such as: {And the Day We will set the mountains in motion} [al-Kahf: 47]. Fourth: the address connected to the essences of the morally responsible, such as: {And indeed We created you, then We formed you} [al-Aʿrāf: 11]. And what is meant by the action of the morally responsible is what issues from him, to include speech and intention. And the morally responsible (mukallaf) is the one who has reached puberty and is rational. From this it is known that the child (al-ṣabī) is not subject to a ruling; thus it is said. But consider this along with what is mentioned in uṣūl al-fiqh concerning the disagreement about the command to command something: is it a command for that thing or not? (1) As in the ḥadīth narrated by Abū Dāwūd (495) from our master ʿAbd Allāh ibn ʿAmr, may Allah be pleased with them both, raised (marfūʿan): 'Command your children to pray when they are seven years old.'
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'legal ruling' (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī). The author begins by stating the definition: 'the address of Allah the Exalted that is connected to the actions of the morally responsible by way of demand, permission, or a disposition for them.'

He then comments on each part of the definition in order. First, he explains 'address' (khiṭāb) as the genus in the definition. He defines the reality of address as speech intended for one capable of understanding, and notes a scholastic disagreement about whether the existence of the addressee is necessary for something to be called 'address'. This leads to a discussion about whether Allah's eternal speech is called 'address' before addressees exist; the author notes that the correct view is that it is permissible to call it address, citing al-Ashʿarī and al-Bāqillānī's differing opinions.

Next, he clarifies that what is meant by 'address' here is the thing addressed (al-mukhāṭab bihi), using the verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle. Then he explains that adding 'of Allah' excludes the address of anyone else—kings, parents, shaykhs, angels, humans, jinn—from being called a legal ruling. Only the address of messengers concerning obligations is called a legal ruling because they are conveyers from Allah, infallible in their conveyance.

Then he examines the phrase 'connected to the actions of the morally responsible' and shows that it excludes four types of divine discourse: (1) speech about Allah's own essence (e.g., 'There is no god but Allah'), (2) speech about Allah's actions (e.g., 'Allah is the Creator of everything'), (3) speech about inanimate objects (e.g., 'And the Day We will set the mountains in motion'), and (4) speech about the essences of the morally responsible (e.g., 'And indeed We created you'). These are not legal rulings because they do not relate to the actions of the mukallaf.

Finally, he defines 'action of the mukallaf' as whatever issues from him, including speech and intention, and defines 'mukallaf' as the one who has reached puberty and is rational. From this it follows that a child is not subject to a legal ruling, though he notes a nuance in uṣūl al-fiqh regarding the command to command something, illustrated by the ḥadīth about commanding children to pray at seven years old.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, which of the following is NOT a legal ruling?
A) The address of a king to his subjects
B) The address of Allah commanding prayer
C) The address of a messenger conveying a prohibition
D) The address of Allah permitting something

Key Takeaway
A legal ruling is defined as Allah's address that relates to the actions of the morally responsible by command, permission, or disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that a legal ruling is specifically Allah's address—not human opinion—how seriously do you take each command and prohibition of the Sharia? Do you ever treat rulings as mere culture or scholarly consensus, forgetting their divine origin? What would change if you truly internalized that every ruling is a direct address from Allah to you?
?
The passage explains that messengers are infallible conveyers of divine address. How does this deepen your trust in the Sunnah? Have you ever allowed doubt about a ruling because of its human transmission? How can you remind yourself that the ultimate source is Allah, and the messenger is merely a protected conduit?
?
You are a mukallaf—one directly addressed by Allah through His rulings. Do you live with the awareness that every action you take is being judged by the One who addressed you? How would your daily choices shift if you listened to every ruling as if it were a personal letter from your Creator?

What Divine Speech is Not a Legal Ruling
Sanusi lists four categories of divine speech excluded from being legal rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that not all divine speech is a legal ruling; only that which concerns the actions of the mukallaf is, and you will learn the four categories of divine speech that are excluded.

Core Idea
Only divine speech about our actions counts as a legal ruling.

Sanusi now examines the phrase 'connected to the actions of the morally responsible' and shows what it excludes. He lists four types of divine discourse that are not legal rulings because they do not pertain to human actions. This lesson clarifies the boundaries of the term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the four exclusions
Read the Arabic text listing the four excluded categories and their examples. Notice each category is defined by what the divine speech is about.
2
Identify each excluded category
Go through each of the four: speech about Allah's essence, His actions, inanimate objects, and the essences of the mukallaf. Understand why each is not connected to the actions of the mukallaf.
3
Understand why they are not legal rulings
Reflect on the definition: a legal ruling must be about the actions of the mukallaf. These four categories do not meet that condition.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'legal ruling' (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī). The author begins by stating the definition: 'the address of Allah the Exalted that is connected to the actions of the morally responsible by way of demand, permission, or a disposition for them.'

He then comments on each part of the definition in order. First, he explains 'address' (khiṭāb) as the genus in the definition. He defines the reality of address as speech intended for one capable of understanding, and notes a scholastic disagreement about whether the existence of the addressee is necessary for something to be called 'address'. This leads to a discussion about whether Allah's eternal speech is called 'address' before addressees exist; the author notes that the correct view is that it is permissible to call it address, citing al-Ashʿarī and al-Bāqillānī's differing opinions.

Next, he clarifies that what is meant by 'address' here is the thing addressed (al-mukhāṭab bihi), using the verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle. Then he explains that adding 'of Allah' excludes the address of anyone else—kings, parents, shaykhs, angels, humans, jinn—from being called a legal ruling. Only the address of messengers concerning obligations is called a legal ruling because they are conveyers from Allah, infallible in their conveyance.

Then he examines the phrase 'connected to the actions of the morally responsible' and shows that it excludes four types of divine discourse: (1) speech about Allah's own essence (e.g., 'There is no god but Allah'), (2) speech about Allah's actions (e.g., 'Allah is the Creator of everything'), (3) speech about inanimate objects (e.g., 'And the Day We will set the mountains in motion'), and (4) speech about the essences of the morally responsible (e.g., 'And indeed We created you'). These are not legal rulings because they do not relate to the actions of the mukallaf.

Finally, he defines 'action of the mukallaf' as whatever issues from him, including speech and intention, and defines 'mukallaf' as the one who has reached puberty and is rational. From this it follows that a child is not subject to a legal ruling, though he notes a nuance in uṣūl al-fiqh regarding the command to command something, illustrated by the ḥadīth about commanding children to pray at seven years old.

الخطاب المتعلق بذوات المكلفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Quranic verses are legal rulings.
Correction: Only divine speech that addresses the actions of the mukallaf (by demand, permission, or disposition) is a legal ruling. Verses about Allah's essence, actions, inanimate objects, or the creation of humans are not legal rulings.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of divine speech that is NOT a legal ruling according to this passage?
A) 'Establish prayer'
B) 'Do not steal'
C) 'There is no god but Allah'
D) 'Pay zakat'

Key Takeaway
Not all divine speech is a legal ruling; it must be specifically about the actions of the morally responsible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse about Allah's essence, like 'There is no god but Allah', do you treat it merely as information to believe, or does it move your heart to abandon all false objects of worship and center your life solely on Him? What specific change in your daily conduct does this verse demand beyond affirming it?
?
Have you ever mistakenly felt that a descriptive verse about creation (like 'We created you') carried a direct command, leading you to either overburden yourself or neglect the actual commands of the Shari'ah? How can this lesson help you distinguish between informative and prescriptive verses so that you prioritize what Allah has actually obligated?
?
If only divine speech about your actions can be a legal ruling, then your accountability rests squarely on what you do. Are there areas in your life where you hide behind beliefs about Allah or self-identity (e.g., 'I am a believer') without translating them into obedient actions? What verse about actions will you act upon today?

The Morally Responsible Person and Their Actions
Sanusi defines the action and the morally responsible person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the mukallaf (morally responsible person) and what constitutes their action in the context of legal rulings, including the status of children.

Core Idea
A legal ruling only applies to a mature, rational person.

In the final part of this passage, Sanusi defines what is meant by 'action of the mukallaf' and who the mukallaf is. He notes that children are not subject to rulings, but also highlights a subtlety regarding commands given to guardians.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the passage that defines 'action of the mukallaf' and 'mukallaf' itself. Notice that action includes speech and intention, and that mukallaf means mature and rational.
2
Consider the implication about children
The passage states that a child (ṣabī) is not subject to a ruling. Think about what this means for obligations like prayer.
3
Reflect on the ḥadīth about commanding children
Sanusi mentions a ḥadīth about commanding children to pray at seven. This introduces a nuance about the command to command, which is discussed in uṣūl al-fiqh.
The ḥadīth shows that although children are not directly addressed, guardians are commanded to train them.

حد الحكم الشرعي
فَالشَّرْعِيُّ : خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِأَفْعَالِ الْمُكَلَّفِينَ بِالطَّلَبِ أَوِ الإِبَاحَةِ ، أَوِ الْوَضْعِ لَهُمَا
قوله : ( خِطاب ) كالجنس في الحدَّ
وحقيقة الخطاب : الكلامُ الذي يُقصد بهِ مَنْ هو أهل للفهم . واختُلِفَ : هل مِنْ شرطِ التسمية به وجود المخاطَب أم لا ؟ وعلى ذلك جرى الخلافُ في كلام الله تعالى : هل يُسمَّى في الأَزْلِ خطاباً قبل وجودِ المخاطَبينَ أم لا ؟(۱) والأصح : جواز إطلاقه؛ لأن تعلقات الكلام قديمة عند الإمام الأشعري واختار القاضي الباقلاني أنه لا يُسمَّى كلام الله في الأزل خطاباً ، وانظر حاشية العطار على شرح جمع الجوامع ) ( ١/ ٦٧ ) ، و رفع الحاجب عن مختصر ابن الحاجب » ( ٤٩٠/١ )
والمراد بالخطابِ هنا : المخاطَبُ بهِ ؛ مِنْ إطلاق المصدرِ على اسم المفعول .
وإضافة الخطاب إلى الله تبارك وتعالى يُخرِجُ خطابَ غيرِهِ ؛ كالملوك والآباء والأمهات والمشايخ .
وبالجملة : يخرجُ بهذا القيدِ خطابُ مَنْ سوى اللهِ تعالى ؛ مِنَ الملائكة والإنس والجن ، فلا يُسمَّى خطاب هؤلاء كلّهم حُكْماً شرعيّاً ، وإنَّما يُسمَّى خطابُ الرُّسُلِ بالتكاليف حُكْماً شرعياً ؛ لأنَّهم مُبلِّغونَ عن الله تعالى ، معصومونَ في تبليغهم مِنَ الكذب عمداً وسهواً .
وقوله : ( المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين ( يُخرِجُ أربعة أشياء : الأول : خطابُهُ تعالى المتعلَّق بذاتِهِ العليَّةِ : نحو : ﴿ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [الصافات : ٣٥]
•
الثاني : الخطاب المتعلّق بفعلِهِ : نحو : ﴿ اللَّهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ [الرعد : ١٦]
الثالثُ : الخطاب المتعلّق بالجماداتِ : نحو : ﴿ وَيَوْمَ نُسَيِّرُ الْجِبَالَ [الكهف : ٤٧]
•
الرابع : الخطاب المتعلّق بذواتِ المكلَّفين : نحو : ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْتَكُمْ ثُمَّ صَوَّرْنَكُمْ﴾ [الأعراف : ١١] .
)
والمراد بفعل المكلَّف : ما يصدر منه ؛ ليشمل القول والنيَّةَ . والمكلَّفُ : هو البالغ العاقل ، ومن هنا يُعلَمُ : أَنَّ الصبي لا يتعلَّق به حكم ، هكذا قيلَ ، وانظر هذا معَ ما ذُكِرَ في الأصولِ مِنَ الخلافِ في الأمر بالأمر بالشيء (۱) : هل هو أمر بذلك الشيء أم لا ؟
(۱) كما في الحديث الذي رواه أبو داود ( ٤٩٥ ) عن سيدنا عبد الله بن عمرو رضي الله عنهما مرفوعاً : « مُرُوا أولادكم بالصلاة وهم أبناء سبع سنينَ )
The definition of the legal ruling. So the legal ruling is: the address of Allah the Exalted that is connected to the actions of the morally responsible (mukallafīn) by way of demand, permission, or a disposition (al-waḍʿ) for them. His saying: (address) is like the genus in the definition. The reality of address is: speech that is intended for one who is capable of understanding. And there is disagreement: is the existence of the addressee a condition for calling it address or not? Upon that depends the disagreement concerning the speech of Allah the Exalted: is it called in pre-eternity 'address' before the existence of the addressees or not? (1) And the correct view is the permissibility of applying it, because the attachments of speech are eternal according to Imam al-Ashʿarī, but al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī chose that it is not called the speech of Allah in pre-eternity 'address'; see the gloss of al-ʿAṭṭār on Sharḥ Jamʿ al-Jawāmiʿ (1/67) and Rafʿ al-Ḥājib ʿan Mukhtaṣar Ibn al-Ḥājib (1/490). And what is meant by 'address' here is the thing addressed (al-mukhāṭab bihi), by way of using the verbal noun for the passive participle. And the attribution of address to Allah the Blessed and Exalted excludes the address of other than Him, such as kings, fathers, mothers, and shaykhs. In summary, by this qualification is excluded the address of anyone other than Allah the Exalted, from the angels, humans, and jinn; so the address of all of these is not called a legal ruling. Rather, only the address of the messengers concerning obligations (takālīf) is called a legal ruling, because they are conveyers from Allah the Exalted, protected in their conveyance from lying intentionally or inadvertently. And His saying: (connected to the actions of the morally responsible) excludes four things: First: His address connected to His own lofty Essence, such as: {There is no god but Allah} [al-Ṣāffāt: 35]. Second: the address connected to His action, such as: {Allah is the Creator of everything} [al-Raʿd: 16]. Third: the address connected to inanimate objects, such as: {And the Day We will set the mountains in motion} [al-Kahf: 47]. Fourth: the address connected to the essences of the morally responsible, such as: {And indeed We created you, then We formed you} [al-Aʿrāf: 11]. And what is meant by the action of the morally responsible is what issues from him, to include speech and intention. And the morally responsible (mukallaf) is the one who has reached puberty and is rational. From this it is known that the child (al-ṣabī) is not subject to a ruling; thus it is said. But consider this along with what is mentioned in uṣūl al-fiqh concerning the disagreement about the command to command something: is it a command for that thing or not? (1) As in the ḥadīth narrated by Abū Dāwūd (495) from our master ʿAbd Allāh ibn ʿAmr, may Allah be pleased with them both, raised (marfūʿan): 'Command your children to pray when they are seven years old.'
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'legal ruling' (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī). The author begins by stating the definition: 'the address of Allah the Exalted that is connected to the actions of the morally responsible by way of demand, permission, or a disposition for them.'

He then comments on each part of the definition in order. First, he explains 'address' (khiṭāb) as the genus in the definition. He defines the reality of address as speech intended for one capable of understanding, and notes a scholastic disagreement about whether the existence of the addressee is necessary for something to be called 'address'. This leads to a discussion about whether Allah's eternal speech is called 'address' before addressees exist; the author notes that the correct view is that it is permissible to call it address, citing al-Ashʿarī and al-Bāqillānī's differing opinions.

Next, he clarifies that what is meant by 'address' here is the thing addressed (al-mukhāṭab bihi), using the verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle. Then he explains that adding 'of Allah' excludes the address of anyone else—kings, parents, shaykhs, angels, humans, jinn—from being called a legal ruling. Only the address of messengers concerning obligations is called a legal ruling because they are conveyers from Allah, infallible in their conveyance.

Then he examines the phrase 'connected to the actions of the morally responsible' and shows that it excludes four types of divine discourse: (1) speech about Allah's own essence (e.g., 'There is no god but Allah'), (2) speech about Allah's actions (e.g., 'Allah is the Creator of everything'), (3) speech about inanimate objects (e.g., 'And the Day We will set the mountains in motion'), and (4) speech about the essences of the morally responsible (e.g., 'And indeed We created you'). These are not legal rulings because they do not relate to the actions of the mukallaf.

Finally, he defines 'action of the mukallaf' as whatever issues from him, including speech and intention, and defines 'mukallaf' as the one who has reached puberty and is rational. From this it follows that a child is not subject to a legal ruling, though he notes a nuance in uṣūl al-fiqh regarding the command to command something, illustrated by the ḥadīth about commanding children to pray at seven years old.

Key Terms

المكلف
البالغ العاقل
الصبي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Children are morally responsible and obligated to perform all religious duties.
Correction: According to Sanusi, the mukallaf is defined as mature and rational; thus children are not subject to legal rulings, though they may be trained.

Checkpoint

B) A person who has reached puberty and is rational
C) Any Muslim adult
D) A child over seven years old

Key Takeaway
A legal ruling applies only to a mature, rational person (mukallaf), and their action includes speech and intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you living as a true mukallaf, using your intellect and maturity to fulfill your obligations, or are you letting your desires override your responsibility? What specific action can you take today to consciously act as a morally responsible person before Allah?
?
The passage reminds us that intention (niyyah) is part of action. Examine one deed you perform regularly—is your intention pure for Allah, or are you doing it out of habit? Commit to correcting one intention today.
?
If you are a parent or guardian, how seriously do you take the Prophet's command to command your children to pray? Does your home reflect this urgency, or have you neglected this responsibility? Write one practical step you will implement this week to guide your children toward prayer before they reach accountability.

The Status of Sharia Commands for Minors
Sanusi addresses objection about minors' obligation and gives ruling for prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi resolves the question of whether sharia commands apply to minors, and the distinction between minors above and below ten years regarding the obligation of prayer.

Core Idea
Minors can be obligated by sharia commands, especially for acts like prayer.

Sanusi addresses a common objection: that sharia commands do not apply to minors because they are not addressed by divine law. He argues that minors are indeed obligated, using the example of prayer. In this lesson, we will examine his reasoning and the different rulings for minors above and below ten years old.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Note the objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Locate where the objector says that minors are not commanded, and where the author responds that they are obligated.
The objection begins with 'فإن قيل' and the response with 'وإن قلنا'.
3
Understand the comparison with recommended acts
See how the author compares the command to pray for minors with the recommended acts for adults. Recognize that even recommended acts are a form of obligation according to some.
4
Examine the age distinction
Note that for minors who have reached ten years, the command is clearly an obligation because they incur punishment. For those under ten, it is like a recommended act but still an obligation according to a view.
The passage ends with a caution about consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ليس أمراً .. فبقي الصبيان لم يأمرهم الشرع ، فالمتعلق بهم ليسَ حكم الشرع ، بل حكم أوليائهم .
وإن قلنا : إِنَّهُ أمرٌ به.. فالأقربُ أنَّ الصبيانَ مُكلَّفونَ مِنَ الشرع بمثل هذا الأمر
وإذا كانَ الندب تكليفاً في حقٌّ البالغين على قول معَ أَنَّهُ لا يلحقُ بتركه عقوبة شرعية لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة.. فأمْرُ الصبيانِ بالصلاة أقرب لأنْ يكونَ تكليفاً ؛ لاستحقاقهم بتركها عقوبة الشرع في الدنيا ، هذا فيمَنْ بلغ منهم عشر سنينَ ، ومَنْ لم يبلغها كانَ طلب الصلاة منه كالمندوب في حقٌّ مَنْ بلغ ؛ وهو تكليف على قول اللهم إلا أن يُوجَدَ إجماعٌ على أنَّ البلوغ شرط التكليف ، فانظر ذلك .
If it is said: It is not a command... so the minors remain; the sharia did not command them, so what pertains to them is not the ruling of sharia, rather the ruling of their guardians.
And if we say: It is a command... then the closest is that minors are obligated by the sharia with such a command.
And if the recommended (nadb) is a legal obligation (taklif) in the case of adults according to a view, even though no punishment follows its abandonment in this world or the next, then commanding minors to pray is closer to being a legal obligation, because they deserve a worldly punishment by sharia for abandoning it. This is regarding those who have reached ten years among them. And who has not reached it, the request of prayer from him is like the recommended (mandub) in the case of the adult; and it is a legal obligation according to a view, unless there is consensus that puberty is a condition for legal obligation. So consider that.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the legal obligation (taklif) of minors. It begins with an objection: someone argues that sharia rulings do not apply to minors because sharia has not commanded them, so their actions fall under the authority of their guardians, not under divine law. The answer responds: if we consider the command to apply, minors are indeed obligated by such a command from sharia. The author then uses the case of recommended acts (nadb) for adults, which are a form of obligation (taklif) even though no punishment follows their abandonment. Similarly, commanding minors to pray is even more like obligation because they incur punishment for abandoning it in this world (physical discipline), especially those who have reached the age of ten. For those under ten, the request is like the recommended for adults, but still considered obligation according to some scholars. The passage ends with a cautionary note: unless there is consensus that puberty is a condition for obligation, the reader should consider this point carefully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Minors are not subject to any sharia commands at all.
Correction: According to Sanusi, minors are obligated by certain sharia commands, like prayer, especially when they reach ten years.

Checkpoint

A) It is not a command at all.
B) It is like the recommended (mandub) for adults but still considered a legal obligation according to some.
C) It is obligatory without any doubt.
D) It is only a recommendation with no binding force.

Key Takeaway
Minors are obligated by sharia commands for acts like prayer, with a distinction between those above and below the age of ten.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have assumed that children are not accountable for prayer before puberty. But this passage shows that minors above ten are obligated and deserve worldly punishment. Have you been negligent in training your children to pray, thinking it does not matter yet? Will you commit to establishing prayer discipline for your children from age ten, knowing the sharia holds them accountable?
?
The passage draws a fine distinction between those above and below ten, showing that even recommended acts for adults are a form of obligation. Do you treat the recommended acts (sunnah) lightly, forgetting that they too are a charge from Allah? How can you strengthen your care for the sunnah prayers after reflecting on this?
?
Sanusi ends with 'consider that,' urging deep thought. Have you considered the weight of your guardianship over minors? Are you fulfilling your duty to command them to pray, or are you leaving them to the world? What will you change today?

Grammatical Attachment and Conceptual Division of the Three Rulings
Grammatical attachment and conceptual division of talab, ibaha, wad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three categories of rulings (talab, ibaha, wad') are grammatically attached and conceptually distinguished, and why all rulings are ultimately from sharia.

Core Idea
All rulings are from sharia; categories are conventional labels.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary clarifies how the three types of rulings—request, permission, and positing—are grammatically connected to the concept of divine address (khitab). It also resolves a common misunderstanding: that wad'i rulings come from reason or custom, when in fact all rulings are from sharia.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the full translation. Note the author's conciseness.
The passage contains both grammatical analysis and a conceptual point.
2
Understand the grammatical issue
The first part addresses how the preposition 'bi' in 'bi al-talab' attaches to 'khatab'. The author explains that the verbal noun (masdar) here has shifted meaning to 'what is addressed', allowing pre-modification.
This is a technical point about Arabic syntax.
3
Recognize the three attachments
The passage states that divine address attaches to actions in three ways: by requesting (talab), by permitting (ibaha), or by positing (wad') a cause, condition, etc.
These are the three categories of rulings.
4
Grasp the conventional nature of wad'i
The author emphasizes that calling one category 'wad'i' is a convention; in reality, all rulings are sharia-made. Reason and custom have no role in establishing them.
This corrects a potential misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله له : ) بالطلب ، أو الإباحة ، أو الوضع لهما ( المجرور الذي ( بالطلب ( أحسنُ ما فيهِ أنْ يتعلَّق بقوله : ( خطاب ) ، وفيهِ وصفُ المصدر قبل إعمالِهِ ، إلا أنَّهُ يُسهَلُهُ أَنَّ المجرور يعملُ فيهِ العاملُ الضعيفُ والقويُّ ، وأيضاً : فالمصدر هنا لم يبق على حقيقتِهِ ، وإِنَّما المراد بهِ : المخاطَبُ بهِ ، على ما سبق .
قوله : ( أو الوضع لهما ) معطوف على الإباحة ؛ أي : تعلُّقُ الخطابِ بالأفعالِ : إما بأنْ يَطلب فيها طلباً ، أو بأن يبيحها ، أو بأن يضع سبباً وشبهه لها .
وتخصيص هذا النوعِ مِنَ الأحكام باسمِ الوضع مخصُ اصطلاح وإلا فالأحكام كلها – أعني : المتعلقاتِ بالأفعال التنجيزية – بوضع الشرع ، لا مجال للعقل ولا للعادة في شيء منها
His statement: 'by request, or permission, or the positing for them' — the preposition that is with 'by request' — the best thing in it is that it is attached to his statement 'addressing' (khatab). And in it is the description of the verbal noun before its operation. However, it is made easy by the fact that the preposition is operated by both the weak and the strong operator. Also, the verbal noun here has not remained in its original meaning; rather, what is intended by it is what is addressed (al-mukhatab bihi), as previously mentioned.
His statement: 'or the positing for both' is conjoined to permission (ibaha); that is, the attachment of the addressing to actions: either by requesting a request in them, or by permitting them, or by positing a cause (sabab) and the like for them.
And the specification of this type of rulings with the name 'wad'i' (posited) is a matter of convention; otherwise, all rulings — I mean those pertaining to actual actions — are by the positing of the sharia; there is no room for reason or custom in any of them.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed grammatical and conceptual explanation of the three categories of rulings: request (talab), permission (ibaha), and positing (wad'). It begins by analyzing the grammatical structure of the phrase 'by request, or permission, or the positing for them' found in a definition. The author notes that the preposition 'bi' attaches to the word 'khatab' (addressing), and discusses the issue of describing the verbal noun (masdar) before it is syntactically operated. The difficulty is alleviated because the preposition can function with both weak and strong operators, and because the verbal noun here does not retain its literal meaning; it refers instead to 'what is addressed' (the content). Next, the passage clarifies that 'wad' lahum' is conjoined to 'ibaha', explaining the attachment of the addressing to actions in three ways: either by making a request, by permitting, or by positing a cause or condition for the action. Finally, the author emphasizes that designating a specific category as 'wad'i' is purely conventional. In reality, all rulings concerning actual actions are posited by the sharia, and neither reason nor custom has any role in establishing them.

إباحة

خطاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wad'i rulings (e.g., cause, condition) are derived from reason or custom rather than from sharia.
Correction: The passage states that all rulings concerning actions are from sharia; reason and custom have no role in any of them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it acceptable to describe the verbal noun 'talab' before its operation?
A) Because it is weak.
B) Because the verbal noun is not in its original meaning; it refers to what is addressed.
C) Because it is attached to a strong operator.
D) Because it is a convention.

Key Takeaway
All rulings concerning actions are from sharia; the division into talab, ibaha, and wad' is conventional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Sanusi explains that the verbal noun 'request' does not keep its literal meaning but refers to the content of the address. Have you ever clung to a literal interpretation of a text while missing its deeper intended meaning? How can you humbly seek the true intent of Allah's speech in the next verse you read?
?
The division into talab, ibaha, and wad' is merely conventional — yet all are binding from Allah. Do you treat some categories of religious obligations as less serious simply because they are not 'explicit' commands? What would it mean to give equal weight to every ruling, knowing each is from your Lord?

The Four Types of Talab in Taklifi Rulings
Sanusi enumerates the four categories of talab in taklifi rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories that fall under the concept of talab (request) in taklifi rulings: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapproval.

Core Idea
Talab is not just command and prohibition – it has four distinct types.

This passage from Sanusi introduces the four categories that constitute the meaning of talab in Islamic legal theory. Before diving into the details of each ruling, it is essential to have this classification clear. These four types form the foundation for understanding how Allah's requests to His servants are structured.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the heading
Read the Arabic passage once, paying attention to the heading 'Explanation of the Taklifi Rulings'. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
The heading indicates the broader topic; the passage itself focuses on the four categories.
2
Identify each of the four terms
Locate the four Arabic terms: إيجاب, ندب, تحريم, كراهة. Write them down along with their English equivalents: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, disapproval.
Notice that three of the terms start with 'alif-lam' (الإيجاب etc.) except كراهة which is without 'alif-lam' in the text.
3
Reflect on why there are four
Consider the common misconception that talab includes only command and prohibition. Why does Sanusi include recommendation and disapproval? Think about the strength of the demand.
The gradation is based on definitiveness: definitive commands (obligation, prohibition) vs. non-definitive (recommendation, disapproval).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ بيانُ الأحكام التكليفية ]
وَيَدْخُلُ فِي الطَّلَبِ أَرْبَعَةُ : الإِيجَابُ ، وَالنَّدْبُ
وَالتَّحْرِيمُ ، وَالْكَرَاهَهُ
[Explanation of the Taklifi Rulings]
And there enter into the request (talab) four [categories]: obligation (ijab), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (tahrim), and disapproval (karaha).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a classification. It begins with a heading: 'Explanation of the Taklifi Rulings'. It then states that the term 'talab' (request) encompasses four specific categories: obligation (ijab), recommendation (nadb), prohibition (tahrim), and disapproval (karaha). These are the four types of rulings that involve a request from the Lawgiver. The passage only lists them without further elaboration.

ندب
تحريم
كراهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think talab only includes command (obligation) and prohibition, ignoring the non-definitive categories.
Correction: The passage shows that talab encompasses four categories: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapproval. Both recommendation and disapproval are forms of request, though not definitive.

Checkpoint
How many types of 'talab' are mentioned in the passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Talab in Islamic law consists of four types: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and disapproval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that Allah's demands come in four degrees. When you feel an inner push to do a good deed, do you rush to fulfill it as an obligation, or do you treat it lightly as a recommendation? Which past good deed did you postpone because you assumed it was merely recommended, when in fact it might have been obligatory upon you? Will you investigate your duties today?
?
Think of a sin you commit repeatedly. Do you consider it a definitive prohibition (haram) or just a disliked act (makruh)? What would change in your heart if you truly believed Allah has declared it haram? Will you repent from it now?
?
This classification reminds you that Allah communicates with you through commands and prohibitions, each with a specific weight. Are you responding to His communication with the appropriate urgency? Which area of your life is most out of alignment with these categories? Commit to adjusting one thing today.

Definition and Three Types of Condition (Shart)
Sanusi defines shart and classifies it into three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a condition (shart) in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, and how it is divided into rational, customary, and legal conditions.

Core Idea
A condition is that from whose absence absence follows, but from whose presence nothing follows in itself.

Before we can discuss causality in theology and law, we need a clear understanding of what a condition (shart) is. Sanusi provides a crisp definition that distinguishes it from a cause, and then illustrates this concept with examples from rational necessity, natural habit, and divine command. This lesson unpacks that definition and classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the structure: 'ma yalzamu min 'adamihi al-'adam, wa la yalzamu min wujudihi wujudun wa la 'adamun lidhatihi'. This is the core definition.
The phrase 'lidhatihi' (in itself) is crucial.
2
Understand the linguistic vs. technical meaning
Sanusi gives the linguistic meaning of 'shart' as 'sign'. Reflect on how a condition is like a sign. Then compare to the technical term.
3
Explore the three types
The passage divides condition into rational ('aqlī), customary ('ādī), and legal (shar'ī). For each type, Sanusi provides an example. Work through each example and see how the definition applies.
Pay attention to the rational condition example of life and perception.
4
Distinguish condition from cause
Ask yourself: How is a condition different from a cause? The definition itself highlights the difference: a condition's non-existence entails non-existence, but its existence does not entail existence. Contrast this with a cause, which, when present, entails its effect.
This distinction is foundational in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالشَّرْطُ : مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ عَدَمِهِ الْعَدَمُ ، وَلا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِهِ وُجُودٌ وَلا عَدَمٌ لِذَاتِهِ ؛ كَتَمَامِ الْحَوْلِ لِوُجُوبِ الزَّكَاةِ . (1) يعني : قوله : ( لذاته ) .
الشرطُ في اللغة : هو العلامة ، ومنه : أشراط الساعة ؛ أي : علاماتها وأما في الاصطلاح : فمعناه ما ذكرَ ، وهو ينقسم إلى : شرط عقليّ ، وشرط عادي ، وشرط شرعي مثال الشرطِ العقليّ : الحياة للإدراكِ ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ عدم الحياةِ عدم الإدراك ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الحياةِ وجود الإدراك ولا عدمه ؛ لأنَّه قد تُوجدُ الحياة ويكونُ معَها غَيبةٌ بنوم أو إغماء أو جنون ، حتى لا يدرك الحيُّ معَ هذه الآفاتِ شيئاً أصلاً (۱) وعدم الإدراك مع وجود هذه الآفات عادي لا عقلي . ومثال الشرطِ العاديّ : النطفة في الرحم للولادة ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ نفي النطفة في الرحم نفي الولادة ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ النطفة في الرحم وجود ولادةٍ ولا عدمُها ؛ لأنَّه بعد أنْ تُوجد في الرحم قد يكون الله تعالى منها ولادة وقد لا يكونُ . ومثال الشرطِ الشرعيّ : الطهارة لصحة الصلاة ، وتمام الحولِ لوجوب الزكاة في العين والماشية مثلاً ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ نفي الطهارة معَ القدرة على تحصيلها عدم صحة الصلاةِ ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ حصول الطهارة صحة الصلاة ولا عدمها ؛ لإمكان فسادِها بعد حصول الطهارة باختلالِ ركن من أركانها ونحو ذلك . وكذا يلزمُ مِنْ عدم تمام الحولِ عدم وجوب الزكاة في العينِ
And the condition (shart) is: that which from its non-existence non-existence necessarily follows, and from its existence neither existence nor non-existence necessarily follows in itself; like the completion of the year for the obligation of zakat. (1) That is: his saying: 'in itself'. The condition (shart) linguistically: it is the sign, from which is 'ashrat al-sā'ah' (signs of the hour); that is: its signs. As for terminologically: its meaning is what was mentioned. And it is divided into: rational condition (shart 'aqlī), customary condition (shart 'ādī), and legal condition (shart shar'ī). An example of the rational condition: life for perception; for from the non-existence of life, non-existence of perception necessarily follows, and from the existence of life neither existence of perception nor its non-existence necessarily follows; because life may exist and along with it there is unconsciousness due to sleep, fainting, or insanity, such that the living being does not perceive anything at all with these afflictions. (1) And non-perception with the existence of these afflictions is customary, not rational. An example of the customary condition: the sperm in the womb for childbirth; for from the negation of the sperm in the womb, negation of childbirth necessarily follows, and from the existence of the sperm in the womb neither existence of childbirth nor its non-existence necessarily follows; because after it exists in the womb, Allah may or may not create childbirth from it. An example of the legal condition: purification (ṭahārah) for the validity of prayer, and the completion of the year for the obligation of zakat on gold and livestock, for instance; for from the negation of purification with the ability to obtain it, non-validity of prayer necessarily follows, and from the occurrence of purification neither validity of prayer nor its non-validity necessarily follows; because of the possibility of its corruption after purification due to disruption of one of its pillars or the like. Similarly, from the non-completion of the year, non-obligation of zakat on gold necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'condition' (shart) in Islamic legal theory and theology. It begins with a precise definition: a condition is something whose non-existence entails non-existence (of its conditioned thing), but whose existence does not entail either existence or non-existence of that thing in itself. The phrase 'in itself' (lidhātihi) is crucial: it means the lack of entailment is due to the condition's own nature, not due to some external factor. An example given is the completion of one lunar year for the obligation of zakat: if the year does not complete, zakat is not obligatory; but if it does complete, zakat may or may not be obligatory (depending on other conditions like nisab). The text then clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'shart' as 'sign', as in the 'signs of the Hour'. The terminological meaning is the same as defined. The passage then divides conditions into three types: rational, customary, and legal. For each, it provides a definition and an example. The rational condition (e.g., life for perception) is based on the nature of things: without life, perception is impossible; but life alone does not guarantee perception because obstacles like sleep can prevent it. The footnote clarifies that non-perception due to sleep is customary, not rational (i.e., it is not a logical impossibility). The customary condition (e.g., sperm in the womb for childbirth) is based on habitual divine action: normally, childbirth occurs from sperm, but Allah may not create a child despite its presence. The legal condition (e.g., purification for prayer) is established by divine law: without purification, prayer is invalid if one can obtain it; but purification alone does not ensure validity because other required elements may be missing. This division is essential for understanding how different types of causes and conditions operate in theology and jurisprudence.

Key Terms
شرط
شرط عقلي
شرط عادي
شرط شرعي
لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Condition and cause are the same thing.
Correction: A condition's non-existence entails non-existence, but its existence does not entail the existence of the conditioned, whereas a cause's existence entails its effect.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between a condition (shart) and a cause (sabab) according to the passage?
A) A condition's non-existence entails non-existence, but its existence entails existence; a cause's existence entails existence.
B) A condition's non-existence entails non-existence, but its existence does not entail existence or non-existence; a cause's existence entails existence.
C) A condition's existence entails existence; a cause's existence does not.
D) There is no difference; they are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
A condition is a prerequisite whose absence ensures absence of the conditioned, but whose presence does not guarantee the conditioned's presence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship like prayer, do you rely on your purification as a guarantee of acceptance? How does this lesson challenge your assumption, and what steps can you take to ensure your deeds are sound beyond just meeting conditions?
?
Consider a time when you saw good means (like having life or wealth) but did not see the expected good results (like perception or obligation). How did that affect your trust in Allah's wisdom, and how can this lesson reshape your expectations of cause and effect?
?
The rational condition of life for perception shows that even with life, perception can be blocked by sleep or insanity. In your spiritual life, what 'blockages' (like heedlessness or sin) prevent you from perceiving truth despite being spiritually 'alive'? What will you do to remove them?

Definition of al-Mani' and the Disagreement on Its Application
Sanusi defines al-mani' and addresses a disagreement about its applicability in the absence of the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-mani' (preventer) in usul al-fiqh and the disagreement about whether it can be the cause for the absence of a ruling even when the cause is absent.

Core Idea
A preventer can block a ruling even without a cause present.

This passage from Sanusi introduces a key concept in usul al-fiqh: the preventer (al-mani'). After defining it, he presents a disagreement among scholars about whether the preventer can be used to explain the absence of a ruling when the cause itself is also absent. Understanding this nuance is crucial for applying legal theory correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for Sanusi's style. Then read the English translation to ensure you follow the argument.
Notice the technical terms: مانع, سبب, مقتضي.
2
Understand the definition
Sanusi states the definition: al-mani' is 'what necessitates non-existence from its existence'. Grasp that the key is necessity: whenever the preventer exists, the ruling is absent.
This definition is broad and does not mention the cause.
3
Identify the disagreement
The scholars disagreed when the preventer coincides with the absence of the cause: can the absence still be attributed to the preventer? Identify the two opposing views.
The example of menstruation (preventer) and lack of time (cause) illustrates the scenario.
4
Compare the opinions
Al-Fakhr al-Razi held that the preventer only applies when the cause is present. Ibn al-Hajib and others argued it applies in both cases. Note which opinion Sanusi supports.
Sanusi argues that his definition inherently includes both cases, supporting Ibn al-Hajib.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The preventer is defined independently of the cause. This makes it a robust tool in legal reasoning. Consider why this matters for usul al-fiqh.
This prevents unnecessary restrictions on applying the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجملة الأولى - وهي قولنا : ( ما يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِهِ العدمُ ) - فمعناها لازم للمانع على كل حال ذلك واختلف الأصوليون : إذا قارن وجود المانع عدم السبب ؛ كان يقارن الحيض مثلاً عدم دخول الوقتِ .. هل يُعلَّلُ عدم الحكم بوجودِ المانع وإن انتفى أيضاً لعدم السببِ ؛ لأنَّ الأماراتِ أدلَّةٌ يصح تعدُّدُها ، أو لا يصح تعليلُ العدم به إلا حيثُ يُوجد السبب المقتضي للحكم ؛ إذ الذي يتبادرُ مِنْ معنى المانعِ أنَّ المقتضي للحكمِ موجود ، لكن انتفى الحكم لوجودِ المانع ؟ وهذا رأي الفخر ، والأوَّلُ مختارُ ابن الحاجب وجماعة ، وهو الذي يُؤخذُ مِنْ حدِّنا للمانع ؛ لأنَّ :قولنا : ( يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِهِ العدمُ ) شامل لما إذا وُجِدَ المقتضي أو فُقدَ . وبالجملة : فقد جعلناه ملزوماً للعدمِ في كلا الحالين ، وهذا هو عينُ القولِ الأَوَّلِ ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
As for the first statement - which is our saying: 'What necessitates from its existence non-existence' - its meaning is necessary for the preventer in all cases. And the scholars of usul differed: when the existence of the preventer coincides with the absence of the cause - for example, menstruation coinciding with the non-entry of the time - is the absence of the ruling attributed to the existence of the preventer even if it is also absent due to the absence of the cause, because indicators are multiple evidences that can be multiplied, or is it not valid to attribute the absence to it except where the cause that necessitates the ruling exists? For what is understood from the meaning of the preventer is that the necessitator of the ruling exists, but the ruling is absent due to the existence of the preventer. And this is the opinion of al-Fakhr (al-Razi), and the first is the choice of Ibn al-Hajib and a group, and it is what is taken from our definition of the preventer, because our saying: 'What necessitates from its existence non-existence' includes both when the necessitator exists and when it is absent. In summary: we have made it a necessary condition for non-existence in both cases, and this is exactly the first opinion. And with Allah Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage first states the definition of al-mani' (the preventer) and then presents a disagreement among usul scholars about whether the absence of a ruling can be attributed to the presence of a preventer even when the cause is absent. The author argues that according to the definition of al-mani' (whatever necessitates absence from its existence), it is valid in both cases, supporting the view of Ibn al-Hajib.

Correction: The passage shows that the definition of al-mani' is 'what necessitates non-existence from its existence', which applies even when the cause is absent. The preventer itself is sufficient to cause the absence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the subject of disagreement among the usul scholars?
A) Whether the definition of al-mani' is correct.
B) Whether the absence of a ruling can be attributed to a preventer when the cause is also absent.

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-mani' is broad: it necessitates non-existence from its existence, regardless of whether the cause is present or absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you confront that preventer today and ask Allah to remove it, or will you continue to excuse yourself by claiming the 'cause' was not present?

The Legal Ruling: Eternal Speech or Contingent Attachment?
Sanusi resolves an objection about the legal ruling's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the legal ruling (ḥukm shar‘ī) is both eternal and contingent, resolving an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
The legal ruling is an eternal speech with a created attachment.

This passage addresses a key question in uṣūl al-fiqh: if the legal ruling is Allah's eternal speech, how can it be said to be placed or created? Sanusi provides a crucial distinction between the eternal speech itself and its contingent attachment to actions after creation. This clarification resolves the seeming contradiction and is fundamental to understanding the nature of divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand how the objection arises from identifying the legal ruling with eternal speech. The objector asks: How can the legal ruling be created if it is Allah's eternal speech?
Notice the tension: eternal vs. created.
2
Identify the answer
Sanusi distinguishes between the eternal speech itself and its contingent attachment (ta‘alluq tanjīzī) to actions after creation. The legal ruling in the sense used by jurists refers to this attachment, not the speech itself.
3
Grasp the two types of attachment
The eternal speech has an 'aptitudinal attachment' (ta‘alluq ‘aqlī ṣalāḥī) in pre-eternity, but the 'operative attachment' (ta‘alluq tanjīzī) occurs after creation. Only the latter is called the legal ruling by jurists.
This distinction resolves the contradiction.
4
Reflect on the significance
Understand why this distinction matters: it allows jurists to speak of legal rulings as both eternal (in their source) and contingent (in their application), preserving both divine transcendence and temporal relevance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا وضع واضع ) فصل آخرُ أخرجَ به الحكم الشرعي . 1 بيانُ المراد بالحكم الشرعي ، وارتباطه بالوضع والجعلِ ] فإن قلت : كيف يصح أن يُقال في الحكم الشرعي : إِنَّهُ حصل بالوضعِ والجعلِ ، وهو خطابُ اللهِ تعالى وكلامه القديم ، والقديم ليس بموضوع ولا مجعول ؟ قلتُ : المراد بالحكم الشرعي هنا : التعلُّق التنجيزي لخطابِ اللهِ تعالى القديم المتعلّق بأفعال المكلَّفين بعد وجودهم وتوفر شرائط التكليف فيهم ، وهذا التعلُّقُ ليس بقديم ، والقديمُ إِنَّما هو كلامُ اللهِ تعالى وتعلقه العقلي الصلاحي بأفعال المكلفين في الأزلِ ، وإطلاق الحكم الشرعي على التعلُّقِ التنجيزي الحادثِ .. مشهورٌ عند الفقهاء والأصوليين ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
His statement: 'and no placing by a placer' is another section by which he excluded the legal ruling. Clarification of what is meant by the legal ruling, and its connection to placing and creation. If you say: How is it correct to say of the legal ruling that it occurred by placing and creation, while it is the address of Allah, Exalted, and His eternal speech, and the eternal is not placed nor created? I say: What is meant by the legal ruling here is the operative attachment (al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī) of the eternal address of Allah, Exalted, related to the actions of morally responsible persons after their existence and the fulfillment of the conditions of obligation in them. This attachment is not eternal. Rather, the eternal is only the speech of Allah, Exalted, and its intellectual aptitudinal attachment (al-ta‘alluq al-‘aqlī al-ṣalāḥī) to the actions of the morally responsible in pre-eternity. The application of the term 'legal ruling' to the contingent operative attachment is well-known among the jurists and legal theoreticians. And with Allah, Exalted, is success.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the nature of the legal ruling (al-ḥukm al-shar‘ī). The objector asks: How can the legal ruling be said to be produced by placing (waḍ‘) and creation (ja‘l) when it is the eternal speech of Allah? The answer distinguishes between the eternal speech itself and its contingent attachment to the actions of morally responsible beings after their existence. The eternal aspect is the speech and its intellectual suitability in pre-eternity, while the legal ruling in the sense used by jurists refers to the contingent operative attachment (al-ta‘alluq al-tanjīzī) that occurs after the existence of the subject. This resolves the apparent contradiction. The author concludes by noting that this usage is well-known among fuqahā’ and uṣūliyyīn.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي
تعلق تنجيزي
تعلق عقلي صلاحي
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The legal ruling itself is eternal because it is Allah's speech.
Correction: Only the speech itself is eternal; the legal ruling in the juristic sense is the contingent operative attachment of that speech, which is created.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'al-ḥukm al-shar‘ī' in this passage?
A) The eternal speech of Allah
B) The operative attachment of the eternal speech to actions
C) The created words of the Qur'an
D) The rules of fiqh derived by scholars

Key Takeaway
The legal ruling in its operative sense is a contingent attachment of Allah's eternal speech, not the speech itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an or consider a specific command like 'establish prayer,' do you tend to feel that the command is an eternal reality pressing upon you from pre-eternity, or do you recognize that its application to your life now is a created, contingent gift? How does that shift your sense of humility and gratitude?
?
The author distinguishes between Allah's eternal speech and its operative attachment—a distinction that means the ruling relevant to your current situation was not 'fixed' on you until you existed. If you truly internalize that Allah's command only attaches to you once you are here, does that make you feel more personally addressed by the Qur'an? Or less? Be honest.
?

Are you living as if Allah's speech is a distant eternal principle, or as an immediate operative command that demands your response right now? What would change if you embraced the latter?

The Three Categories of Decisive Religious Knowledge
Sanusi classifies decisive religious knowledge into three categories based on al-Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of decisive religious knowledge according to al-Ghazali and the different rulings for error in each category.

Core Idea
Three decisive domains—kalam, usul, fiqh—each with its own penalty for error.

Al-Ghazali, the Proof of Islam, classified decisive religious knowledge into three categories: kalam matters (pure rational theology), usul matters (foundational principles of jurisprudence), and fiqh matters (practical rulings). This passage explains each category and the consequences of being mistaken in them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation to understand the overall classification.
Notice that the passage begins by situating the issue of voluntary actions within decisive knowledge.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three divisions: kalam, usul, fiqh. Note the examples given for each: for kalam, the world's origination, proving the Creator, etc.; for usul, consensus and solitary report; for fiqh, prayer and adultery.
3
Understand the rulings for error
Observe how the ruling differs: error in kalam can be kufr or bid'a depending on the topic; error in usul is sinful; denial in fiqh is repudiation of revelation.
This nuance is important to avoid misunderstanding.
4
Relate to the issue at hand
The passage states that the issue of the creation of deeds is a kalam matter and error in it is bid'a, not kufr, because it is not in the fundamentals of faith.
This connects the classification to a specific theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْدَرِجُ مَسْأَلَةُ أَفْعَالِ الْحَيَوَانِ الِاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ ضِمْنَ الْمَسَائِلِ الْمَعْرِفِيَّةِ الْقَطْعِيَّةِ. هَذِهِ الْقَطْعِيَّاتُ قَسَّمَهَا حُجَّةُ الْإِسْلَامِ إِلَى أَقْسَامٍ ثَلَاثَةٍ: كَلَامِيَّةٍ، وَأُصُولِيَّةٍ، وَفِقْهِيَّةٍ. فَالْكَلَامِيَّةُ: الْعَقْلِيَّاتُ الْمَحْضَةُ، وَالْحَقُّ فِيهَا وَاحِدٌ، وَمَنْ أَخْطَأَ فَهُوَ آثِمٌ؛ كَمَسْأَلَةِ حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ، وَإِثْبَاتِ الْمُحْدِثِ وَصِفَاتِهِ، وَبَعْثَةِ الرُّسُلِ وَصِفَاتِهِمْ. وَمَسْأَلَتُنَا هُنَا - خَلْقُ الْأَعْمَالِ لَهُ سُبْحَانَهُ - دَاخِلَةٌ فِيهَا. وَحَدُّهَا: مَا يَصِحُّ لِلنَّاظِرِ دَرَكُ حَقِيقَتِهِ بِنَظَرِ الْعَقْلِ قَبْلَ وُرُودِ الشَّرْعِ. وَلَكِنَّ الْمُخْطِئَ فِيهَا إِنْ كَانَ فِي أُصُولِ الْإِيمَانِ بِاللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ فَخَطَؤُهُ كُفْرٌ، وَإِلَّا - كَمَسْأَلَتِنَا هُنَا - فَبِدْعَةٌ. وَالْأُصُولِيَّةُ: كَكَوْنِ الْإِجْمَاعِ حُجَّةً، وَخَبَرِ الْوَاحِدِ حُجَّةً، وَغَيْرِهَا مِنَ الْقَوَاعِدِ الَّتِي أَدِلَّتُهَا قَطْعِيَّةٌ، وَمَنْ أَخْطَأَ فِيهَا فَهُوَ آثِمٌ بِلَا شَكٍّ. وَالْفِقْهِيَّةُ: كُلُّ مَا عُلِمَ قَطْعًا مِنْ دِينِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى مِنَ الْعَمَلِيَّاتِ، فَإِنْكَارُهُ تَكْذِيبٌ لِلشَّرْعِ؛ كَوُجُوبِ الصَّلَوَاتِ الْخَمْسِ وَتَحْرِيمِ الزِّنَا.
The issue of voluntary actions of living beings falls within the decisive epistemological matters. These decisive matters were divided by the Proof of Islam (al-Ghazali) into three divisions: kalam matters, usul matters, and fiqh matters. The kalam matters are pure rational matters; the truth in them is one, and whoever errs is sinful; such as the issue of the world's origination, proving the Originator and His attributes, and the sending of messengers and their attributes. Our issue here — the creation of deeds by Him, exalted be He — is included in it. And its definition: what it is valid for the observer to grasp its reality by rational inquiry before the arrival of revelation. But the one who errs in it: if he errs in the fundamentals of faith in Allah and His messenger, his error is kufr; otherwise — like our issue here — it is bid'a. The usul matters: such as consensus being a proof, and the solitary report being a proof, and other rules whose evidence is decisive; whoever errs in them is sinful without doubt. The fiqh matters: everything that is known decisively from the religion of Allah, the Exalted, concerning practices; denying it is a repudiation of revelation; such as the obligation of the five prayers and the prohibition of adultery.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note classifying decisive religious knowledge. It begins by stating that the issue of voluntary actions of living beings falls under the category of decisive epistemological matters (al-masā'il al-ma‘rifiyya al-qaṭ‘iyya). These decisive matters are then divided, according to al-Ghazali (Ḥujjat al-Islam), into three types: (1) kalam matters (kalāmiyya), (2) usul matters (uṣūliyya), and (3) fiqh matters (fiqhiyya). For the kalam category: these are purely rational issues where the truth is unique and the one who errs is sinful. Examples include the creation of the world, proving the Creator and His attributes, and prophethood. The specific issue at hand—the creation of deeds by God—falls under this category. The definition of a kalam matter is: something whose reality can be grasped by the inquirer through rational inquiry before the coming of revelation. However, the ruling on error in these matters depends: if the error concerns the fundamentals of faith in God and His messenger, it is kufr; but if not—as in the current issue—it is bid'a (heresy). The usul category includes matters like the authority of consensus and solitary reports—these are evidentially decisive, and error in them makes one sinful without doubt. The fiqh category includes all matters known decisively from the religion concerning actions, such as the obligation of prayer and the prohibition of adultery; denying these constitutes a repudiation of revelation. This passage thus situates the theological debate on human actions within the broader framework of knowledge types and their consequences for error.

Key Terms
قطعيات
كلامية
أصولية
فقهية
آثم
كفر
بدعة
حجة الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All error in theological matters is kufr.
Correction: Only error in the fundamentals of faith (belief in Allah and His messenger) is kufr; error in other kalam matters is bid'a.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on one who errs in a kalam matter that does not relate to the fundamentals of faith?
A) It is kufr
B) It is sinful but not kufr
C) It is bid'a
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
Decisive religious knowledge is divided into kalam (pure reason), usul (foundational proofs), and fiqh (practical certainties), each with distinct consequences for error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a matter of 'aqidah like the creation of deeds, do you realize that being wrong could be bid'a or even kufr? How does that weight press on your heart to seek the truth with humility and diligence? What will you do differently starting today?
?
The passage says that denying the obligation of the five prayers is a repudiation of revelation. Have you ever treated salah as optional or negotiable? What does your heart say about the certainty of this obligation? Will you honor it with the seriousness it demands?
?
Al-Ghazali divided decisive knowledge into three: kalam, usul, fiqh. In which of these three areas are you most likely to be careless or rely on others without understanding? Identify one decisive matter you have neglected to verify for yourself. What step will you take by tomorrow to move from ignorance to certainty?
