The Salaf's Tafwid: Consigning Meaning for Ambiguous Texts
Sanusi presents the first school (Salaf's tafwid) for texts with multiple interpretations, citing Imam Malik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salaf's approach to texts with multiple possible interpretations: affirming divine transcendence and consigning the true meaning to Allah without specifying a particular interpretation unless textually supported.

Core Idea
The Salaf consign ambiguous meanings to Allah while affirming His transcendence.

This lesson explores the first of three scholarly schools regarding Quranic and prophetic texts that admit more than one valid interpretation. Sanusi presents the position of the early pious predecessors (Salaf), who required believers to consign the precise meaning to Allah after negating any impossible literal sense. He supports this view with the famous exchange between Imam Malik and a questioner about the verse of istiwa'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the full English translation. Let the author's classification and the Imam Malik anecdote stand out.
Notice the examples given first: verses about 'sailing under Our eyes', 'with My hands', and 'istawa'.
2
Identify the trigger for this school
The passage begins by specifying that this school applies to texts that can bear more than one valid interpretation (ta'wil). This is not about texts with a single obvious meaning.
3
Understand the core of tafwid
The Salaf's method has two components: first, affirm divine transcendence (tanzih) by rejecting any impossible literal meaning; second, consign (tafwid) the true meaning to Allah alone. The passage calls this 'obligatory'.
Tafwid is not ignorance but a deliberate act of entrusting knowledge to Allah.
4
Examine Imam Malik's response
Malik's statement 'The istiwa' is known, the how is unknown, and asking about it is innovation' is the key evidence. The commentary explains that the word istiwa' has permissible metaphorical senses in Arabic, but which sense is intended is unknown to us.
Malik ordered the questioner expelled, showing the severity of asking without textual basis.
5
Reflect on the implication
Asking to specify a meaning not prescribed by revelation is considered an innovation (bid'ah). This highlights the importance of limiting theological inquiry to what the texts themselves determine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنْ كانَ يقبلُ مِنَ التأويل أكثرَ مِنْ معنى واحدٍ ؛ كقوله تعالى : تَجْرِى بِأَعْيُنِنَا [القمر: ١٤]، وقوله جلَّ وعلا : لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَى [ ص: ٧٥] ، وقولِهِ تعالى : الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ﴾ [طه : o] ونحوه .. فقد اختلف العلماء في ذلك على ثلاثة مذاهب : المذهبُ الأَوَّلُ : وجوب تفويض معنى ذلك إلى اللهِ تعالى بعد القطع بالتنزيه عن الظاهرِ المستحيل : وهو مذهب السلف ، ولهذا لمَّا سألَ السائلُ مالكَ بنَ أنسٍ رضيَ اللهُ تعالى عنه عن قوله تعالى : عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ) قال في جوابِهِ : ( الاستواء معلوم ، والكيف مجهول (۱) ، والسؤال عن هذا بدعةٌ ) ، وأمر بإخراج السائل (۲) يعني رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ : أنَّ الاستواء معلومٌ مِنْ لغة العرب محامله المجازية التي تصح في حق مولانا جل وعلا ، والمراد في الآية منها أو من غيرها بما لم نعلمه (۳) .. مجهول لنا ، والسؤال عن تعيين ما لم يرد نص فيهِ مِنَ الشرع بتعيينه .. بدعةٌ ، وصاحب البدعة رجل سوءٍ
And if it accepts from ta'wil more than one meaning; like His statement, the Most High: 'Sailing under Our eyes' [al-Qamar: 14], and His statement, Almighty and Sublime: 'For what I created with My hands' [Sad: 75], and His statement, the Most High: 'The Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne' [Taha: 5], and the like... Then the scholars differed regarding that into three schools. The first school: the obligation of consigning the meaning of that to Allah, the Most High, after being certain of transcendence above the impossible apparent meaning; and this is the way of the Salaf. For this reason, when the questioner asked Malik ibn Anas, may Allah be pleased with him, about His statement, the Most High: 'established Himself above the Throne,' he said in his answer: 'The istiwa' is known, and the how is unknown, and asking about this is an innovation,' and he ordered the expulsion of the questioner. Meaning, may Allah be pleased with him, that istiwa' is known from the language of the Arabs—its metaphorical connotations that are valid in the right of our Lord, Almighty and Sublime—and the intended meaning in the verse from these or from others which we do not know is unknown to us, and asking about specifying what no text from the Shari'a has specified is an innovation, and the innovator is an evil man.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage addresses the second scenario: when a problematic text admits more than one valid interpretation. The author lists examples like 'sailing under Our eyes,' 'with My hands,' and 'istawa 'ala al-'arsh.' He then states that scholars divided into three schools. The first school is that of the Salaf: they require consigning (tafwid) the true meaning to Allah after affirming transcendence (tanzih). The author supports this by citing the well-known response of Imam Malik to a question about istiwa'. Malik said: 'The istiwa' is known (i.e., the word has permissible meanings), but the modality (kayf) is unknown, and asking about it is an innovation.' The author explains that Malik meant that the word istiwa' has licit metaphorical senses in Arabic, but which specific meaning is intended is unknown to us, and asking to specify it without textual evidence is an innovation.

Key Terms

السلف
الاستواء
الكيف
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Salaf completely avoid any interpretation and treat ambiguous verses as meaningless.
Correction: The Salaf affirm that the words have valid meanings (e.g., istiwa' has permissible senses), but they consign the specific intended meaning to Allah, not knowing which one applies.

Checkpoint
What does the first school (madhhab) regarding texts with multiple possible interpretations require?
A) To adopt any metaphorical meaning that fits reason.
B) To consign the meaning to Allah after affirming transcendence.
C) To adopt the literal apparent meaning despite impossibility.
D) To ignore all such texts.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's tafwid means consigning the true meaning of ambiguous texts to Allah while affirming His transcendence above any impossible literal sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you repent from this tendency and adopt the humility of the Salaf today?
?
Imam Malik ordered the expulsion of the questioner. If you were in that gathering, would you have felt offended or grateful for such firmness? Does your heart love the clarity of tafwid, or does it secretly resent being told that some meanings are unknown? Three Positions on Interpreting Ambiguous Texts
Sanusi enumerates three positions on mutashabihat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on interpreting ambiguous Qur'anic and hadith texts (mutashabihat) and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree on whether to interpret ambiguous texts, how to interpret them, or simply affirm without knowing how.

Sanusi outlines the three main approaches to ambiguous texts in the Qur'an and Sunnah. These positions reflect differing views on the use of ta'wil and the limits of human understanding. Understanding them helps navigate discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the three positions
Read the Arabic passage that presents the three positions. Notice that each position is introduced with 'المذهب' and a number.
The first position is about avoiding the innovator; the second permits ta'wil with evidence; the third affirms without knowing modality.
2
Understand the first position
The first position (implied from context) is to avoid and expel those who openly advocate innovation, to prevent fitna. This is a precautionary approach.
3
Understand the second position
The second position, attributed to Imam al-Haramayn, permits assigning a specific interpretation (ta'wil) when linguistic evidence supports one meaning over others. Examples: 'ayn, yad, istiwa'.
The preponderance is based on context or frequent usage.
4
Understand the third position
The third position, that of Imam al-Ash'ari, affirms that these texts refer to real attributes of Allah, but their true nature (kayfiyya) is unknown. This is tafwid.
This is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah.
5
Compare the positions
Consider the differences: the second position uses ta'wil, the third affirms without ta'wil, and the first avoids discussion altogether.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجبُ مجانبته وإخراجُهُ عن مجالس العلمِ ؛ لئلا يُدخِلَ على المسلمينَ فتنةً بسبب إظهارِ بدعتِهِ (۱)
المذهب الثاني : جواز تعيين التأويل للمشكل ، ويترجح على غيرِهِ ممَّا يصحُ بدَلالةِ سياقٍ أو بكثرة استعمال العرب للفظ المشكل فيهِ ؛ فتُحمَلُ العينُ على العلم أو البصر أو الحفظ ، وتُحمَلُ اليد على القدرة أو النعمة ، ويُحمَلُ الاستواء على القهر ، وهذا مذهب إمام الحرمين وجماعة كثيرة مِنَ العلماء (۲)
المذهب الثالثُ : حمل تلكَ المشكلات على إثباتِ صفات الله تعالى تليق بجلالِهِ وجمالِهِ لا يُعرَفُ كنهها ، وهذا مذهب شيخ أهلِ السنَّةِ الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله تعالى ورضي عنه (۳)
قلتُ : والظاهرُ أَنَّ مَنِ احتاطَ ، وعبَّرَ بما يذكرُهُ مِنْ تأويل لذلكَ المشكل بلفظ الاحتمالِ ؛ فيقولُ : يحتملُ أنْ يكونَ المرادُ مِنَ الآية والحديث كذا .. فقد سَلِمَ مِنَ التجاسر وسوء الأدب بالجزم بتعيين ما لم يقم الدليل القطعي على تعيينه ، والله تعالى أعلم
It is obligatory to avoid him and expel him from the assemblies of knowledge, so that he does not cause affliction among the Muslims by manifesting his innovation. The second position: permissibility of specifying an interpretation for the ambiguous (mutashābih), and it is considered preponderant over other [interpretations] that are valid due to contextual indication or the frequent usage of the Arabs for the ambiguous word in that [meaning]; thus al-ʿayn can be taken as knowledge, sight, or preservation; al-yad can be taken as power or favor; and al-istiwā' can be taken as subjugation. This is the position of Imām al-Ḥaramayn and a large group of scholars. The third position: [is] to consider those ambiguous [texts] as affirming attributes of Allah Almighty that befit His majesty and beauty, the reality of which is not known. This is the position of the shaykh of Ahl al-Sunnah, Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him. I say: It appears that whoever exercises caution and expresses what he mentions as an interpretation for that ambiguous [text] using the word of probability, saying: 'It is possible that the intended meaning of the verse or hadith is such and such...' has escaped from recklessness and bad manners of asserting definitively that which no decisive proof has been established for its specification. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents three positions on how to deal with ambiguous Qur'anic verses and hadiths (al-mutashābihāt) that could lead to anthropomorphism. The first position, mentioned at the beginning (though not fully quoted here), typically advocates avoiding discussion of such texts entirely to prevent fitna. The second position, attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) and many scholars, permits assigning a specific interpretation (ta'wīl) to an ambiguous term when there is linguistic evidence favoring one meaning over others. Examples are given: the word 'ayn can be interpreted as knowledge, sight, or preservation depending on context; yad as power or favor; and istiwā' as subjugation (qahr). The third position, that of Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and the Ashʿarī school, affirms that these ambiguous expressions denote real attributes of Allah that are befitting His majesty, but their exact modality (kayfiyya) is unknown; tafwīd (delegation of knowledge) is the proper approach. The author (Sanusi) then adds his own recommendation: one who is cautious should express potential interpretations using probabilistic language (e.g., 'it is possible that the meaning is...') rather than making a definitive claim without decisive evidence. This avoids arrogance and ill adab (improper conduct) toward Allah's speech.

Key Terms
التأويل
المشكل
المذهب
البدعة
الاستواء
إمام الحرمين
شيخ أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position is complete agnosticism about divine attributes, denying that they are real.
Correction: The Ash'ari position affirms that these attributes are real and befitting Allah's majesty, but the modality (kayfiyya) is unknown; it is not agnosticism about their reality.

Checkpoint
According to the third position (al-Ash'ari), how should we understand ambiguous attributes like istiwā'?
A) They should be interpreted metaphorically to avoid anthropomorphism.
B) They should be affirmed as real attributes of Allah, but their true nature is unknown.
C) They should be denied completely because they lead to innovation.
D) They should be explained literally as being something bodily.

Key Takeaway
Scholars hold three positions on ambiguous texts: avoidance, permissible ta'wil with evidence, and tafwid (affirmation without modality).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse about Allah's hand or throne, are you genuinely content to say 'I affirm it without knowing how,' or does your heart secretly yearn to picture something? What does that yearning reveal about your tawhid?
?
If a friend insists on interpreting 'istiwā' as 'sat physically on the throne,' would you have the courage to gently correct them using the linguistic evidence from this passage? What holds you back from doing so?
?
The first position warns of fitna caused by openly propagating innovation. Have you ever actively avoided or distanced yourself from a person whose beliefs caused confusion in your community? If not, what needs to change in your priorities?

Sanusi's Caution on Ta'wil: Use Probabilistic Language
Sanusi advises caution in ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's recommendation to use probabilistic language (lafz al-ihtimal) when offering interpretations of ambiguous texts, avoiding definitive claims without decisive proof.

Core Idea
When interpreting ambiguous texts, use words like 'it is possible' to avoid arrogance and ill adab.

After presenting the three positions, Sanusi adds a personal recommendation: even when offering an interpretation, one should do so cautiously by using probabilistic language. This avoids the recklessness of making definitive claims (jazzm) without decisive evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's recommendation
Read the final paragraph starting with 'قلتُ'. Notice how Sanusi qualifies his advice with 'الظاهر'.
2
Understand the caution
Sanusi emphasizes that one who exercises caution should express interpretations using phrases like 'يحتمل أن يكون المراد' (it is possible that the meaning is...).
This is to protect against arrogance and bad manners.
3
Identify the principle
The principle is: do not make definite claims about the meaning of ambiguous texts unless there is decisive proof (dalil qat'i).
This respects the sacredness of Allah's speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجبُ مجانبته وإخراجُهُ عن مجالس العلمِ ؛ لئلا يُدخِلَ على المسلمينَ فتنةً بسبب إظهارِ بدعتِهِ (۱)
المذهب الثاني : جواز تعيين التأويل للمشكل ، ويترجح على غيرِهِ ممَّا يصحُ بدَلالةِ سياقٍ أو بكثرة استعمال العرب للفظ المشكل فيهِ ؛ فتُحمَلُ العينُ على العلم أو البصر أو الحفظ ، وتُحمَلُ اليد على القدرة أو النعمة ، ويُحمَلُ الاستواء على القهر ، وهذا مذهب إمام الحرمين وجماعة كثيرة مِنَ العلماء (۲)
المذهب الثالثُ : حمل تلكَ المشكلات على إثباتِ صفات الله تعالى تليق بجلالِهِ وجمالِهِ لا يُعرَفُ كنهها ، وهذا مذهب شيخ أهلِ السنَّةِ الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله تعالى ورضي عنه (۳)
قلتُ : والظاهرُ أَنَّ مَنِ احتاطَ ، وعبَّرَ بما يذكرُهُ مِنْ تأويل لذلكَ المشكل بلفظ الاحتمالِ ؛ فيقولُ : يحتملُ أنْ يكونَ المرادُ مِنَ الآية والحديث كذا .. فقد سَلِمَ مِنَ التجاسر وسوء الأدب بالجزم بتعيين ما لم يقم الدليل القطعي على تعيينه ، والله تعالى أعلم
It is obligatory to avoid him and expel him from the assemblies of knowledge, so that he does not cause affliction among the Muslims by manifesting his innovation. The second position: permissibility of specifying an interpretation for the ambiguous (mutashābih), and it is considered preponderant over other [interpretations] that are valid due to contextual indication or the frequent usage of the Arabs for the ambiguous word in that [meaning]; thus al-ʿayn can be taken as knowledge, sight, or preservation; al-yad can be taken as power or favor; and al-istiwā' can be taken as subjugation. This is the position of Imām al-Ḥaramayn and a large group of scholars. The third position: [is] to consider those ambiguous [texts] as affirming attributes of Allah Almighty that befit His majesty and beauty, the reality of which is not known. This is the position of the shaykh of Ahl al-Sunnah, Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him. I say: It appears that whoever exercises caution and expresses what he mentions as an interpretation for that ambiguous [text] using the word of probability, saying: 'It is possible that the intended meaning of the verse or hadith is such and such...' has escaped from recklessness and bad manners of asserting definitively that which no decisive proof has been established for its specification. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents three positions on how to deal with ambiguous Qur'anic verses and hadiths (al-mutashābihāt) that could lead to anthropomorphism. The first position, mentioned at the beginning (though not fully quoted here), typically advocates avoiding discussion of such texts entirely to prevent fitna. The second position, attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) and many scholars, permits assigning a specific interpretation (ta'wīl) to an ambiguous term when there is linguistic evidence favoring one meaning over others. Examples are given: the word 'ayn can be interpreted as knowledge, sight, or preservation depending on context; yad as power or favor; and istiwā' as subjugation (qahr). The third position, that of Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and the Ashʿarī school, affirms that these ambiguous expressions denote real attributes of Allah that are befitting His majesty, but their exact modality (kayfiyya) is unknown; tafwīd (delegation of knowledge) is the proper approach. The author (Sanusi) then adds his own recommendation: one who is cautious should express potential interpretations using probabilistic language (e.g., 'it is possible that the meaning is...') rather than making a definitive claim without decisive evidence. This avoids arrogance and ill adab (improper conduct) toward Allah's speech.

Key Terms
الاحتمال
الجزم
القطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to definitively assert any interpretation of ambiguous verses as long as it seems plausible.
Correction: Sanusi says one should only use probabilistic language (e.g., 'it is possible') unless there is decisive proof that establishes a specific meaning.

Checkpoint
What phrase does Sanusi recommend using when offering an interpretation of an ambiguous text?
A) 'This is the intended meaning'
B) 'It is possible that the meaning is...'
C) 'Only Allah knows the meaning'
D) 'The literal meaning is clear'

Key Takeaway
Sanusi advises using probabilistic language when interpreting ambiguous texts to avoid arrogance and to respect the lack of decisive proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read an ambiguous verse and feel certain you know its meaning, do you express that certainty with humility or arrogance? Will you now adopt the phrase 'it is possible that...' to guard your tongue against tajāsur? Commit to this practice in your next discussion.
?
Are you more concerned with being right or with showing adab towards Allah's words? How will you change your language to reflect humility?
?
Reflect on a time you confidently asserted an opinion about a religious text without evidence. What drove that confidence? How does Sanusi's advice challenge your pride?

The Obligation to Learn Rational and Arabic Sciences
Sanusi concludes that ignorance of these sciences leads to heresy and obligates learning them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why learning the rational and Arabic sciences is a personal obligation for every capable Muslim and the grave consequence of ignorance.

Core Idea
Ignorance of essential sciences leads to heresy or disbelief.

Sanusi now delivers a powerful conclusion to his discussion on the foundational sciences. Having established their importance, he explains that neglecting them is not merely a scholarly shortcoming but a spiritual danger. This passage clarifies who must learn them and why, turning a theoretical discussion into a personal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If... then...'.
The passage begins with a heading in brackets.
2
Identify the two categories of people
Sanusi distinguishes between those who have the capacity to understand these sciences and those who do not. Observe the different obligations for each.
3
Reflect on the consequence
The passage states that ignorance leads to kufr (disbelief) or bid'a (heresy). Ask yourself why that is and how these sciences protect the faith.
This is a strong warning, not a mere suggestion.
4
Connect to personal responsibility
Consider what this passage means for your own learning. Do you have the capacity? What is the fard 'ayn (personal obligation) for you?
The footnote clarifies that 'these sciences' are rational and Arabic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرورة تعلم العلوم العقلية وعلوم العربية ]
وإذا عرفت أنَّ الجهل بهذهِ العلومِ يُوقِعُ صاحبَهُ في كفرٍ أو بدعةٍ . . تعيَّنَ على مَنْ لهُ قابليَّةٌ لفهمها أنْ يجتهد في تحصيلها (3) ، ومَنْ ليسَ لهُ قابليَّةٌ لفهْمِها وجب عليهِ أنْ يتعلَّمَ ما هو فرض عين عليهِ مِنْ علم (3) أراد: العلوم العقلية وعلوم العربية الأصلية
[The necessity of learning rational sciences and the sciences of Arabic]
And when you know that ignorance of these sciences causes its possessor to fall into kufr or bid'a, then it becomes incumbent upon one who has the capacity to understand them to strive in acquiring them (3), and upon one who does not have the capacity to understand them, it is obligatory for him to learn what is a personal obligation (fard 'ayn) upon him from knowledge. (3) He meant: the rational sciences and the original Arabic sciences.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a methodological conclusion and exhortation. The author states that ignorance of the sciences of semantics, rhetoric, and related disciplines (the rational sciences and Arabic sciences) leads one to either kufr (disbelief) or bid'a (heresy). Therefore, whoever has the intellectual capacity should strive to acquire them. For those without such capacity, they must still learn what is personally obligatory (fard 'ayn) upon them—namely, the essentials of these sciences. The footnote clarifies that 'these sciences' refer to the rational sciences and the original Arabic sciences (i.e., grammar, rhetoric, etc.).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only scholars need to learn these sciences; laypeople are excused.
Correction: Sanusi states that even those without full capacity must learn the personally obligatory parts, and those with capacity must strive to acquire them. Ignorance leads to kufr or bid'a, so everyone has some duty.

C) It leads to confusion
D) It leads to sin

Key Takeaway
Learning rational and Arabic sciences is a personal responsibility for every Muslim who has the ability, because ignorance can lead to disbelief or heresy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Ignorance of Rhetoric and Mental Metaphor
Sanusi gives examples of errors from ignorance of rhetoric and defines mental metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how ignorance of the rhetorical sciences (ma'ani and bayan) can lead to erroneous theological beliefs, and how the concept of mental metaphor (al-isnad al-majazi al-'aqli) resolves apparent attributions of actions to created beings in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Qur'anic attributions to creation are mental metaphors, not literal independent actions.

Sanusi begins by illustrating common errors that arise when people misunderstand the figurative language of the Qur'an. He provides three examples where the apparent meaning seems to attribute an effect to a created cause, but in reality the attribution is a mental metaphor. Understanding this concept is crucial for correct theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples of error
Read the three examples Sanusi gives of beliefs that stem from ignorance of rhetoric. Notice how each example is based on a Qur'anic verse that appears to attribute an action to a created entity.
These examples are from Surahs Al-Anfal, Al-A'raf, and Ar-Rum.
2
Understand the root cause
Sanusi explains that the error is due to ignorance of the arts of ma'ani and bayan. Ask yourself: what is the correct interpretation according to those who know these sciences?
The correct interpretation is that these are instances of mental metaphor.
3
Define mental metaphor
Read Sanusi's definition of mental metaphor (al-isnad al-majazi al-'aqli). Note that it is an attribution of an action to something connected to it (mulabis) but not the actual doer, in apparent meaning.
Contrast with linguistic metaphor: mental metaphor is rational, not dependent on a linguistic convention.
4
Reflect on the consequences
Sanusi warns that ignorance of these sciences can lead to disbelief or innovation. Consider why this is so important in Islamic theology.
This shows that rhetorical knowledge is not just literary but theological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا مِنَ الجهلِ بفنّ المعاني والبيانِ : اعتقاد صدورِ حوادثَ مِنْ غير المولى تبارك وتعالى ؛ كاعتقادِ زيادة الإيمانِ مِنْ سماع آياتِ القرآنِ ؛ أخذاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ آيَاتُهُ زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَانًا ﴾ [الأنفال : ٢]، وستر العورةِ مِنَ اللباس ؛ أخذاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ يَا بَنِي آدَمَ قَدْ أَنزَلْنَا عَلَيْكُمْ لِبَاسًا يُوَارِي سَوْآتِكُمْ ﴾ [الأعراف : ٢٦]، وإثارة الرياح للسحاب ونشرها ؛ أخذاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ اللَّهُ الَّذِي يُرْسِلُ الرِّيحَ فَنُشِيرُ [؟] سَحَابًا ﴾ [الروم : ٤٨]، ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا هو في القرآنِ والسنَّةِ كثير. ومَنْ خالطَ فنَّ البيانِ عرفَ أنَّ الإسناد في جميعِ ذلكَ مِنْ بابِ الإسنادِ المجازي العقلي (١)؛ وهو إسنادُ الفعل أو ما في معناه إلى ملابس (٢) له غير ما هو له في الظاهرِ عند المتكلّم. (١) وإنما كان عقلياً : لأنه لا يتوقف على وجود وضع لغوي ؛ إذ إسناد الفعل لما لا يتأتى منه ذاك الفعل لا يعقل ، أما المجاز اللغوي فيكون في لفظ لم يوضع أصالة لهذا المعنى ، وبهذا تعلم قوة المجاز العقلي. (٢) قوله : ( ملابس ( هو بفتح الباء ؛ لأن الفعل هو يلابس الفاعل أو المفعول به مثلاً . وانظر (حاشية التارزي) (ق) (٣٠) . وإذا عرفت أنَّ الجهل بهذهِ العلومِ يُوقِعُ صاحبَهُ في كفرٍ أو بدعةٍ
Likewise, from ignorance of the art of meanings (ma'ani) and eloquence (bayan) is the belief in the occurrence of events from other than the Lord, Blessed and Exalted; such as the belief in the increase of faith from hearing the verses of the Qur'an, taking it from His saying, Exalted: 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' [Al-Anfal 8:2], and covering the private parts from clothing, taking it from His saying, Exalted: 'O children of Adam, We have sent down upon you clothing to cover your private parts' [Al-A'raf 7:26], and the stirring up of winds for clouds and their spreading, taking it from His saying, Exalted: 'Allah is the One who sends the winds, and they stir up clouds' [Ar-Rum 30:48], and the like of that which is in the Qur'an and Sunnah abundantly. And whoever has mixed with the art of eloquence knows that the attribution (isnad) in all of that is from the category of mental metaphor (al-isnad al-majazi al-'aqli); it is the attribution of an action or what is in its meaning to something connected to it other than what it is for in appearance according to the speaker. (1) And it is only mental because it does not depend on the existence of a linguistic convention; since the attribution of an action to that from which that action cannot proceed is not rationally conceivable. As for linguistic metaphor, it occurs in a word that was not originally placed for this meaning. And by this you learn the strength of mental metaphor. (2) His saying: 'connected to it' (mulabis) is with a fatha on the ba, because the verb is 'yulabis' (to be connected to) the agent or the object, for example. And see (Hashiyat al-Tarazi) (q) (30). And if you know that ignorance of these sciences causes its possessor to fall into disbelief or innovation...
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-argument combination. The author first provides concrete examples of erroneous beliefs that arise from ignorance of the rhetorical sciences (ma'ani and bayan). The examples are: (1) believing that the increase of faith comes directly from hearing Quranic verses, based on Surah Al-Anfal 8:2; (2) believing that clothing itself covers private parts, based on Surah Al-A'raf 7:26; (3) believing that winds themselves stir up clouds, based on Surah Ar-Rum 30:48. In each case, the apparent meaning of the verse seems to attribute an action to a created entity, but the author argues that this is a misunderstanding due to ignorance of figurative language. The author then states that whoever is familiar with eloquence (bayan) knows that all such attributions are instances of 'mental metaphor' (al-isnad al-majazi al-'aqli). He defines mental metaphor as attributing an action or something similar to something that is connected to it (mulabis) but not the actual doer, in apparent meaning. The footnotes elaborate: mental metaphor does not depend on a linguistic convention (it is rational), whereas linguistic metaphor depends on a word being used for a meaning it was not originally placed for. The second footnote clarifies the word 'mulabis' (connected) by its vowelization and meaning. Finally, the author concludes that ignorance of these sciences leads to disbelief (kufr) or innovation (bid'a), underscoring the importance of rhetorical training.

Key Terms
فن المعاني والبيان
الإسناد المجازي العقلي
الجهل
ملابس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Quranic verses attributing actions like increasing faith or covering private parts to created entities imply those entities have independent causal power.
Correction: Sanusi explains that these are mental metaphors: the attribution is not literal but figurative, and the true agent is Allah. The created entity is merely the occasion or locus.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what type of rhetorical device is used in Quranic verses that attribute actions like increasing faith or covering private parts to created entities?
A) Linguistic metaphor (al-majaz al-lughawi)
B) Mental metaphor (al-isnad al-majazi al-'aqli)
C) Literal statement (al-haqiqa)
D) Allegory (al-tamthil)

Key Takeaway
Mental metaphor is a rational device that attributes an action to something connected to it, not to the actual doer, and ignorance of this can lead to theological error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a verse like 'the winds stir up clouds' and unconsciously attributed the action to the wind itself, forgetting that Allah is the true mover? How does this lesson force you to retrain your mind to see Allah's hand in every cause?
?
If ignorance of mental metaphor can lead to disbelief or innovation, what does that say about your own attentiveness to how you interpret the Quran? Are there verses you might be misinterpreting in this way? What will you do to learn the sciences of rhetoric to protect your faith?
?
The examples given are about faith increasing from hearing Quran, covering from clothing, and clouds from wind. Can you think of a similar instance in your own life where you attribute an effect to a created cause rather than to Allah? How does this lesson change your perspective on daily blessings?

The Individual Obligation of Learning Tawhid
Sanusi states the obligation to learn tawhid and rational sciences according to one's capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the categories of obligation to learn tawhid and the rational sciences based on one's capacity.

Core Idea
Learning tawhid is a personal duty for every Muslim according to their ability.

Sanusi begins by clarifying who must study theological and linguistic sciences. He distinguishes between those who can handle deep study and those who only need the essentials. This sets a realistic yet demanding standard for acquiring foundational knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's ruling on obligation
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note how Sanusi phrases the obligation for two groups: those capable of deep study and those who are not.
The footnote (3) explains what 'these sciences' refers to.
2
Distinguish the two categories
Identify the two rulings: the capable must strive to learn rational and Arabic sciences; the incapable must at least learn the essential tawhid (fard 'ayn).
Fard 'ayn means an individual obligation binding on every Muslim.
3
Reflect on the wisdom in this division
Consider why the author differentiates. This shows that Islam requires knowledge but adjusts the depth based on ability.
This prevents both negligence and overburdening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعيَّنَ على مَنْ لهُ قابليَّةٌ لفهمها أنْ يجتهد في تحصيلها (٣)، ومَنْ ليسَ لهُ قابليَّةٌ لفهْمِها وجب عليهِ أنْ يتعلَّمَ ما هو فرض عين عليهِ مِنْ علمِ التوحيدِ. ومهما سمعَ في الكتاب أوِ السنَّةِ ما يقتضي ظاهرُهُ خلافَ ما عرف في علم التوحيدِ . . يقطع بأنَّ ذلكَ الظاهر المستحيل غيرُ مرادٍ لله تعالى ولا لرسولِهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ، وأنَّ لذلك الكلام معنى صحيحاً، وتأويلاً ممكناً مليحاً، ويؤمن على سبيل القطع بأنَّ كلامَ اللهِ تعالى وكلام رسولِهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ حقٌّ، لا تناقض فيهِ ولا اختلاف، ولا باطل فيه ولا جهلَ ولا وَهْمَ ولا حيدَ عَنِ الصواب ولا غلط ولا انحراف، ولا يضرُّهُ بعد ذلكَ الجهل بالمرادِ؛ لأنَّ القلب محشو باعتقادِ تنزيه المولى تبارك وتعالى ورسله عليهم الصلاة والسلام عن كل نقص وخلل وفسادٍ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق، لا ربَّ غيرُهُ. (٣) أراد : العلوم العقلية وعلوم العربية الأصلية
It becomes incumbent upon one who has the capacity to understand them to strive in acquiring them (3), and one who does not have the capacity to understand them, it is obligatory upon him to learn that which is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) upon him from the science of tawhid. And whenever he hears in the Book or the Sunnah something whose apparent meaning necessitates opposition to what is known in the science of tawhid, he makes a definitive judgment that that impossible apparent meaning is not intended by Allah Most High nor by His Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and that for that speech there is a correct meaning and a possible, beautiful interpretation (ta'wil). And he believes with certainty that the speech of Allah Most High and the speech of His Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are truth, with no contradiction in them and no discrepancy, and no falsehood in them, nor ignorance, nor illusion, nor deviation from correctness, nor error, nor perversion. And after that, ignorance of the intended meaning does not harm him, because the heart is filled with the belief in the transcendence (tanzih) of the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, and His messengers, peace be upon them, from every deficiency, defect, and corruption. And with Allah Most High is success; there is no lord other than Him. (3) He meant: the rational sciences and the original Arabic sciences.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a methodological directive and a principle of scriptural interpretation. The author first states the obligation: those who have the capacity to understand the rational and Arabic sciences must strive to acquire them (footnote 3 clarifies that these are the 'aql and 'ulum al-'arabiyya). Those who lack the capacity are still obligated to learn the essential knowledge of tawhid (fard 'ayn). The author then outlines the correct attitude when encountering apparent textual contradictions. Whenever a verse or hadith appears to contradict established tawhid (e.g., by implying an imperfection in Allah), the believer must definitively rule that such an impossible apparent meaning is not intended by Allah or His Messenger. Instead, the text has a correct meaning and a possible, beautiful interpretation (ta'wil). The believer affirms with certainty that all divine and prophetic speech is truth, free from any contradiction, falsehood, ignorance, illusion, deviation, error, or perversion. If the believer holds this conviction, ignorance of the specific intended interpretation does not harm him, because his heart is already filled with the belief in Allah's transcendence (tanzih) and that of His messengers, free from all imperfection. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
فرض عين
التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only scholars need to learn tawhid or that it is optional.
Correction: Sanusi states that learning tawhid is fard 'ayn (individual obligation) for every Muslim who cannot engage with deeper sciences, making it compulsory for all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the obligation of a person who does NOT have the capacity to understand the rational and Arabic sciences?
A) He is excused from any learning.
B) He must learn the science of tawhid as an individual duty.
C) He must strive to acquire rational sciences anyway.
D) He must only memorize Quran.

Key Takeaway
Learning the essentials of tawhid is an individual obligation on every Muslim, while deeper study is required only for those with capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that studying tawhid is only for scholars, and thus neglected your personal duty? What specific step will you take today to ensure you have sound creed?
?
If you have the capacity to study deeper rational sciences (e.g., logic, philosophy), are you using it to strengthen your faith, or are you wasting it? Commit now to one action that turns your capacity into obligation.
?
When the Day of Judgment comes, will you be able to say you fulfilled the fard 'ayn of learning tawhid to the best of your ability? What is one gap in your knowledge that you have been ignoring, and when will you address it?

Resolving Apparent Contradictions in Scripture
Sanusi lays down the principle for interpreting apparent contradictions in scripture against tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct method for interpreting verses or hadith that seem to contradict the established principles of tawhid.

Core Idea
When scripture seems to contradict tawhid, the apparent meaning is never intended.

Sanusi now addresses a common concern: what to do when a sacred text appears to attribute imperfection to Allah. He lays out a clear principle that safeguards both the integrity of revelation and the transcendence of Allah. This method is essential for every believer to maintain sound faith without rejecting scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on interpretation
Read the Arabic and its translation. Notice the strong language used: he does not say 'maybe' but 'definitively judges'.
The phrase 'يقطع بأن' indicates certainty.
2
Identify the four components of the method
The passage gives steps: (1) rule out the impossible apparent meaning, (2) affirm a valid interpretation, (3) believe with certainty that revelation is true and consistent, (4) understand that ignorance of the exact meaning does not harm if one holds tanzih.
Each step is crucial for a coherent approach.
3
Reflect on the guarantee against harm
Sanusi emphasizes that the heart filled with tanzih (transcendence) protects the believer, even if the precise interpretation is unknown.
This reassures that correct intention is enough when knowledge is limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعيَّنَ على مَنْ لهُ قابليَّةٌ لفهمها أنْ يجتهد في تحصيلها (٣)، ومَنْ ليسَ لهُ قابليَّةٌ لفهْمِها وجب عليهِ أنْ يتعلَّمَ ما هو فرض عين عليهِ مِنْ علمِ التوحيدِ. ومهما سمعَ في الكتاب أوِ السنَّةِ ما يقتضي ظاهرُهُ خلافَ ما عرف في علم التوحيدِ . . يقطع بأنَّ ذلكَ الظاهر المستحيل غيرُ مرادٍ لله تعالى ولا لرسولِهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ، وأنَّ لذلك الكلام معنى صحيحاً، وتأويلاً ممكناً مليحاً، ويؤمن على سبيل القطع بأنَّ كلامَ اللهِ تعالى وكلام رسولِهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ حقٌّ، لا تناقض فيهِ ولا اختلاف، ولا باطل فيه ولا جهلَ ولا وَهْمَ ولا حيدَ عَنِ الصواب ولا غلط ولا انحراف، ولا يضرُّهُ بعد ذلكَ الجهل بالمرادِ؛ لأنَّ القلب محشو باعتقادِ تنزيه المولى تبارك وتعالى ورسله عليهم الصلاة والسلام عن كل نقص وخلل وفسادٍ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق، لا ربَّ غيرُهُ. (٣) أراد : العلوم العقلية وعلوم العربية الأصلية
It becomes incumbent upon one who has the capacity to understand them to strive in acquiring them (3), and one who does not have the capacity to understand them, it is obligatory upon him to learn that which is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) upon him from the science of tawhid. And whenever he hears in the Book or the Sunnah something whose apparent meaning necessitates opposition to what is known in the science of tawhid, he makes a definitive judgment that that impossible apparent meaning is not intended by Allah Most High nor by His Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and that for that speech there is a correct meaning and a possible, beautiful interpretation (ta'wil). And he believes with certainty that the speech of Allah Most High and the speech of His Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are truth, with no contradiction in them and no discrepancy, and no falsehood in them, nor ignorance, nor illusion, nor deviation from correctness, nor error, nor perversion. And after that, ignorance of the intended meaning does not harm him, because the heart is filled with the belief in the transcendence (tanzih) of the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, and His messengers, peace be upon them, from every deficiency, defect, and corruption. And with Allah Most High is success; there is no lord other than Him. (3) He meant: the rational sciences and the original Arabic sciences.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a methodological directive and a principle of scriptural interpretation. The author first states the obligation: those who have the capacity to understand the rational and Arabic sciences must strive to acquire them (footnote 3 clarifies that these are the 'aql and 'ulum al-'arabiyya). Those who lack the capacity are still obligated to learn the essential knowledge of tawhid (fard 'ayn). The author then outlines the correct attitude when encountering apparent textual contradictions. Whenever a verse or hadith appears to contradict established tawhid (e.g., by implying an imperfection in Allah), the believer must definitively rule that such an impossible apparent meaning is not intended by Allah or His Messenger. Instead, the text has a correct meaning and a possible, beautiful interpretation (ta'wil). The believer affirms with certainty that all divine and prophetic speech is truth, free from any contradiction, falsehood, ignorance, illusion, deviation, error, or perversion. If the believer holds this conviction, ignorance of the specific intended interpretation does not harm him, because his heart is already filled with the belief in Allah's transcendence (tanzih) and that of His messengers, free from all imperfection. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
التأويل
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That apparent contradictions in scripture indicate that the text is unreliable or contradictory.
Correction: Sanusi teaches that such texts are not contradictory; the apparent impossible meaning is not intended, and there is always a valid interpretation.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say a person should do when hearing a verse or hadith whose apparent meaning contradicts tawhid?
A) Accept the literal meaning as is.
B) Reject the text as unreliable.
C) Declare that the apparent meaning is not intended and seek a valid interpretation.
D) Ignore the text and avoid thinking about it.

Key Takeaway
When scripture seems to contradict tawhid, the believer must rule out the literal meaning, affirm a valid interpretation, and trust in the truth and consistency of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a verse or hadith that made you uncomfortable because it seemed to describe Allah in human terms? Did you immediately trust that there is a valid interpretation, or did you let that discomfort shake your certainty? Will you now, from this lesson, train your heart to cut off doubt at the root?
?
Your heart's filling with tanzih is not optional—Sanusi says it renders ignorance of the specific meaning harmless. Is your heart truly filled with the conviction that Allah is absolutely beyond any deficiency, or are you still holding onto mental images and comparisons? What one habit can you start today to cleanse your heart of such imaginings?
?
If you were to die tonight not knowing the precise ta'wil of a single difficult verse, would you die with the peace that your belief in Allah's transcendence suffices? Or would you die with anxiety over what you didn't understand? Decide now to surrender that anxiety and trust in the truth of Allah's speech, even when your mind cannot grasp it.

Interpreting Apparent Meanings Contradicting Tawhid
The author states the principle that apparent meanings contradicting tawhid must be reinterpreted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic hermeneutical principle that when scripture appears to contradict tawhid, the apparent meaning must be rejected and a valid interpretation sought.

Core Idea
Apparent meanings contradicting tawhid are impossible and must be reinterpreted.

How should a believer approach a verse or hadith that seems to conflict with the core truths of tawhid? This passage lays down the rule: the apparent meaning is impossible and not intended, and the true meaning lies in a correct interpretation. The principle protects both the integrity of revelation and the transcendence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the decisive language: 'يقطع بأن' (decisively concludes) and 'غير مراد' (not intended).
2
Identify the key principle
The author states a rule for handling apparent contradictions. Find the sentence that says what the believer must do when hearing something that implies the impossible.
The principle is that the impossible apparent meaning is not intended.
3
Understand why reinterpretation is necessary
The passage explains that all divine and prophetic speech is true and free from contradiction. Therefore, any apparent conflict with tawhid must be resolved through valid interpretation (ta'wil).
This protects the transcendence of Allah.
4
Reflect on the role of certainty
Note that ignorance of the specific interpretation does not harm the believer, because the heart is filled with belief in Allah's transcendence. This shows that correct belief (aqida) takes precedence over literal reading.
Certainty in tawhid is the foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التوحيد ، ومهما سمعَ في الكتاب أوِ السنَّةِ ما يقتضي ظاهرُهُ خلافَ ما عرف في علم التوحيدِ . . يقطع بأنَّ ذلكَ الظاهر المستحيل غيرُ مرادٍ لله تعالى ولا لرسولِهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، وأنَّ لذلك الكلام معنى صحيحاً ، وتأويلاً ممكناً مليحاً ، ويؤمن على سبيل القطع بأنَّ كلامَ اللهِ تعالى وكلام رسولِهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ حقٌّ ، لا تناقض فيهِ ولا اختلاف ، ولا باطل فيه ولا جهلَ ولا وَهْمَ ولا حيدَ عَنِ الصواب ولا غلط ولا انحراف ، ولا يضرُّهُ بعد ذلكَ الجهل بالمرادِ ؛ لأنَّ القلب محشو باعتقادِ تنزيه المولى تبارك وتعالى ورسله عليهم الصلاة والسلام عن كل نقص وخلل وفسادٍ ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق ، لا ربَّ غيرُهُ
Tawhid, and whenever one hears in the Book or the Sunnah something whose apparent implication contradicts what is known in the science of tawhid, one decisively concludes that that impossible apparent meaning is not intended by Allah Most High nor by His Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and that that speech has a correct meaning and a possible, beautiful interpretation. And one believes with certainty that the speech of Allah Most High and the speech of His Messenger, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are true, with no contradiction in them nor disagreement, nor falsehood, nor ignorance, nor delusion, nor deviation from correctness, nor error, nor perversion. And ignorance of the intended meaning does not harm him after that, because the heart is filled with the belief in the transcendence of the Mawla (the Lord), blessed and exalted, and His messengers, upon them blessings and peace, above every deficiency, flaw, and corruption. And with Allah Most High is success; there is no lord other than Him.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the hermeneutical principle governing apparent contradictions between scripture and the established truths of tawhid. The author begins by referencing tawhid, the foundational science. He then states the rule: whenever a verse or hadith has an apparent meaning that conflicts with what is known in tawhid, the believer must decisively reject that impossible apparent meaning and affirm that it was not intended by Allah or His Messenger. Instead, the believer must assert that the text has a correct meaning and a beautiful, valid interpretation. The author then emphasizes that one must firmly believe that all divine and prophetic speech is true, free from contradiction, falsehood, ignorance, error, etc. Finally, he explains that even if one does not know the specific intended interpretation, this does not harm him because his heart is already filled with the conviction that Allah and His messengers are transcendent above any defect. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms

الظاهر
المستحيل
التأويل
التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the apparent meaning of scripture must always be taken literally, even if it contradicts tawhid.
Correction: This passage clarifies that if the apparent meaning contradicts tawhid, it must be reinterpreted; it is not intended literally.

Checkpoint
What must a believer do when encountering a Quranic verse or hadith with an apparent meaning that contradicts the science of tawhid?
A) Accept the apparent meaning since it is scripture.
B) Reject both the verse and tawhid.
C) Decisively conclude that the impossible apparent meaning is not intended and seek a valid interpretation.
D) Ignore the contradiction.

Key Takeaway
When scripture appears to conflict with tawhid, the believer must reject the impossible apparent meaning and affirm a valid interpretation, trusting in the absolute truth and transcendence of Allah's speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that describes Allah as having a hand or sitting on a throne, does your heart immediately fill with tanzih, or does your mind try to shape an image? Which belief do you truly hold at that moment?
?
Are you content to say 'I do not know the exact interpretation' when faced with a difficult text, or does your ego require you to have a full explanation to feel secure in your faith? Can you trust in Allah's transcendence even without understanding everything?
?

Defending a Proof Against Objections
Sanusi notes that a rule's critique does not negate the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single argument's refutation does not necessarily invalidate the overall doctrine, as other proofs may still support it.

Core Idea
Weakening one proof does not destroy the proposition.

Theologians often rely on specific rules to prove key doctrines. When an opponent (like al-Aḍud) criticizes one such rule, it might seem that the whole doctrine is undermined. In this brief note, Sanusi clarifies that a rule's weakness does not necessarily negate the conclusion, because alternative arguments might still establish it. This lesson explores that methodological safeguard.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic note
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the word 'tanbīh' (تنبيه), signaling an important caution. Identify the key terms: قاعدة (rule), مدلول (subject/madlūl).
The passage is very concise, so each word carries weight.
2
Understand the objection and response
Al-Aḍud weakened (تضعيف) a rule (القاعدة). Sanusi says this does not prove the subject is false. Why? Because it's possible (احتمال) that the subject is proven by another rule.
This is a standard defense: multiple independent proofs can support a single doctrine.
3
Apply the principle
Reflect on why this matters. In kalam, a doctrine often has multiple arguments. Criticizing one does not refute the doctrine. This prevents a common fallacy.
This principle prevents 'shoot the messenger' errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : تَضعيفُ العلَّامةِ العَضُدِ في «المواقف» للقاعدةِ المذكورةِ لا يَدُلُّ على نفيِّ المدلولِ؛ لاحتمالِ ثبوتِهِ بغيرِها.
A note: The weakening of the distinguished al-`Aḍud in 'al-Mawāqif' of the mentioned rule does not indicate the negation of the subject; because it is possible that it is proven by other than it.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This is a brief methodological note (tanbīh) addressing a potential objection. The 'mentioned rule' (al-qā'idah al-madhkūrah) refers to the argument used earlier (likely about impossibility of infinite regress). Al-`Aḍud (likely `Aḍud al-Dīn al-Ījī) in his work 'al-Mawāqif' attempted to weaken that rule. Sanusi points out that such a critique does not necessarily negate the conclusion (al-madlūl), because the conclusion might be proven by other arguments. The note thus defends the overall proof by clarifying that even if one specific argument is challenged, the proposition may still be sound based on alternative reasoning.

Key Terms
تنبيه
القاعدة
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one proof for a doctrine is shown to be weak, the entire doctrine is false.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the doctrine may still be true if other proofs support it.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of this tanbīh?
A) To reject al-`Aḍud's work completely
B) To note that weakening a rule does not disprove the subject
C) To affirm that only one proof exists
D) To introduce a new rule

Key Takeaway
Criticizing one argument does not refute the conclusion; other proofs may still uphold it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a single argument for your faith is challenged, do you feel your entire belief is shaken? How does this note from Sanusi call you to reconsider that reaction?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that you have only supported by one argument? What other proofs might exist that you have not yet considered? Commit to seeking them this week.
?
When you encounter a critique of a theological argument, do you immediately assume the doctrine is false? How can this lesson transform your approach to objections and deepen your trust in the broader tradition?

The Objection: Can We Define Allah's Attributes?
Sanusi responds to an objection that attributes cannot be defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi’s definitions of divine attributes are not true definitions but descriptive identifications.

Core Idea
Our knowledge of Allah’s attributes is descriptive, not essential.

This passage addresses a common objection: if we cannot know the inner reality of Allah’s attributes, how can they be defined? Sanusi’s answer clarifies the type of definition he is using, a crucial methodological distinction in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the skeptic's challenge: since the inner reality of attributes is hidden, how can they be defined?
2
Read the answer
Notice Sanusi’s key distinction between ḥadd ḥaqīqī (true definition) and rasm (descriptive identification).
3
Reflect on the distinction
Why is this distinction important for the rest of his discussion of attributes?
It shows that our knowledge is limited but still useful for distinguishing attributes.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قيل: صفاتُه تعالى كذاته، كُنْهُها محجوب عن العقل، فكيف يحدها؟ فالجواب: هذا ليس بحد حقيقي، وإنما هو رسم؛ لتمييز بعضها عن بعض.
If it is said: His attributes are like His essence, their inner reality is hidden from the intellect, so how can he define them? The answer: This is not a true definition, but rather a descriptive identification to distinguish some from others.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This is an objection and answer. The objector argues that since the reality of Allah's attributes is unknowable, how can the author define them? The author replies that his definitions are not true definitions (ḥadd ḥaqīqī) capturing the essence, but merely descriptive identifications (rasm) to distinguish one attribute from another. This is a common methodological point in kalam: definitions of divine attributes are nominal or descriptive.

Key Terms
حد حقيقي
رسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi claims to fully define the essence of Allah's attributes.
Correction: He clarifies his definitions are only descriptive identifications, not essential definitions.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi defend his definition of Allah's attributes?
A) He claims the intellect can fully know them.
B) He says it is a true definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī).
C) He says it is a descriptive identification (rasm) to distinguish them.
D) He does not answer.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's definitions of Allah's attributes are descriptive identifications (rasm) to distinguish them, not essential definitions (ḥadd ḥaqīqī).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's mercy or power, do you ever assume you fully understand what these mean? How does knowing that we only have 'descriptive identifications' change the way you use these words in prayer and conversation?
?
Are there areas where you act as though you have captured the 'essence' of an attribute—like expecting Allah to show mercy exactly as humans show mercy? What specific belief or behavior must you repent of or adjust?
?
This lesson reveals a limit to human knowledge: we cannot define Allah's attributes truly. Does this thought make you uneasy, or does it increase your awe? What is one practical change you will make to let this truth humble you in your daily life?

The Concluding Prayer and Its Lessons in Humility
Sanusi concludes with supplication and a historical note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and significance of Sanusi's concluding prayer, including the comprehensive supplications and the historical note about the composition's motivation.

Core Idea
A scholar's work ends with humble prayer, not pride.

Sanusi brings his commentary to a close with a heartfelt prayer that reflects his deep piety and concern for all who will benefit from the work. This concluding passage invites readers to reflect on the humility and reliance on Allah that should characterize scholarly endeavors. It also contains a rare historical clue about the person who prompted the writing.

Guided Path
1
Read the prayer carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the flow from declaring the end to the series of supplications.
Pay attention to the inclusive language: 'the author, the one who prompted, the reader, writer, learner, teacher.'
2
Identify the different groups mentioned
List all the people for whom Sanusi prays. They include himself, the motivator, readers, writers, learners, teachers, and all believers.
This shows the author's concern for the entire chain of transmission.
3
Examine the specific supplications
Break down the prayer into distinct requests: sincerity, neglect of desires, piety, guidance, repentance, mercy, a good death, and paradise.
Each request addresses a different aspect of spiritual well-being.
4
Reflect on the tone of humility
Notice that Sanusi does not boast about his work but asks Allah to make it beneficial. He includes himself in the prayers for improvement.
This is a model of scholarly humility.
5
Understand the footnote
Read the footnote where Sanusi hints at a person who motivated the writing of either the original Muqaddimāt or its commentary.
This provides historical context, though the identity is not revealed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا آخر ما قيَّدناه على ( المقدِّماتِ )، نسأله سبحانَهُ أَنْ ينفع بها وبشرحها الواضعَ والمتسبِّبَ (١)، والقارئ والكاتب، والناظر والمعلِّم والمتعلِّم، في الحياة وبعد المماتِ. اللهم؛ اجعلني وإيَّاهم وسائرَ الأحبَّةِ والمؤمنين والمؤمنات ممَّنْ أخلص لوجهك الكريم العمل، وقصَّرَ في أمور شهَواتِهِ ودنياه الأمل، وتزوَّدَ للآخرةِ بلزوم التقوى، وخالف إلى المماتِ الشيطان والنفس والهوى. اللهم؛ اجعلنا يا مولانا بفضلِكَ مِنْ ذوي الألباب، وأرشدنا يا أرحم الراحمين في أقوالنا وأفعالنا وظاهرِنا وباطننا إلى سلوك طريقِ الحق والصواب، وتُبْ علينا يا مولانا توبة صادقة لا معصية بعدها إِنَّكَ أنتَ الرحيم الرحمنُ التوَّابُ، وهَبْ لنا مِنْ لدنك رحمةٌ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الكريمُ الوهَّابُ، وتوفَّنا تائبين مؤمنينَ مسلمين، وأدخلنا الجنةَ مع المؤمنين والمؤمنات برحمتك يا أرحم الراحمين. (١) قوله : ( والمتسببَ ) مشعر بأنَّ هناك مَنْ بعثه على تأليف المقدمات أو على تأليف شرحها أو على كليهما ، ولم يذكر ذلك في طالع الكتاب
This is the last of what we have recorded on the 'Preliminaries (al-Muqaddimāt).' We ask Him, glorified be He, to grant benefit through them and through their commentary to the author and the one who prompted (the writing) (1), and to the reader and writer, and to the learner and teacher, in life and after death. O Allah, make me and them and all the loved ones and believing men and women among those who perform deeds sincerely for Your noble countenance, and who are neglectful in the matters of their desires and worldly aspirations, and who have provisions for the Hereafter by adhering to piety, and who oppose the devil, the self, and whims until death. O Allah, make us, O our Master, by Your grace, among those of understanding, and guide us, O Most Merciful of the merciful, in our words and actions, outward and inward, to following the path of truth and rightness, and grant us repentance, O our Master, a sincere repentance after which there is no disobedience, for You are the Merciful, the Compassionate, the Oft-Returning. And grant us from Your presence mercy, for You are the Generous, the Bestower. And cause us to die repentant, believing, and Muslim, and admit us into Paradise with the believing men and women by Your mercy, O Most Merciful of the merciful. (1) His statement: 'and the one who prompted (the writing)' suggests that there was someone who urged him to compose the 'Muqaddimāt' or to compose its commentary, or both, but he did not mention that in the beginning of the book.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is the concluding prayer (khātimah) of Sanusi's commentary on the 'Muqaddimāt'. He begins by stating that this is the end of his recorded notes. He prays that Allah make the work and its commentary beneficial to all involved — the author, the one who prompted the writing (the person who motivated him), the reader, writer, learner, and teacher — both in this life and the hereafter. Then he offers several supplications: that all be made sincere in their deeds, that they be provided with provisions of piety, that they be guided to truth, that they be granted sincere repentance, mercy, and a good death. He ends by asking for entry into Paradise. The footnote clarifies that the term 'al-mutasabbib' (the one who prompted) indicates that there was a person who motivated the author to either write the 'Muqaddimāt' or to write this commentary, but this motivation was not mentioned in the introduction of the book. This provides historical context about the composition of the work.

Key Terms
خاتمة (khātimah)
المقدمات (al-Muqaddimāt)
مسبب (mutasabbib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who does Sanusi ask Allah to benefit through his work?
A) Only the reader
B) Only the author
C) The author, the one who prompted the writing, the reader, writer, learner, and teacher
D) Only the learner

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's concluding prayer reflects his humility and his desire that his work benefit all who encounter it, both in this life and the next.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi prays for neglect of worldly desires. Which desire in your life are you most clinging to, and what would it cost you to abandon it for the sake of sincerity?
?
He asks for a sincere repentance after which there is no disobedience. Is there a sin you keep rationalizing? What would true repentance require you to change today?
?
The prayer includes all who contributed to the book's transmission. How are you contributing to the spread of knowledge? Are you actively learning and teaching, or are you consuming passively?
